====================================================================
1.Basics of JavaScript (Done)
What is JavaScript? (History & Usage)
=================================================================
ğŸ“Œ What is JavaScript?

JavaScript (JS) is a high-level, interpreted programming language that makes web pages interactive, dynamic, and user-friendly.

It runs on the client side (browser) but also on the server side using environments like Node.js.

It follows the ECMAScript standard.

ğŸ‘‰ In short:

HTML â†’ Defines structure of a webpage.

CSS â†’ Defines style and presentation.

JavaScript â†’ Defines behavior (adds logic, interactivity, and dynamic content).

ğŸ“œ History of JavaScript

1995 â†’ Created by Brendan Eich at Netscape in just 10 days.

Initially called Mocha, then renamed to LiveScript, and finally JavaScript.

1997 â†’ Standardized by ECMA International as ECMAScript (ES).

Over the years, updates like ES5 (2009), ES6/ES2015, and beyond have introduced powerful features (classes, arrow functions, async/await, modules, etc.).

âš¡ Fun fact: Despite its name, JavaScript is not related to Java. The name was mainly for marketing reasons, as Java was popular at that time.

ğŸ¯ Usage of JavaScript

JavaScript is everywhere today:

Web Development

Adds interactivity to web pages.

Example: Form validation, sliders, popups, animations.

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<body>
  <h2>JavaScript Example</h2>
  <button onclick="greet()">Click Me</button>

  <script>
    function greet() {
      alert("Hello! Welcome to JavaScript.");
    }
  </script>
</body>
</html>


Web Applications

Frameworks like React, Angular, Vue make Single Page Applications (SPAs).

Example: Showing current time dynamically.

<p id="time"></p>
<script>
  document.getElementById("time").innerHTML = new Date().toLocaleTimeString();
</script>


Server-Side Development

Using Node.js, JavaScript can build scalable backend applications.

// Simple Node.js server
const http = require('http');

http.createServer((req, res) => {
  res.write("Hello from Node.js!");
  res.end();
}).listen(3000);


Mobile Apps

Frameworks like React Native, Ionic allow building cross-platform apps.

Game Development

Libraries like Phaser.js let you build 2D games.

AI & Machine Learning

Libraries like TensorFlow.js enable running ML models in browsers.

âœ… Summary

JavaScript is a lightweight, versatile language.

Born in 1995 at Netscape â†’ Standardized as ECMAScript.

Used for frontend, backend, mobile apps, games, and even AI.

Today, itâ€™s one of the core technologies of the web alongside HTML & CSS.

========================================================
Adding JS to HTML (<script> tag, internal, external)
========================================================
ğŸ“Œ Adding JavaScript to HTML

There are 3 main ways to include JavaScript in an HTML document:

1ï¸âƒ£ Inline JavaScript (inside HTML tag)

You can write JavaScript directly inside an elementâ€™s attribute such as onclick, onmouseover, etc.

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<head>
  <title>Inline JavaScript Example</title>
</head>
<body>
  <button onclick="alert('Hello! Inline JS works.')">Click Me</button>
</body>
</html>


âœ… Used for small snippets, but not recommended for larger projects (hard to maintain).

2ï¸âƒ£ Internal JavaScript (inside <script> tag in HTML)

JavaScript is placed inside <script> tags within the HTML file.
You can put it in <head> or <body>.

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<head>
  <title>Internal JavaScript Example</title>
  <script>
    function greet() {
      alert("Hello from Internal JavaScript!");
    }
  </script>
</head>
<body>
  <button onclick="greet()">Click Me</button>
</body>
</html>


âœ… Good for small projects or quick testing.

3ï¸âƒ£ External JavaScript (separate .js file)

JavaScript code is written in a separate .js file and linked using <script src="...">.

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

index.html
<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<head>
  <title>External JavaScript Example</title>
  <script src="script.js"></script>
</head>
<body>
  <button onclick="greet()">Click Me</button>
</body>
</html>

script.js
function greet() {
  alert("Hello from External JavaScript!");
}


âœ… Best practice for large projects â†’ keeps HTML and JS separate and reusable.

ğŸ“Œ Where to Place <script> Tag?

In <head> â†’ JS loads before content, may block page rendering.

At end of <body> â†’ Recommended for better performance (ensures HTML loads first).

ğŸ‘‰ Example (best practice):

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<head>
  <title>Script Placement</title>
</head>
<body>
  <h1>Welcome</h1>
  <button onclick="greet()">Click Me</button>

  <!-- JavaScript loaded after HTML -->
  <script>
    function greet() {
      alert("Best practice: Script at the bottom!");
    }
  </script>
</body>
</html>


âœ… Summary

Inline â†’ onclick="..." (quick but not recommended).

Internal â†’ <script> ... </script> inside HTML.

External â†’ Separate .js file linked with <script src="...">.

===============================
Variables (var, let, const)
===============================
What are Variables in JavaScript?

Variables are containers for storing data values.
In JavaScript, we can declare variables using:

var

let

const

ğŸŸ¢ 1. var (Old way â€“ function scoped)

Introduced in 1995 (original JS).

Function-scoped â†’ accessible only inside the function where declared.

Can be re-declared and updated.

Gets hoisted (moved to top of scope with undefined).

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

var name = "Suraj";
console.log(name); // Suraj

var name = "Pawar"; // Re-declared
console.log(name); // Pawar

// Function scope
function test() {
  var x = 10;
  console.log(x); // 10
}
// console.log(x); // âŒ Error (x not defined outside function)

ğŸŸ¡ 2. let (Modern â€“ block scoped)

Introduced in ES6 (2015).

Block-scoped â†’ accessible only inside { }.

Can be updated, but not re-declared in the same scope.

Supports hoisting, but not initialized (gives error if used before declaration).

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let age = 25;
console.log(age); // 25

age = 26; // âœ… Update allowed
console.log(age); // 26

// let age = 30; âŒ Error (can't re-declare in same scope)

// Block scope
{
  let city = "Pune";
  console.log(city); // Pune
}
// console.log(city); // âŒ Error (city not accessible outside block)

ğŸ”µ 3. const (Constant â€“ block scoped)

Introduced in ES6 (2015).

Block-scoped like let.

Must be initialized at declaration.

Cannot be updated or re-declared.

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

const country = "India";
console.log(country); // India

// country = "USA"; âŒ Error (can't change value)

// const country; âŒ Error (must initialize when declared)

// Block scope
{
  const pi = 3.14159;
  console.log(pi); // 3.14159
}
// console.log(pi); // âŒ Error (not accessible outside block)

ğŸ“Š Quick Comparison Table
Feature	var	let	const
Scope	Function-scoped	Block-scoped	Block-scoped
Re-declaration	âœ… Allowed	âŒ Not allowed	âŒ Not allowed
Update value	âœ… Allowed	âœ… Allowed	âŒ Not allowed
Hoisting	âœ… Yes (initialized as undefined)	âœ… Yes (but not initialized)	âœ… Yes (but not initialized)
Use case	Old code (avoid now)	Variables that change	Constants, fixed values
ğŸ¯ Example (All together)
var x = 10;
let y = 20;
const z = 30;

x = 15;   // âœ… allowed
y = 25;   // âœ… allowed
// z = 35; // âŒ Error (const can't change)

console.log(x, y, z); // 15 25 30


âœ… Best Practice (Modern JS):

Use let â†’ for variables that change.

Use const â†’ for constants or values that donâ€™t change.

Avoid var in new code.

====================================================================================
Data Types (string, number, boolean, null, undefined, symbol, bigint) with examples
====================================================================================
JavaScript Data Types

In JavaScript, data types are divided into two categories:

Primitive Data Types (immutable, single value)

string

number

boolean

null

undefined

symbol

bigint

Non-Primitive (Reference) Data Types

Objects, Arrays, Functions (weâ€™ll cover later).

Here, weâ€™ll focus on primitive types.

1ï¸âƒ£ String

Represents text (characters inside quotes).

Can use "double", 'single', or `` backticks (template literals).

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let firstName = "Suraj";   // double quotes
let lastName = 'Pawar';    // single quotes
let fullName = `${firstName} ${lastName}`; // template literal

console.log(fullName); // Suraj Pawar
console.log(typeof fullName); // string

2ï¸âƒ£ Number

Represents integers, decimals, or floating-point numbers.

Special values: Infinity, -Infinity, NaN (Not a Number).

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let age = 25;
let price = 99.99;
let infinityValue = Infinity;
let notNumber = "abc" / 2; // NaN

console.log(age, price); // 25 99.99
console.log(typeof price); // number
console.log(notNumber); // NaN

3ï¸âƒ£ Boolean

Represents true or false values.

Often used in conditions and logic.

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let isStudent = true;
let isWorking = false;

console.log(isStudent); // true
console.log(typeof isWorking); // boolean

4ï¸âƒ£ Null

Represents an intentional empty value (nothing).

Type is "object" (this is a long-standing JavaScript bug).

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let data = null;
console.log(data); // null
console.log(typeof data); // object (weird but true)

5ï¸âƒ£ Undefined

A variable declared but not assigned a value.

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let city;
console.log(city); // undefined
console.log(typeof city); // undefined

6ï¸âƒ£ Symbol (ES6)

Used to create unique identifiers.

Even if symbols have the same description, they are different.

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let sym1 = Symbol("id");
let sym2 = Symbol("id");

console.log(sym1 === sym2); // false
console.log(typeof sym1);   // symbol

7ï¸âƒ£ BigInt (ES11 / ES2020)

Used for very large integers beyond Number.MAX_SAFE_INTEGER (2^53 - 1).

Add n at the end of the number.

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let bigNumber = 123456789012345678901234567890n;
let normalNumber = 100;

console.log(bigNumber); 
console.log(typeof bigNumber); // bigint

ğŸ“Š Summary Table
Data Type	Example	typeof result
String	"Hello"	string
Number	42, 3.14, NaN	number
Boolean	true, false	boolean
Null	null	object (bug)
Undefined	let x;	undefined
Symbol	Symbol("id")	symbol
BigInt	12345678901234567890n	bigint

âš¡ Real-World Example (Mixing Data Types):

let userName = "Suraj";     // string
let userAge = 25;           // number
let isAdmin = false;        // boolean
let profilePic = null;      // null
let phone;                  // undefined
let id = Symbol("userID");  // symbol
let bigID = 987654321987654321n; // bigint

console.log(typeof userName); // string
console.log(typeof userAge);  // number
console.log(typeof isAdmin);  // boolean
console.log(typeof profilePic); // object
console.log(typeof phone);    // undefined
console.log(typeof id);       // symbol
console.log(typeof bigID);    // bigint

====================================================================
Operators (arithmetic, assignment, comparison, logical, ternary)
====================================================================
JavaScript Operators

Operators are special symbols used to perform operations on values and variables.
Main categories:

Arithmetic Operators

Assignment Operators

Comparison Operators

Logical Operators

Ternary Operator (Conditional)

1ï¸âƒ£ Arithmetic Operators

Used for mathematical calculations.

Operator	Description	Example (a = 10, b = 3)	Result
+	Addition	a + b	13
-	Subtraction	a - b	7
*	Multiplication	a * b	30
/	Division	a / b	3.333â€¦
%	Modulus (remainder)	a % b	1
**	Exponentiation	a ** b	1000
++	Increment (add 1)	a++ â†’ 11	
--	Decrement (subtract 1)	b-- â†’ 2	

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let a = 10, b = 3;

console.log(a + b); // 13
console.log(a - b); // 7
console.log(a * b); // 30
console.log(a / b); // 3.333...
console.log(a % b); // 1
console.log(a ** b); // 1000

2ï¸âƒ£ Assignment Operators

Used to assign values.

Operator	Description	Example (x = 5)	Result
=	Assign	x = 5	5
+=	Add and assign	x += 2	7
-=	Subtract and assign	x -= 2	3
*=	Multiply and assign	x *= 2	10
/=	Divide and assign	x /= 2	2.5
%=	Modulus and assign	x %= 2	1

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let x = 5;
x += 3;  // x = 8
x *= 2;  // x = 16
console.log(x); // 16

3ï¸âƒ£ Comparison Operators

Used to compare values (return true/false).

Operator	Description	Example (a = 5, b = "5", c = 10)	Result
==	Equal (value only)	a == b	true
===	Strict equal (value + type)	a === b	false
!=	Not equal (value only)	a != c	true
!==	Strict not equal	a !== b	true
>	Greater than	c > a	true
<	Less than	a < c	true
>=	Greater than or equal	a >= 5	true
<=	Less than or equal	a <= 4	false

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let a = 5, b = "5", c = 10;

console.log(a == b);  // true
console.log(a === b); // false
console.log(a != c);  // true
console.log(c > a);   // true

4ï¸âƒ£ Logical Operators

Used for conditions (return true/false).

Operator	Description	Example (x = true, y = false)	Result
&&	Logical AND	x && y	false
||	Logical OR	`x	
!	Logical NOT	!x	false

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let x = true, y = false;

console.log(x && y); // false
console.log(x || y); // true
console.log(!x);     // false

5ï¸âƒ£ Ternary Operator (? :)

A shorthand for if-else.

ğŸ‘‰ Syntax:

condition ? valueIfTrue : valueIfFalse;


ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let age = 18;
let result = (age >= 18) ? "Eligible to vote" : "Not eligible";
console.log(result); // Eligible to vote

âœ… Quick Summary

Arithmetic â†’ + - * / % ** ++ --

Assignment â†’ = += -= *= /= %=

Comparison â†’ == === != !== > < >= <=

Logical â†’ && || !

Ternary â†’ condition ? trueValue : falseValue

========================================
Type Conversion & Coercion
========================================
Type Conversion vs Type Coercion

Type Conversion (Explicit Casting)
â†’ You manually convert a value from one type to another using built-in functions (Number(), String(), Boolean()).

Type Coercion (Implicit Casting)
â†’ JavaScript automatically converts data types when different types are used together (behind the scenes).

1ï¸âƒ£ Type Conversion (Explicit)

You decide how to convert the type.

ğŸ‘‰ Example 1: String â†’ Number

let str = "123";
let num = Number(str);

console.log(num); // 123
console.log(typeof num); // number


ğŸ‘‰ Example 2: Number â†’ String

let age = 25;
let strAge = String(age);

console.log(strAge); // "25"
console.log(typeof strAge); // string


ğŸ‘‰ Example 3: Truthy/Falsy â†’ Boolean

console.log(Boolean(1));      // true
console.log(Boolean(0));      // false
console.log(Boolean("hello"));// true
console.log(Boolean(""));     // false
console.log(Boolean(null));   // false


ğŸ‘‰ Example 4: Parse methods

let strNum = "50.75";
console.log(parseInt(strNum));   // 50
console.log(parseFloat(strNum)); // 50.75

2ï¸âƒ£ Type Coercion (Implicit)

JavaScript automatically converts data types depending on context.

ğŸ‘‰ Example 1: String + Number â†’ String

console.log("5" + 2); // "52" (number coerced to string)


ğŸ‘‰ Example 2: Number - String â†’ Number

console.log("5" - 2); // 3 (string coerced to number)
console.log("5" * "2"); // 10


ğŸ‘‰ Example 3: Boolean coercion in operations

console.log(true + 1);  // 2  (true â†’ 1)
console.log(false + 1); // 1  (false â†’ 0)


ğŸ‘‰ Example 4: Equality operators (== vs ===)

console.log(5 == "5");  // true  (coercion: "5" â†’ 5)
console.log(5 === "5"); // false (strict: no coercion)

âš¡ Special Cases of Coercion
console.log("" + 1);    // "1"   (string coercion)
console.log("5" - 1);   // 4     (number coercion)
console.log(null + 1);  // 1     (null â†’ 0)
console.log(undefined + 1); // NaN (undefined â†’ NaN)

ğŸ“Š Summary Table
Value	Number(value)	String(value)	Boolean(value)
"123"	123	"123"	true
"abc"	NaN	"abc"	true
true	1	"true"	true
false	0	"false"	false
null	0	"null"	false
undefined	NaN	"undefined"	false
"" (empty)	0	""	false

âœ… Key Takeaway

Type Conversion â†’ Explicit, you control it (Number("5")).

Type Coercion â†’ Implicit, JS does it automatically ("5" - 2 â†’ 3).

======================================================================
2. Control Flow (Done)
Conditional Statements (if, else if, else, switch) with best examples
======================================================================
. if Statement

Executes a block of code if the condition is true.

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let age = 20;

if (age >= 18) {
  console.log("You are eligible to vote.");
}


âœ… Output â†’ You are eligible to vote.

ğŸ“Œ 2. if ... else Statement

Provides an alternative block if the condition is false.

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let isLoggedIn = false;

if (isLoggedIn) {
  console.log("Welcome back, User!");
} else {
  console.log("Please log in first.");
}


âœ… Output â†’ Please log in first.

ğŸ“Œ 3. if ... else if ... else Ladder

Used when you need to test multiple conditions.

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let marks = 75;

if (marks >= 90) {
  console.log("Grade: A+");
} else if (marks >= 75) {
  console.log("Grade: A");
} else if (marks >= 50) {
  console.log("Grade: B");
} else {
  console.log("Grade: Fail");
}


âœ… Output â†’ Grade: A

ğŸ“Œ 4. switch Statement

Used when you want to compare the same variable with multiple values.

Cleaner than multiple else if statements.

Uses case and requires break to stop execution.

ğŸ‘‰ Example:

let day = 3;
let dayName;

switch (day) {
  case 1:
    dayName = "Monday";
    break;
  case 2:
    dayName = "Tuesday";
    break;
  case 3:
    dayName = "Wednesday";
    break;
  case 4:
    dayName = "Thursday";
    break;
  case 5:
    dayName = "Friday";
    break;
  case 6:
    dayName = "Saturday";
    break;
  case 7:
    dayName = "Sunday";
    break;
  default:
    dayName = "Invalid day";
}
console.log(dayName);


âœ… Output â†’ Wednesday

ğŸ“Š Quick Comparison
Statement	Use Case
if	Single condition
if...else	Two-way choice
else if	Multiple conditions
switch	Multiple fixed values for a single variable
âš¡ Real-World Example (Login System)
let role = "admin";

if (role === "admin") {
  console.log("Access granted: You can manage everything.");
} else if (role === "editor") {
  console.log("Access granted: You can edit content.");
} else if (role === "user") {
  console.log("Access granted: You can view content.");
} else {
  console.log("Access denied: Unknown role.");
}


âœ… Output â†’ Access granted: You can manage everything.


=======================================================
Loops (for, while, do...while, for...of, for...in)
=======================================================
. for Loop

Used when you know how many times you want to repeat something.

ğŸ‘‰ Example: Print numbers 1 to 5

for (let i = 1; i <= 5; i++) {
  console.log("Number:", i);
}


âœ… Output:

Number: 1  
Number: 2  
Number: 3  
Number: 4  
Number: 5  

ğŸ“Œ 2. while Loop

Executes code while a condition is true.
Used when you donâ€™t know the exact number of iterations in advance.

ğŸ‘‰ Example: Print numbers 1 to 5

let i = 1;
while (i <= 5) {
  console.log("While Loop:", i);
  i++;
}

ğŸ“Œ 3. do...while Loop

Like while, but ensures the block runs at least once, even if the condition is false.

ğŸ‘‰ Example: Run at least once

let j = 6;
do {
  console.log("Do While Loop:", j);
  j++;
} while (j <= 5);


âœ… Output:

Do While Loop: 6


(Even though condition was false, it ran once.)

ğŸ“Œ 4. for...of Loop

Used to iterate over arrays, strings, or other iterable objects.

ğŸ‘‰ Example: Loop through array

let fruits = ["Apple", "Banana", "Mango"];

for (let fruit of fruits) {
  console.log("Fruit:", fruit);
}


âœ… Output:

Fruit: Apple  
Fruit: Banana  
Fruit: Mango  


ğŸ‘‰ Example: Loop through string

let word = "JS";

for (let char of word) {
  console.log("Character:", char);
}


âœ… Output:

Character: J  
Character: S  

ğŸ“Œ 5. for...in Loop

Used to iterate over object properties (keys).

ğŸ‘‰ Example: Loop through object

let user = { name: "Suraj", age: 25, country: "India" };

for (let key in user) {
  console.log(key, ":", user[key]);
}


âœ… Output:

name : Suraj  
age : 25  
country : India  

ğŸ“Š Quick Comparison
Loop Type	Best Use Case
for	Known number of iterations
while	Unknown number of iterations (runs while condition is true)
do...while	Run at least once, then check condition
for...of	Iterate over arrays, strings, sets, maps
for...in	Iterate over object keys
âš¡ Real-World Example: Shopping Cart
let cart = [
  { item: "Book", price: 200 },
  { item: "Pen", price: 50 },
  { item: "Bag", price: 500 }
];

let total = 0;
for (let product of cart) {
  total += product.price;
}
console.log("Total Price:", total);


âœ… Output â†’ Total Price: 750

============================================
Break & Continue
============================================
1. break Statement

ğŸ‘‰ Used to exit a loop completely when a condition is met.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Stop searching after finding an item in stock
let products = ["Laptop", "Phone", "Tablet", "Camera"];
let search = "Tablet";

for (let item of products) {
  console.log("Checking:", item);

  if (item === search) {
    console.log("âœ… Found:", item);
    break; // Exit the loop
  }
}


âœ… Output:

Checking: Laptop  
Checking: Phone  
Checking: Tablet  
âœ… Found: Tablet


ğŸ‘‰ The loop stops after finding Tablet.

ğŸ”¹ 2. continue Statement

ğŸ‘‰ Used to skip the current iteration and move to the next one.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Skip out-of-stock items in a shopping cart
let cart = [
  { item: "Book", stock: true },
  { item: "Pen", stock: false },
  { item: "Bag", stock: true }
];

for (let product of cart) {
  if (!product.stock) {
    console.log("â›” Skipping:", product.item);
    continue; // Skip this product
  }
  console.log("ğŸ›’ Adding to cart:", product.item);
}


âœ… Output:

ğŸ›’ Adding to cart: Book  
â›” Skipping: Pen  
ğŸ›’ Adding to cart: Bag  


ğŸ‘‰ The loop skips the Pen since stock is false.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Real-Life Example: Login Attempts (using break)
let correctPassword = "12345";
let attempts = ["11111", "22222", "12345", "44444"];

for (let attempt of attempts) {
  if (attempt === correctPassword) {
    console.log("ğŸ” Login Successful!");
    break; // Stop checking after correct password
  }
  console.log("âŒ Wrong Password:", attempt);
}


âœ… Output:

âŒ Wrong Password: 11111  
âŒ Wrong Password: 22222  
ğŸ” Login Successful!

ğŸ”¹ 4. Real-Life Example: Skipping Invalid Emails (using continue)
let emails = ["test@gmail.com", "", "user@yahoo.com", null, "admin@site.com"];

for (let email of emails) {
  if (!email) {
    console.log("âš ï¸ Invalid email, skipping...");
    continue; // Skip empty/null emails
  }
  console.log("ğŸ“© Sending mail to:", email);
}


âœ… Output:

ğŸ“© Sending mail to: test@gmail.com  
âš ï¸ Invalid email, skipping...  
ğŸ“© Sending mail to: user@yahoo.com  
âš ï¸ Invalid email, skipping...  
ğŸ“© Sending mail to: admin@site.com  


âœ¨ Quick Summary

break â†’ Exit the loop immediately.

continue â†’ Skip current iteration, continue with the next.

============================================
Error Handling (try...catch...finally, throw)
============================================
JavaScript provides:

try...catch â†’ To handle runtime errors.

finally â†’ Runs cleanup code regardless of success/failure.

throw â†’ Used to create custom errors.

Letâ€™s see real-world best examples ğŸ‘‡

ğŸ”¹ 1. Basic try...catch

ğŸ‘‰ Prevent app crash when error occurs.

try {
  let user = JSON.parse('{"name":"Suraj", "age":25}');
  console.log("âœ… User loaded:", user.name);
} catch (error) {
  console.log("âŒ Failed to parse user data:", error.message);
}


âœ… Output:

âœ… User loaded: Suraj


If JSON was invalid â†’ âŒ Failed to parse user data: Unexpected token ...

ğŸ”¹ 2. finally (Cleanup Always Runs)

ğŸ‘‰ Example: Closing DB connection or loader spinner.

function fetchData() {
  try {
    console.log("â³ Fetching data...");
    throw new Error("Server not responding!");
  } catch (error) {
    console.log("âŒ Error:", error.message);
  } finally {
    console.log("ğŸ”„ Closing connection / Hiding loader...");
  }
}

fetchData();


âœ… Output:

â³ Fetching data...  
âŒ Error: Server not responding!  
ğŸ”„ Closing connection / Hiding loader...

ğŸ”¹ 3. throw (Custom Errors)

ğŸ‘‰ Example: Validate form input.

function registerUser(name, age) {
  try {
    if (!name) throw new Error("Name is required!");
    if (age < 18) throw new Error("User must be 18+ to register.");

    console.log(`âœ… Registered: ${name}, Age: ${age}`);
  } catch (error) {
    console.log("âš ï¸ Registration failed:", error.message);
  }
}

registerUser("Suraj", 25);   // Works
registerUser("", 20);        // Error: Name is required
registerUser("Ravi", 15);    // Error: Must be 18+


âœ… Output:

âœ… Registered: Suraj, Age: 25  
âš ï¸ Registration failed: Name is required!  
âš ï¸ Registration failed: User must be 18+ to register.

ğŸ”¹ 4. Real-Life Example: API Call with try...catch

ğŸ‘‰ Handling API fetch errors.

async function getUsers() {
  try {
    let response = await fetch("https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/users");

    if (!response.ok) {
      throw new Error("Failed to fetch users!");
    }

    let data = await response.json();
    console.log("âœ… Users:", data);
  } catch (error) {
    console.log("âŒ API Error:", error.message);
  } finally {
    console.log("ğŸ”„ API call completed!");
  }
}

getUsers();


âœ… Handles cases like no internet, wrong URL, or server errors.

ğŸ”¹ 5. Nested try...catch Example

ğŸ‘‰ Example: Payment process (card check â†’ transaction).

function processPayment(card, amount) {
  try {
    if (!card) throw new Error("Card not found!");

    try {
      if (amount <= 0) throw new Error("Invalid amount!");
      console.log(`âœ… Payment of $${amount} successful.`);
    } catch (err) {
      console.log("âš ï¸ Transaction failed:", err.message);
    }

  } catch (err) {
    console.log("âŒ Payment error:", err.message);
  } finally {
    console.log("ğŸ”„ Closing payment session...");
  }
}

processPayment("Visa", 100);   // Works  
processPayment("Visa", 0);     // Transaction failed  
processPayment("", 200);       // Card not found  


âœ… Summary Table

Statement	Purpose
try	Test a block of code.
catch	Handle error if occurs.
finally	Always runs (cleanup).
throw	Create a custom error.

============================================================
3. Functions
Function Declaration & Expression
============================================================
In JavaScript, functions are building blocks. There are mainly two common ways to define them:

Function Declaration

Function Expression

Letâ€™s go step by step with examples ğŸ‘‡

ğŸ”¹ 1. Function Declaration

ğŸ‘‰ A named function defined using the function keyword.

Can be called before or after its definition (hoisting).

ğŸ“Œ Example: Greeting Function
// Function Declaration
function greet(name) {
  return `Hello, ${name}! ğŸ‘‹`;
}

console.log(greet("Suraj")); // âœ… Can call here also


âœ… Output:

Hello, Suraj! ğŸ‘‹


ğŸ‘‰ Key Point: Function Declarations are hoisted (moved to the top of scope automatically).

ğŸ”¹ 2. Function Expression

ğŸ‘‰ A function assigned to a variable (anonymous or named).

Not hoisted â†’ must be defined before use.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Sum Function
// Function Expression
const sum = function(a, b) {
  return a + b;
};

console.log(sum(5, 10)); // âœ… Must call after definition


âœ… Output:

15

ğŸ”¹ Differences (Declaration vs Expression)
Feature	Function Declaration	Function Expression
Hoisting	âœ… Yes (can call before defining)	âŒ No (must define first)
Name	Always has a name	Can be anonymous or named
Usage	For reusable named functions	For callbacks, dynamic use
Syntax	function myFunc(){}	const myFunc = function(){}
ğŸ”¹ 3. Real-Life Examples
âœ… Function Declaration (Login check)
function checkLogin(username, password) {
  return username === "admin" && password === "12345";
}

console.log(checkLogin("admin", "12345")); // true
console.log(checkLogin("guest", "12345")); // false

âœ… Function Expression (Button click handler)
const handleClick = function() {
  console.log("ğŸ”˜ Button clicked!");
};

// Imagine attaching this in HTML button
// <button onclick="handleClick()">Click Me</button>

handleClick(); // Works like an event callback


âš¡ Bonus: Arrow Functions (a shorthand of Function Expression)

const multiply = (x, y) => x * y;
console.log(multiply(4, 5)); // 20

============================================================
Parameters & Arguments
============================================================
In JavaScript, parameters and arguments are related but not the same:

ğŸ”¹ Parameters vs Arguments

Parameters â†’ Variables defined in the function declaration (placeholders).

Arguments â†’ Actual values passed when calling the function.

ğŸ‘‰ Think of it like this:

Parameters = Empty slots.

Arguments = The actual data you put in those slots.

ğŸ”¹ Example 1: Simple Function
// Parameters: name, age
function introduce(name, age) {
  console.log(`Hello, my name is ${name} and I am ${age} years old.`);
}

// Arguments: "Suraj", 25
introduce("Suraj", 25);


âœ… Output:

Hello, my name is Suraj and I am 25 years old.

ğŸ”¹ Example 2: Real-Life Shopping Cart
// Parameters: item, price, quantity
function addToCart(item, price, quantity) {
  let total = price * quantity;
  console.log(`ğŸ›’ Added ${quantity} ${item}(s). Total = â‚¹${total}`);
}

// Arguments: "Book", 200, 3
addToCart("Book", 200, 3);


âœ… Output:

ğŸ›’ Added 3 Book(s). Total = â‚¹600

ğŸ”¹ Example 3: Default Parameters

ğŸ‘‰ You can give default values to parameters if no arguments are passed.

function greetUser(name = "Guest") {
  console.log(`Welcome, ${name}!`);
}

greetUser("Suraj"); // Argument: "Suraj"
greetUser();        // No argument â†’ uses default


âœ… Output:

Welcome, Suraj!
Welcome, Guest!

ğŸ”¹ Example 4: Rest Parameters (...)

ğŸ‘‰ Used when number of arguments is unknown.

function calculateTotal(...prices) {
  let sum = 0;
  for (let price of prices) {
    sum += price;
  }
  console.log("ğŸ’° Total Price:", sum);
}

// Multiple arguments
calculateTotal(200, 150, 50, 100);


âœ… Output:

ğŸ’° Total Price: 500

ğŸ”¹ Example 5: Real-Life â€” Sending Email
function sendEmail(to, subject, message = "No message") {
  console.log(`ğŸ“§ Email sent to: ${to}`);
  console.log(`   Subject: ${subject}`);
  console.log(`   Message: ${message}`);
}

// Passing arguments
sendEmail("user@gmail.com", "Welcome!", "Thanks for joining us.");
sendEmail("admin@gmail.com", "Alert"); // message uses default


âœ… Output:

ğŸ“§ Email sent to: user@gmail.com
   Subject: Welcome!
   Message: Thanks for joining us.
ğŸ“§ Email sent to: admin@gmail.com
   Subject: Alert
   Message: No message


âœ… Summary Table

Term	Meaning
Parameters	Placeholders inside function definition (function(x, y))
Arguments	Actual values passed (myFunc(5, 10))
Default Parameters	Provide fallback values
Rest Parameters	Handle unlimited arguments

============================================================
Return Values
============================================================
In JavaScript, functions can return values using the return keyword. The return statement:

Sends a value back to the caller.

Ends function execution immediately.

Can return any data type (string, number, object, array, function, etc.).

ğŸ”¹ 1. Basic Return Example
function add(a, b) {
  return a + b; // returning result
}

let sum = add(5, 10);
console.log("âœ… Sum is:", sum);


âœ… Output:

âœ… Sum is: 15

ğŸ”¹ 2. Real-Life Example: Discount Calculation
function calculateDiscount(price, discountPercent) {
  let discount = (price * discountPercent) / 100;
  let finalPrice = price - discount;
  return finalPrice;
}

let discountedPrice = calculateDiscount(1000, 10); 
console.log("ğŸ’° Final Price after Discount:", discountedPrice);


âœ… Output:

ğŸ’° Final Price after Discount: 900

ğŸ”¹ 3. Returning Objects

ğŸ‘‰ Useful for APIs or structured data.

  return {
    username: name,
    userAge: age,
    isActive: true
  };
}

âœ… Output:

ğŸ‘¤ User: { username: 'Suraj', userAge: 25, isActive: true }

ğŸ”¹ 4. Returning Arrays

  return ["Amit", "Neha", "Suraj"];

âœ… Output:

ğŸ“ Top Students: [ 'Amit', 'Neha', 'Suraj' ]

ğŸ”¹ 5. Real-Life Example: Checking Login

  if (username === "admin" && password === "12345") {
    return "âœ… Login Successful!";
  }
  return "âŒ Invalid Credentials!";

âœ… Output:

âœ… Login Successful!
âŒ Invalid Credentials!

ğŸ”¹ 6. Returning Functions (Higher-Order Function)

ğŸ‘‰ Useful in real-world apps (callbacks, closures, functional programming).

  return function(number) {
    return number * factor;
  };
}

let triple = multiplier(3);

console.log("Triple of 5:", triple(5));


âœ… Output:

Double of 5: 10
Triple of 5: 15

âš¡ Summary

return sends values back to where the function was called.

Functions can return â†’ numbers, strings, objects, arrays, functions.

Useful for â†’ calculations, API data, validation, reusable logic.

============================================================
Arrow Functions () => {}
============================================================
What are Arrow Functions?

Introduced in ES6 (2015).

A shorter syntax for writing functions.

Do not have their own this, arguments, super, or new.target.

Mostly used for callbacks, array methods, and concise functions.

ğŸ”¹ 1. Basic Syntax
// Normal function
function add(a, b) {
  return a + b;
}

// Arrow function
const addArrow = (a, b) => a + b;

console.log("Normal:", add(5, 10));
console.log("Arrow:", addArrow(5, 10));


âœ… Output:

Normal: 15
Arrow: 15

ğŸ”¹ 2. With One Parameter
const greet = name => `Hello, ${name}! ğŸ‘‹`;

console.log(greet("Suraj"));


âœ… Output:

Hello, Suraj! ğŸ‘‹

ğŸ”¹ 3. With No Parameters
const welcome = () => "ğŸ‰ Welcome to our website!";

console.log(welcome());


âœ… Output:

ğŸ‰ Welcome to our website!

ğŸ”¹ 4. Real-Life Example: Shopping Cart (Arrow Function + Map)
let cart = [
  { item: "Book", price: 200 },
  { item: "Pen", price: 50 },
  { item: "Bag", price: 500 }
];

// Arrow function inside map()
let prices = cart.map(product => product.price);

console.log("ğŸ’° Prices:", prices);


âœ… Output:

ğŸ’° Prices: [200, 50, 500]

ğŸ”¹ 5. Real-Life Example: Filtering Users
let users = [
  { name: "Suraj", age: 25 },
  { name: "Amit", age: 17 },
  { name: "Neha", age: 30 }
];

// Arrow function inside filter()
let adults = users.filter(user => user.age >= 18);

console.log("âœ… Adults:", adults);


âœ… Output:

âœ… Adults: [ { name: 'Suraj', age: 25 }, { name: 'Neha', age: 30 } ]

ğŸ”¹ 6. Arrow Function with this (Important Difference)

ğŸ‘‰ Normal functions create their own this, but arrow functions do not.

// Normal function
const person1 = {
  name: "Suraj",
  greet: function() {
    console.log("Hello, I'm " + this.name);
  }
};
person1.greet();

// Arrow function (doesn't bind `this`)
const person2 = {
  name: "Suraj",
  greet: () => {
    console.log("Hello, I'm " + this.name); // `this` = undefined/global
  }
};
person2.greet();


âœ… Output:

Hello, I'm Suraj
Hello, I'm undefined


ğŸ‘‰ So:

Use normal functions when you need this.

Use arrow functions for short callbacks where this isnâ€™t needed.

ğŸ”¹ 7. Real-Life Example: Event Listener
// Normal function
document.getElementById("btn").addEventListener("click", function() {
  console.log("Button clicked âœ…");
});

// Arrow function
document.getElementById("btn").addEventListener("click", () => {
  console.log("Button clicked with Arrow âœ…");
});

âš¡ Summary

Arrow Functions = Short, cleaner, best for callbacks.

Do not bind their own this â†’ useful in avoiding bugs.

Widely used in â†’ array methods (map, filter, reduce), event handlers, async code.

============================================================
What are Default Parameters?

Introduced in ES6 (2015).

Allow you to set default values for function parameters if no argument (or undefined) is provided.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Real-Life Example: Online Shopping Delivery

ğŸ”¹ 3. Real-Life Example: Event Booking

============================================================
Rest & Spread Operators (...)
============================================================
The ... (three dots) in JavaScript have two different roles depending on where they are used:

Rest Operator (...) â†’ Collects multiple values into an array.

Spread Operator (...) â†’ Spreads (expands) values from an array/object.

Letâ€™s go step by step with real-based examples ğŸ‘‡

ğŸ”¹ 1. Rest Operator (...)

ğŸ‘‰ Used in function parameters to gather remaining arguments into an array.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Shopping Cart (Unknown number of items)
function addToCart(...items) {
  console.log("ğŸ›’ Items in cart:", items);
}

addToCart("Book", "Pen", "Laptop");


âœ… Output:

ğŸ›’ Items in cart: [ 'Book', 'Pen', 'Laptop' ]

ğŸ“Œ Example: Calculate Total Price
function calculateTotal(...prices) {
  return prices.reduce((sum, price) => sum + price, 0);
}

console.log("ğŸ’° Total:", calculateTotal(200, 150, 50, 100));


âœ… Output:

ğŸ’° Total: 500

ğŸ”¹ 2. Spread Operator (...)

ğŸ‘‰ Used to expand arrays/objects into individual elements.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Copying Arrays (Avoid mutation)
let fruits = ["Apple", "Banana", "Mango"];
let newFruits = [...fruits, "Orange"];

console.log("ğŸ Fruits:", newFruits);


âœ… Output:

ğŸ Fruits: [ 'Apple', 'Banana', 'Mango', 'Orange' ]

ğŸ“Œ Example: Merging Arrays
let electronics = ["Laptop", "Phone"];
let accessories = ["Charger", "Headphones"];

let storeItems = [...electronics, ...accessories];
console.log("ğŸ¬ Store Items:", storeItems);


âœ… Output:

ğŸ¬ Store Items: [ 'Laptop', 'Phone', 'Charger', 'Headphones' ]

ğŸ“Œ Example: Copying & Updating Objects
let user = { name: "Suraj", age: 25 };
let updatedUser = { ...user, country: "India" };

console.log("ğŸ‘¤ User:", updatedUser);


âœ… Output:

ğŸ‘¤ User: { name: 'Suraj', age: 25, country: 'India' }

ğŸ“Œ Example: Passing Array as Function Arguments
function sum(a, b, c) {
  return a + b + c;
}

let numbers = [10, 20, 30];
console.log("â• Sum:", sum(...numbers));


âœ… Output:

â• Sum: 60

ğŸ”¹ 3. Real-Life Examples
âœ… Rest: Event Attendance System
function registerEvent(eventName, ...participants) {
  console.log(`ğŸ‰ Event: ${eventName}`);
  console.log("ğŸ‘¥ Participants:", participants);
}

âœ… Output:

ğŸ‰ Event: Hackathon
ğŸ‘¥ Participants: [ 'Suraj', 'Neha', 'Amit' ]

âœ… Spread: API Response Merge

let apiResponse2 = { age: 25, country: "India" };

let userProfile = { ...apiResponse1, ...apiResponse2 };
console.log("ğŸ“¡ User Profile:", userProfile);


âœ… Output:

ğŸ“¡ User Profile: { id: 1, name: 'Suraj', age: 25, country: 'India' }

âš¡ Summary
Operator	Meaning	Use Case
Rest (...)	Collects arguments into an array	Unknown number of function arguments
Spread (...)	Expands arrays/objects	Copy, merge, pass elements

============================================================
Callback Functions
============================================================
What is a Callback Function?

A callback function is a function passed as an argument to another function and executed later, usually after some task is completed.

ğŸ‘‰ They are heavily used in asynchronous JavaScript (e.g., API calls, timers, events).

ğŸ”¹ 1. Basic Example of Callback
function greetUser(name, callback) {
  console.log("Hello, " + name);
  callback(); // calling the callback function
}

function sayGoodbye() {
  console.log("Goodbye! ğŸ‘‹");
}

greetUser("Suraj", sayGoodbye);


âœ… Output:

Hello, Suraj
Goodbye! ğŸ‘‹

ğŸ”¹ 2. Callback with Anonymous Function
function processNumber(num, callback) {
  let result = num * 2;
  callback(result);
}

processNumber(5, function(output) {
  console.log("Doubled Number:", output);
});


âœ… Output:

Doubled Number: 10

ğŸ”¹ 3. Real-Life Examples of Callback Functions
ğŸ“Œ Example 1: Using setTimeout (Simulating async task like order confirmation)
function placeOrder(order, callback) {
  console.log(`ğŸ“¦ Placing order for: ${order}...`);
  
  setTimeout(() => {
    console.log(`âœ… Order ready: ${order}`);
    callback();
  }, 2000);
}

placeOrder("Pizza", function() {
  console.log("ğŸšš Delivered to your address!");
});


âœ… Output:

ğŸ“¦ Placing order for: Pizza...
âœ… Order ready: Pizza
ğŸšš Delivered to your address!

ğŸ“Œ Example 2: Reading File (Simulation)
function readFile(fileName, callback) {
  console.log(`ğŸ“‚ Reading file: ${fileName}...`);

  setTimeout(() => {
    callback(`File content of ${fileName}`);
  }, 1500);
}

readFile("notes.txt", function(content) {
  console.log("ğŸ“‘ File Data:", content);
});


âœ… Output:

ğŸ“‚ Reading file: notes.txt...
ğŸ“‘ File Data: File content of notes.txt

ğŸ“Œ Example 3: Event Listener (Button Click)
<button id="clickBtn">Click Me</button>

<script>

  console.log("ğŸ‘† Button was clicked!");
});
</script>


âœ… Output when button is clicked:

ğŸ‘† Button was clicked!

ğŸ”¹ 4. Callback in Array Methods

ğŸ‘‰ Functions like map, filter, forEach use callbacks.

let numbers = [1, 2, 3, 4, 5];

let doubled = numbers.map(function(num) {
  return num * 2;
});

console.log("ğŸ”¢ Doubled:", doubled);


âœ… Output:

ğŸ”¢ Doubled: [ 2, 4, 6, 8, 10 ]

âš¡ Summary

A callback function is passed as an argument to another function.

Used in async programming (API calls, setTimeout, events).

Real-life use cases: order system, file reading, button clicks, array operations.

============================================================
Higher-Order Functions
============================================================
What is a Higher-Order Function?

A Higher-Order Function (HOF) is a function that either:

Takes another function as an argument (callback), OR

Returns a function.

ğŸ‘‰ In JavaScript, functions are first-class citizens, meaning they can be passed around like variables.

ğŸ”¹ 1. HOF Taking a Function as Argument
ğŸ“Œ Example: Payment System
function makePayment(amount, onSuccess, onFailure) {
  if (amount > 0) {
    onSuccess(amount); // calling callback
  } else {
    onFailure("Invalid payment amount");
  }
}

makePayment(
  500,
  (amt) => console.log(`âœ… Payment of â‚¹${amt} successful!`),
  (error) => console.log(`âŒ Payment failed: ${error}`)
);


âœ… Output:

âœ… Payment of â‚¹500 successful!

ğŸ”¹ 2. HOF Returning Another Function
ğŸ“Œ Example: Discount Generator
function discountCalculator(discount) {
  return function(price) {
    return price - (price * discount);
  };
}

let studentDiscount = discountCalculator(0.2); // 20% discount
console.log("ğŸ“ Student Price:", studentDiscount(1000));

let festivalDiscount = discountCalculator(0.5); // 50% discount
console.log("ğŸ‰ Festival Price:", festivalDiscount(1000));


âœ… Output:

ğŸ“ Student Price: 800
ğŸ‰ Festival Price: 500

ğŸ”¹ 3. Real-Based Examples Using Built-in HOFs
âœ… Example 1: Online Store â€“ map
let products = [100, 200, 300];
let gstPrices = products.map(price => price + price * 0.18);

console.log("ğŸ›ï¸ Prices with GST:", gstPrices);


âœ… Output:

ğŸ›ï¸ Prices with GST: [ 118, 236, 354 ]

âœ… Example 2: Filtering Students â€“ filter
let students = [
  { name: "Suraj", marks: 85 },
  { name: "Neha", marks: 45 },
  { name: "Amit", marks: 70 }
];

let passed = students.filter(student => student.marks >= 50);
console.log("ğŸ“ Passed Students:", passed);


âœ… Output:

ğŸ“ Passed Students: [ { name: 'Suraj', marks: 85 }, { name: 'Amit', marks: 70 } ]

âœ… Example 3: Shopping Cart Total â€“ reduce
let cart = [200, 150, 50];

let total = cart.reduce((sum, price) => sum + price, 0);
console.log("ğŸ’° Cart Total:", total);


âœ… Output:

ğŸ’° Cart Total: 400

âœ… Example 4: Event Management â€“ forEach
let attendees = ["Suraj", "Neha", "Amit"];

attendees.forEach(person => console.log(`ğŸ‰ Welcome, ${person}!`));


âœ… Output:

ğŸ‰ Welcome, Suraj!
ğŸ‰ Welcome, Neha!
ğŸ‰ Welcome, Amit!

ğŸ”¹ 4. HOF in Real-World Automation
ğŸ“Œ Example: Email Notification System
function sendNotification(users, callback) {
  users.forEach(user => {
    callback(user);
  });
}

let users = ["suraj@example.com", "neha@example.com"];

sendNotification(users, (email) => {
  console.log(`ğŸ“§ Email sent to ${email}`);
});


âœ… Output:

ğŸ“§ Email sent to suraj@example.com
ğŸ“§ Email sent to neha@example.com

âš¡ Summary
Concept	Example
Takes a function as argument	map, filter, reduce, custom callback systems
Returns a function	Discount calculator, configuration functions
Real-life use	Payments, shopping carts, student filtering, notifications

===========================================================
Scope (Block, Function, Global)
===========================================================
What is Scope?

Scope defines where a variable can be accessed in your code.
In JavaScript, we mainly have:

Block Scope

Function Scope

Global Scope

ğŸ”¹ 1. Global Scope ğŸŒ

ğŸ‘‰ A variable declared outside any function or block is global.
ğŸ‘‰ Accessible from anywhere in the program.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Website Theme
let theme = "ğŸŒ™ Dark Mode"; // Global Scope

function showTheme() {
  console.log("Current Theme:", theme);
}

showTheme();
console.log("Applied Theme Everywhere:", theme);


âœ… Output:

Current Theme: ğŸŒ™ Dark Mode
Applied Theme Everywhere: ğŸŒ™ Dark Mode


ğŸ’¡ Real-life: A global theme applies to all pages of a website.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Function Scope ğŸ”§

ğŸ‘‰ Variables declared with var, let, or const inside a function are only available within that function.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Online Banking â€“ Balance
function checkBalance() {
  let balance = 5000; // Function Scope
  console.log("ğŸ’° Inside function - Balance:", balance);
}

checkBalance();
// console.log(balance); // âŒ Error: balance is not defined


âœ… Output:

ğŸ’° Inside function - Balance: 5000


ğŸ’¡ Real-life: Your bank balance is private inside your account, not visible outside.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Block Scope ğŸ“¦

ğŸ‘‰ Variables declared with let or const inside a block {} are only accessible within that block.
ğŸ‘‰ var ignores block scope (it leaks out).

ğŸ“Œ Example: Discount Offer
if (true) {
  let discount = "ğŸ‰ 20% OFF";  // Block Scope
  console.log("Available Offer:", discount);
}

// console.log(discount); // âŒ Error: discount is not defined


âœ… Output:

Available Offer: ğŸ‰ 20% OFF


ğŸ’¡ Real-life: Discounts are limited to a festival period (block), not available globally.

ğŸ”¹ 4. Comparing var, let, and const in Scope
if (true) {
  var city = "Pune";     // function or global scope
  let state = "Maharashtra"; // block scope
  const country = "India";   // block scope
}

console.log(city);    // âœ… Works
// console.log(state);  // âŒ Error
// console.log(country);// âŒ Error


âœ… Output:

Pune


ğŸ’¡ var leaks out of blocks, while let and const stay inside.

ğŸ”¹ 5. Nested Scope (Scope Chain)

ğŸ‘‰ Inner functions can access variables from outer functions (but not vice versa).

ğŸ“Œ Example: Restaurant Order
let restaurant = "ğŸ´ Food Hub";

function placeOrder() {
  let order = "Pizza";

  function kitchen() {
    let chef = "ğŸ‘¨â€ğŸ³ Chef Ramesh";
    console.log(`${chef} is preparing ${order} at ${restaurant}`);
  }

  kitchen();
}

placeOrder();


âœ… Output:

ğŸ‘¨â€ğŸ³ Chef Ramesh is preparing Pizza at ğŸ´ Food Hub


ğŸ’¡ Real-life: The kitchen (inner scope) knows the restaurant and order, but the restaurant cannot see the chefâ€™s private details.

âš¡ Summary Table
Scope Type	Keyword	Access
Global	var, let, const (outside functions)	Accessible everywhere
Function	var, let, const (inside function)	Only inside that function
Block	let, const	Only inside {} block
Scope Chain	Nested scopes	Inner can access outer variables

===========================================================
Hoisting
===========================================================
What is Hoisting?

Hoisting is JavaScriptâ€™s default behavior of moving variable and function declarations to the top of their scope before code execution.

ğŸ‘‰ In simple words:
You can use functions or variables before they are declared (but with different behaviors depending on var, let, const).

ğŸ”¹ 1. Hoisting with var

var declarations are hoisted but initialized as undefined.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Shopping Cart Quantity
console.log("ğŸ›’ Items in cart:", items); // undefined
var items = 5;
console.log("ğŸ›’ Items after update:", items);


âœ… Output:

ğŸ›’ Items in cart: undefined
ğŸ›’ Items after update: 5


ğŸ’¡ Real-life: Like keeping a placeholder basket in a shopping cart â€” it exists, but itâ€™s empty (undefined) until you add items.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Hoisting with let and const

let and const are hoisted but not initialized.

They stay in the Temporal Dead Zone (TDZ) until the actual declaration.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Festival Discount
// console.log(discount); // âŒ ReferenceError
let discount = "ğŸ‰ 20% OFF";
console.log("Festival Discount:", discount);


âœ… Output:

Festival Discount: ğŸ‰ 20% OFF


ğŸ’¡ Real-life: The discount exists in system memory but you canâ€™t use it until the festival actually starts.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Function Hoisting

ğŸ‘‰ Function declarations are hoisted completely.
ğŸ‘‰ Function expressions/arrow functions behave like variables (var, let, const).

ğŸ“Œ Example: ATM Withdrawal
withdraw(2000); // âœ… Works because function is hoisted

function withdraw(amount) {
  console.log(`ğŸ’° Withdrawn: â‚¹${amount}`);
}


âœ… Output:

ğŸ’° Withdrawn: â‚¹2000

ğŸ“Œ Example: Function Expression (Not Hoisted Properly)
// withdraw(2000); // âŒ Error: Cannot access 'withdraw' before initialization

const withdraw = function(amount) {
  console.log(`ğŸ’° Withdrawn: â‚¹${amount}`);
};


ğŸ’¡ Real-life:

Function Declaration = ATM machine is always ready.

Function Expression = ATM machine isnâ€™t set up until staff configures it.

ğŸ”¹ 4. Real-Based Example: Restaurant Menu
console.log("Today's Special:", menu); // undefined

var menu = "ğŸ• Pizza";
console.log("Updated Menu:", menu);

// console.log(drink); // âŒ ReferenceError
let drink = "ğŸ¥¤ Coke";
console.log("Drink of the Day:", drink);


âœ… Output:

Today's Special: undefined
Updated Menu: ğŸ• Pizza
Drink of the Day: ğŸ¥¤ Coke


ğŸ’¡ Real-life:

Menu (var) exists but blank (undefined) until chef writes it.

Drink (let) canâ€™t even be seen until the staff puts it up.

âš¡ Summary Table
Keyword	Hoisted?	Initialized?	Access before declaration
var	âœ… Yes	undefined	Allowed, gives undefined
let	âœ… Yes	âŒ No	âŒ ReferenceError
const	âœ… Yes	âŒ No	âŒ ReferenceError
function	âœ… Yes	âœ… Yes	Allowed, fully usable
function expression	Like variable	Depends on var/let/const	Error or undefined

===========================================================
Closures
===========================================================
.

ğŸ”¹ What is a Closure?

A closure is created when a function remembers and accesses variables from its outer scope even after that outer function has finished executing.

ğŸ‘‰ In simple words:

"A closure gives you access to an outer functionâ€™s variables from an inner function, even after the outer function has closed."

ğŸ”¹ 1. Basic Closure Example
function outer() {
  let message = "Hello from Closure ğŸ‘‹";

  function inner() {
    console.log(message); // inner has access to outer's variable
  }

  return inner;
}

const greet = outer();
greet(); // calling inner even after outer is done


âœ… Output:

Hello from Closure ğŸ‘‹


ğŸ’¡ Even though outer() finished, inner() still remembers message.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Real-Life Examples of Closures
ğŸ“Œ Example 1: Bank Account (Private Balance)
function createBankAccount(initialBalance) {
  let balance = initialBalance; // private variable

  return {
    deposit: function(amount) {
      balance += amount;
      console.log(`ğŸ’° Deposited: â‚¹${amount}, New Balance: â‚¹${balance}`);
    },
    withdraw: function(amount) {
      if (amount <= balance) {
        balance -= amount;
        console.log(`ğŸ§ Withdrawn: â‚¹${amount}, Remaining Balance: â‚¹${balance}`);
      } else {
        console.log("âŒ Insufficient funds!");
      }
    },
    checkBalance: function() {
      console.log(`ğŸ“Š Current Balance: â‚¹${balance}`);
    }
  };
}

const myAccount = createBankAccount(1000);
myAccount.deposit(500);
myAccount.withdraw(300);
myAccount.checkBalance();


âœ… Output:

ğŸ’° Deposited: â‚¹500, New Balance: â‚¹1500
ğŸ§ Withdrawn: â‚¹300, Remaining Balance: â‚¹1200
ğŸ“Š Current Balance: â‚¹1200


ğŸ’¡ Here, balance is private. Itâ€™s not directly accessible from outside, only through closure functions.

ğŸ“Œ Example 2: Restaurant Order Counter
function orderCounter() {
  let count = 0; // private variable

  return function() {
    count++;
    console.log(`ğŸ½ï¸ Orders placed: ${count}`);
  };
}

const takeOrder = orderCounter();

takeOrder(); // ğŸ½ï¸ Orders placed: 1
takeOrder(); // ğŸ½ï¸ Orders placed: 2
takeOrder(); // ğŸ½ï¸ Orders placed: 3


ğŸ’¡ Closure keeps track of how many orders are placed even after orderCounter() has finished.

ğŸ“Œ Example 3: Event Booking System
function createEvent(eventName) {
  let attendees = [];

  return {
    register: function(name) {
      attendees.push(name);
      console.log(`ğŸŸï¸ ${name} registered for ${eventName}`);
    },
    listAttendees: function() {
      console.log(`ğŸ‘¥ Attendees for ${eventName}: ${attendees.join(", ")}`);
    }
  };
}

const hackathon = createEvent("Hackathon 2025");
hackathon.register("Suraj");
hackathon.register("Neha");
hackathon.listAttendees();


âœ… Output:

ğŸŸï¸ Suraj registered for Hackathon 2025
ğŸŸï¸ Neha registered for Hackathon 2025
ğŸ‘¥ Attendees for Hackathon 2025: Suraj, Neha


ğŸ’¡ Closure keeps track of attendees specific to each event.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Why Are Closures Useful?

âœ… Data privacy (like private variables)
âœ… Maintaining state (like counters, balance, attendees)
âœ… Used in callbacks, event handlers, and async code
âœ… Popular in module patterns

âš¡ Summary

A closure allows an inner function to access outer functionâ€™s variables even after the outer function finishes.

Real-life use: Bank accounts, order counters, booking systems, event management, private data.

===========================================================
The this keyword
===========================================================
What is this?

The this keyword refers to the object that is currently executing the function.
Its value depends on how (and where) a function is called.

ğŸ”¹ 1. Global Context ğŸŒ

In the global scope:

In browser, this â†’ window object.

In Node.js, this â†’ {} (empty object in modules).

ğŸ“Œ Example: Website Environment
console.log(this); // In browser â†’ window


ğŸ’¡ Real-life: Think of window as the entire website environment, and this points to it globally.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Inside an Object (Method Call)

When a function is called as an objectâ€™s method, this refers to that object.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Online Store Product
let product = {
  name: "Laptop",
  price: 50000,
  showDetails: function() {
    console.log(`ğŸ’» ${this.name} costs â‚¹${this.price}`);
  }
};

product.showDetails();


âœ… Output:

ğŸ’» Laptop costs â‚¹50000


ğŸ’¡ Real-life: this points to the product object, so it knows its own name and price.

ğŸ”¹ 3. In a Regular Function (Not Method)

If a function is called normally (not as object method):

In strict mode, this = undefined.

In non-strict mode, this = global object (window).

ğŸ“Œ Example: Random Utility Function
function show() {
  console.log(this);
}
show(); // window (in browser), undefined (in strict mode)

ğŸ”¹ 4. With this in Constructor Functions / Classes

When used inside a constructor function or class, this refers to the newly created object.

ğŸ“Œ Example: User Account System
function User(name, balance) {
  this.name = name;
  this.balance = balance;
}

let user1 = new User("Suraj", 1000);
console.log(`ğŸ‘¤ ${user1.name}, Balance: â‚¹${user1.balance}`);


âœ… Output:

ğŸ‘¤ Suraj, Balance: â‚¹1000


ğŸ’¡ Real-life: Each new user gets their own data, and this points to that user object.

ğŸ”¹ 5. Arrow Functions & this

ğŸ‘‰ Arrow functions do not have their own this.
ğŸ‘‰ They inherit this from their surrounding scope (lexical scope).

ğŸ“Œ Example: Event Listener
let button = {
  label: "Submit",
  click: function() {
    setTimeout(() => {
      console.log(`ğŸ”˜ Button clicked: ${this.label}`);
    }, 1000);
  }
};

button.click();


âœ… Output:

ğŸ”˜ Button clicked: Submit


ğŸ’¡ Here, arrow function keeps this from click method (the button object).

ğŸ”¹ 6. this in Event Listeners

ğŸ‘‰ In regular functions, this = the element that triggered the event.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Form Submit Button
<button id="submitBtn">Submit</button>

<script>
document.getElementById("submitBtn").addEventListener("click", function() {
  console.log("Clicked:", this.innerText); // "Submit"
});
</script>


âœ… Output (on click):

Clicked: Submit


ğŸ’¡ Real-life: this points to the button element itself.

ğŸ”¹ 7. call, apply, bind with this

ğŸ‘‰ We can manually control what this points to.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Borrowing Methods
let customer = { name: "Suraj" };
let bank = {
  name: "SBI",
  welcome: function() {
    console.log(`ğŸ¦ Welcome ${this.name} to our bank`);
  }
};

bank.welcome.call(customer);


âœ… Output:

ğŸ¦ Welcome Suraj to our bank


ğŸ’¡ Real-life: Like borrowing SBIâ€™s welcome message and applying it for Suraj.

âš¡ Summary Table
Context	this Refers To
Global (browser)	window
Object Method	The object
Function (strict)	undefined
Function (non-strict)	window
Constructor / Class	New object
Arrow Function	Inherits from parent scope
Event Listener	The element
call / apply / bind	Explicitly set object

===========================================================
Execution Context & Call Stack
===========================================================
1. What is Execution Context?

An Execution Context (EC) is the environment in which JavaScript code is evaluated and executed.

There are 3 main types:

Global Execution Context (GEC) â€“ Created when JS first runs (default).

Function Execution Context (FEC) â€“ Created every time a function is called.

Eval Execution Context (less common, using eval()).

ğŸ‘‰ Each context has:

Variable Environment (Memory/Hoisting phase)

Scope Chain

this binding

ğŸ”¹ 2. What is Call Stack?

The Call Stack is a stack data structure that keeps track of execution contexts.

When a function is invoked â†’ A new FEC is pushed onto the stack.

When the function finishes â†’ It is popped off the stack.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Example: Online Shopping Flow ğŸ›’
function browseProducts() {
  console.log("ğŸ›ï¸ Browsing products...");
}

function addToCart() {
  console.log("ğŸ›’ Adding product to cart...");
  payment(); // call another function
}

function payment() {
  console.log("ğŸ’³ Processing payment...");
}

function shopping() {
  browseProducts();
  addToCart();
}

shopping();

ğŸ”¹ Step-by-Step Execution (Call Stack)

Global Execution Context (GEC) created â†’ shopping() is defined.

shopping() is called â†’ Push shopping() FEC onto stack.

Inside shopping() â†’ Calls browseProducts() â†’ Push browseProducts() FEC.

browseProducts() executes â†’ Logs message â†’ Pop it off.

Back to shopping(), calls addToCart() â†’ Push addToCart() FEC.

Inside addToCart() â†’ Calls payment() â†’ Push payment() FEC.

payment() executes â†’ Pop off after completion.

addToCart() finishes â†’ Pop off.

shopping() finishes â†’ Pop off.

Only GEC remains.

âœ… Output:
ğŸ›ï¸ Browsing products...
ğŸ›’ Adding product to cart...
ğŸ’³ Processing payment...

ğŸ”¹ 4. Real-Life Analogy

Think of Call Stack like a restaurant kitchen order system ğŸ½ï¸:

Each Execution Context = one order.

Call Stack = pile of orders (stack of plates).

Chef (JS engine) handles last order first (LIFO).

When one dish is completed (function finishes), the plate (FEC) is removed.

ğŸ”¹ 5. Example with setTimeout (Async) â³

JavaScript is single-threaded, but async code is handled by Web APIs + Callback Queue (not on Call Stack directly).

console.log("ğŸ½ï¸ Order placed");

setTimeout(() => {
  console.log("ğŸ² Food served after 2 sec");
}, 2000);

console.log("ğŸ’¬ Chatting with friends");

âœ… Output:
ğŸ½ï¸ Order placed
ğŸ’¬ Chatting with friends
ğŸ² Food served after 2 sec


ğŸ’¡ Real-life: You order food (setTimeout), meanwhile you chat (other tasks). When food is ready (callback), waiter brings it after the delay.

âš¡ Summary
Concept	Meaning	Real-Life Example
Execution Context	Environment where code runs	Kitchen preparing a dish
Global Execution Context	Default when script runs	Restaurant setup
Function Execution Context	Created when function runs	Each new food order
Call Stack	Tracks order of execution	Stack of plates with orders
Pop/Push	Add/remove from stack	Chef starts/finishes a dish

===========================================================
Lexical Environment
===========================================================
1. What is Lexical Environment?

A Lexical Environment in JavaScript is the place where variables and functions are physically declared in the code.

ğŸ‘‰ In simple terms:

Lexical = related to â€œposition in codeâ€ (where written)

Every Execution Context has a Lexical Environment.

A Lexical Environment has two parts:

Environment Record â†’ Stores variables & function declarations.

Reference to outer environment â†’ For scope chain (if variable not found locally).

ğŸ”¹ 2. Simple Example
function outer() {
  let shopName = "SuperMart ğŸ¬";

  function inner() {
    console.log("Shopping at", shopName);
  }

  inner();
}

outer();


âœ… Output:

Shopping at SuperMart ğŸ¬


ğŸ‘‰ Here:

inner() has no variable shopName.

It looks into its Lexical Environment â†’ outer() â†’ found shopName.

This is possible because inner is defined inside outer (lexical position).

ğŸ”¹ 3. Real-Life Example: Bank ğŸ¦
function bank() {
  let bankName = "HDFC Bank ğŸ¦";

  function account(customer) {
    console.log(`${customer} has an account in ${bankName}`);
  }

  account("Suraj");
  account("Neha");
}

bank();


âœ… Output:

Suraj has an account in HDFC Bank ğŸ¦
Neha has an account in HDFC Bank ğŸ¦


ğŸ‘‰ Here:

Function account() doesnâ€™t have bankName.

It uses outer lexical environment (bank) to access it.

ğŸ”¹ 4. Nested Lexical Environments (Scope Chain)
let country = "India ğŸ‡®ğŸ‡³";

function state() {
  let stateName = "Maharashtra";

  function city() {
    let cityName = "Pune";
    console.log(`${cityName}, ${stateName}, ${country}`);
  }

  city();
}

state();


âœ… Output:

Pune, Maharashtra, India ğŸ‡®ğŸ‡³


ğŸ‘‰ Execution flow:

cityName â†’ found in city()

stateName â†’ not in city(), found in state() lexical environment

country â†’ not in state(), found in global lexical environment

Thatâ€™s the scope chain powered by lexical environments.

ğŸ”¹ 5. Real-Life Analogy ğŸ“–

Think of a Lexical Environment like a filing cabinet system:

If you canâ€™t find a file (variable) in your own drawer (local scope),

You check the next outer drawer (parent scope),

Keep going until you find it (global scope).

âš¡ Summary
Concept	Meaning	Real-Life Example
Lexical Environment	Where variable/function is declared (position in code)	Filing cabinet with drawers
Environment Record	Stores variables and function declarations	Documents in current drawer
Outer Reference	Link to parent lexical scope	Key to parent drawer
Scope Chain	Chain of lexical environments	Searching multiple drawers

===========================================================
Strict Mode ("use strict")
===========================================================
1. What is Strict Mode?

"use strict" was introduced in ECMAScript 5 (ES5).

It enforces stricter parsing and error handling in JavaScript.

Helps developers write cleaner, more secure, and less error-prone code.

ğŸ‘‰ To enable it:

"use strict";   // at top of file (applies globally)
// or inside a function (applies locally)

ğŸ”¹ 2. Why Use Strict Mode?

âœ… Prevents accidental errors
âœ… Avoids using undeclared variables
âœ… Makes debugging easier
âœ… Reserved keywords (like public, static) cannot be used as variable names

ğŸ”¹ 3. Examples
ğŸ“Œ Example 1: Using Undeclared Variables âŒ
// Without strict mode
name = "Suraj"; 
console.log(name); // Works, but creates global variable accidentally

// With strict mode
"use strict";
name = "Suraj";  // âŒ Error: name is not defined


ğŸ‘‰ Real-life analogy: Imagine accidentally writing customer data in the wrong file â€” strict mode forces you to declare properly before use.

ğŸ“Œ Example 2: Prevents Duplicate Parameters ğŸš«
// Without strict mode
function sum(a, a, b) {
  return a + b; // Works, but confusing
}
console.log(sum(1, 2, 3)); // 5

// With strict mode
"use strict";
function sum(a, a, b) {   // âŒ SyntaxError
  return a + b;
}


ğŸ‘‰ Real-life analogy: Like giving two students the same roll number â€” strict mode avoids confusion.

ğŸ“Œ Example 3: Prevents Writing to Read-Only Properties ğŸ”’
"use strict";
const obj = {};
Object.defineProperty(obj, "id", { value: 101, writable: false });

obj.id = 202;  // âŒ TypeError: Cannot assign to read only property


ğŸ‘‰ Real-life analogy: Think of trying to edit your Aadhaar number â€” system doesnâ€™t allow modification.

ğŸ“Œ Example 4: Disallows Reserved Keywords ğŸš«
"use strict";
let public = "data";  // âŒ SyntaxError
let static = 123;     // âŒ SyntaxError


ğŸ‘‰ Prevents future conflicts since these may be reserved in newer versions of JS.

ğŸ“Œ Example 5: In Functions (Local Strict Mode)
function normalFunc() {
  x = 10;  // Works (becomes global accidentally)
  console.log("Normal:", x);
}

function strictFunc() {
  "use strict";
  y = 20;  // âŒ Error: y is not defined
}

normalFunc();
strictFunc();


ğŸ‘‰ Real-life analogy: Think of strict exam hall rules (inside function) vs. casual class rules (normal function).

âš¡ Summary
Without Strict Mode	With Strict Mode
Allows undeclared variables	âŒ Error if undeclared
Duplicate function params	âŒ Not allowed
Silent failures (no error)	âŒ Throws error
Reserved keywords allowed	âŒ Not allowed
Global pollution risk	âœ… Safer code

=============================================================
5. Objects & Arrays
=============================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. Objects in JavaScript

ğŸ‘‰ An object is a collection of keyâ€“value pairs (like a dictionary).
It represents a real-world entity with properties (data) and methods (functions).

ğŸ“Œ Real-Life Example: Customer Profile ğŸ‘¤
let customer = {
  name: "Suraj Pawar",
  age: 28,
  email: "suraj@example.com",
  address: {
    city: "Pune",
    country: "India"
  },
  orders: ["Laptop", "Phone", "Headphones"],

  // method
  greet: function() {
    console.log(`Hello, ${this.name}! Welcome back to our store.`);
  }
};

console.log(customer.name);       // Suraj Pawar
console.log(customer.address.city); // Pune
console.log(customer.orders[1]);  // Phone
customer.greet();                 // Hello, Suraj! Welcome back to our store.


âœ… Output:

Suraj Pawar
Pune
Phone
Hello, Suraj! Welcome back to our store.


ğŸ‘‰ Real-life analogy: An object is like a customer profile in a shopâ€™s database.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Arrays in JavaScript

ğŸ‘‰ An array is an ordered collection of values (can store multiple items in one variable).

ğŸ“Œ Real-Life Example: Shopping Cart ğŸ›’
let cart = ["Shirt", "Jeans", "Shoes", "Watch"];

console.log("ğŸ›’ Items in cart:", cart);
console.log("First Item:", cart[0]);  // Shirt
console.log("Total Items:", cart.length); // 4

// Adding new item
cart.push("Cap");
console.log("Updated Cart:", cart);

// Removing last item
cart.pop();
console.log("After Removing:", cart);

// Iterating over cart
cart.forEach((item, index) => {
  console.log(`${index + 1}. ${item}`);
});


âœ… Output:

ğŸ›’ Items in cart: [ 'Shirt', 'Jeans', 'Shoes', 'Watch' ]
First Item: Shirt
Total Items: 4
Updated Cart: [ 'Shirt', 'Jeans', 'Shoes', 'Watch', 'Cap' ]
After Removing: [ 'Shirt', 'Jeans', 'Shoes', 'Watch' ]
1. Shirt
2. Jeans
3. Shoes
4. Watch


ğŸ‘‰ Real-life analogy: An array is like a shopping cart holding multiple products.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Objects + Arrays Together
ğŸ“Œ Real-Life Example: E-commerce Order System ğŸ›ï¸
let orders = [
  {
    orderId: 101,
    customer: "Suraj",
    items: ["Laptop", "Mouse"],
    total: 55000
  },
  {
    orderId: 102,
    customer: "Neha",
    items: ["Phone", "Earbuds"],
    total: 32000
  }
];

// Accessing data
console.log("First Order by:", orders[0].customer);  
console.log("Neha bought:", orders[1].items[0]);  

// Loop through orders
orders.forEach(order => {
  console.log(`Order ${order.orderId} - ${order.customer} - Total: â‚¹${order.total}`);
});


âœ… Output:

First Order by: Suraj
Neha bought: Phone
Order 101 - Suraj - Total: â‚¹55000
Order 102 - Neha - Total: â‚¹32000


ğŸ‘‰ Real-life analogy: An array of objects is like a list of all customer orders in a database.

âš¡ Summary
Concept	Real-Life Example
Object	Customer profile (name, age, email, address, orders)
Array	Shopping cart (list of items)
Array of Objects	List of all orders in an e-commerce app

âœ… Objects represent entities (with details).
âœ… Arrays represent collections (lists of items).
âœ… Together, they power real-world apps like e-commerce, banking, ticket booking.

=============================================================
Creating Objects ({} and new Object())
=============================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. Creating Object using Object Literal {}

ğŸ‘‰ The most common and simple way to create objects.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Employee Profile ğŸ¢
let employee = {
  id: 101,
  name: "Suraj Pawar",
  role: "Software Developer",
  skills: ["JavaScript", "React", "Node.js"],

  work: function() {
    console.log(`${this.name} is working as ${this.role}`);
  }
};

console.log(employee.name);   // Suraj Pawar
employee.work();              // Suraj Pawar is working as Software Developer


âœ… Output:

Suraj Pawar
Suraj Pawar is working as Software Developer


ğŸ‘‰ Real-life analogy: Think of this as quickly filling out an employee form with details.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Creating Object using new Object()

ğŸ‘‰ Another way is using the Object constructor.
You create an empty object and then add properties.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Bank Account ğŸ¦
let bankAccount = new Object();

bankAccount.accountNumber = "1234567890";
bankAccount.holderName = "Neha Sharma";
bankAccount.balance = 50000;
bankAccount.deposit = function(amount) {
  this.balance += amount;
  console.log(`Deposited â‚¹${amount}. New Balance: â‚¹${this.balance}`);
};

console.log(bankAccount.holderName);  // Neha Sharma
bankAccount.deposit(10000);


âœ… Output:

Neha Sharma
Deposited â‚¹10000. New Balance: â‚¹60000


ğŸ‘‰ Real-life analogy: Like opening a new blank account in a bank and then filling in details step by step.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Comparing {} vs new Object()
Feature	{}	new Object()
Syntax	Short & clean	Longer & explicit
Usage	Most commonly used	Rare, but useful when adding properties dynamically
Example	let obj = { name: "Suraj" }	let obj = new Object(); obj.name = "Suraj";
ğŸ”¹ 4. Real-Life Example: Online Store ğŸ›ï¸
Using Object Literal {}
let product = {
  id: 201,
  name: "Laptop",
  price: 55000,
  inStock: true
};

console.log(product);

Using new Object()
let order = new Object();
order.orderId = 501;
order.customer = "Suraj";
order.items = ["Laptop", "Mouse"];
order.total = 56000;

console.log(order);


âœ… Output:

{ id: 201, name: 'Laptop', price: 55000, inStock: true }
{ orderId: 501, customer: 'Suraj', items: [ 'Laptop', 'Mouse' ], total: 56000 }

âš¡ Summary

{} â†’ Best for quick object creation.

new Object() â†’ Useful when you want to create an empty object and add properties later.

Both represent real-world entities (employees, bank accounts, products, orders).
