Object Methods (Object.keys, Object.values, Object.entries)
=============================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. Object.keys(obj)

ğŸ‘‰ Returns an array of all property names (keys) in an object.

ğŸ“Œ Real-Life Example: Product Info ğŸ›ï¸
let product = {
  id: 101,
  name: "Laptop",
  brand: "Dell",
  price: 55000
};

let keys = Object.keys(product);
console.log("Product Keys:", keys);


âœ… Output:

Product Keys: [ 'id', 'name', 'brand', 'price' ]


ğŸ‘‰ Use Case: To list all available details about a product.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Object.values(obj)

ğŸ‘‰ Returns an array of all values in an object.

ğŸ“Œ Real-Life Example: Customer Profile ğŸ‘¤
let customer = {
  name: "Suraj Pawar",
  email: "suraj@example.com",
  city: "Pune"
};

let values = Object.values(customer);
console.log("Customer Values:", values);


âœ… Output:

Customer Values: [ 'Suraj Pawar', 'suraj@example.com', 'Pune' ]


ğŸ‘‰ Use Case: To export customer data for reports.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Object.entries(obj)

ğŸ‘‰ Returns an array of [key, value] pairs.

ğŸ“Œ Real-Life Example: Bank Account ğŸ¦
let bankAccount = {
  accountNumber: "1234567890",
  holder: "Neha Sharma",
  balance: 60000
};

let entries = Object.entries(bankAccount);
console.log("Bank Account Entries:", entries);

// Looping through entries
entries.forEach(([key, value]) => {
  console.log(`${key}: ${value}`);
});


âœ… Output:

Bank Account Entries: [
  [ 'accountNumber', '1234567890' ],
  [ 'holder', 'Neha Sharma' ],
  [ 'balance', 60000 ]
]
accountNumber: 1234567890
holder: Neha Sharma
balance: 60000


ğŸ‘‰ Use Case: Useful when you want to display key-value pairs (like account statements).

ğŸ”¹ 4. Combining All Methods
ğŸ“Œ Real-Life Example: Online Order System ğŸ›’
let order = {
  orderId: 501,
  customer: "Suraj",
  items: ["Laptop", "Mouse"],
  total: 56000
};

console.log("ğŸ”‘ Keys:", Object.keys(order));
console.log("ğŸ“¦ Values:", Object.values(order));
console.log("ğŸ“‹ Entries:", Object.entries(order));


âœ… Output:

ğŸ”‘ Keys: [ 'orderId', 'customer', 'items', 'total' ]
ğŸ“¦ Values: [ 501, 'Suraj', [ 'Laptop', 'Mouse' ], 56000 ]
ğŸ“‹ Entries: [
  [ 'orderId', 501 ],
  [ 'customer', 'Suraj' ],
  [ 'items', [ 'Laptop', 'Mouse' ] ],
  [ 'total', 56000 ]
]

âš¡ Summary
Method	Returns	Real-Life Use Case
Object.keys()	Array of property names	List fields in product database
Object.values()	Array of property values	Export customer details
Object.entries()	Array of [key, value] pairs	Display bank account / order summary

=============================================================
Object Destructuring
=============================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. What is Object Destructuring?

ğŸ‘‰ Object Destructuring is a shorthand way to unpack properties from an object into variables.

Instead of this:

let person = { name: "Suraj", city: "Pune" };
let name = person.name;
let city = person.city;


We can do this ğŸ‘‡

let { name, city } = person;
console.log(name, city); // Suraj Pune

ğŸ”¹ 2. Real-Life Examples
ğŸ“Œ Example 1: Customer Profile ğŸ‘¤
let customer = {
  id: 101,
  name: "Suraj Pawar",
  email: "suraj@example.com",
  city: "Pune"
};

// Destructuring
let { name, email, city } = customer;
console.log(`Customer: ${name}, Email: ${email}, City: ${city}`);


âœ… Output:

Customer: Suraj Pawar, Email: suraj@example.com, City: Pune


ğŸ‘‰ Use case: Quickly access customer details for displaying in UI.

ğŸ“Œ Example 2: E-Commerce Order ğŸ›’
let order = {
  orderId: 501,
  product: "Laptop",
  price: 55000,
  status: "Shipped"
};

// Destructuring with different variable names
let { orderId, product: item, price, status } = order;

console.log(`Order#${orderId}: ${item} - â‚¹${price} (${status})`);


âœ… Output:

Order#501: Laptop - â‚¹55000 (Shipped)


ğŸ‘‰ Use case: Useful when fetching order details from API response.

ğŸ“Œ Example 3: Nested Object Destructuring ğŸ¦
let bankAccount = {
  accountNumber: "1234567890",
  holder: {
    name: "Neha Sharma",
    city: "Mumbai"
  },
  balance: 75000
};

// Nested destructuring
let { holder: { name, city }, balance } = bankAccount;

console.log(`${name} from ${city} has â‚¹${balance} in account.`);


âœ… Output:

Neha Sharma from Mumbai has â‚¹75000 in account.


ğŸ‘‰ Use case: When accessing nested JSON data (like API response from banking systems).

ğŸ“Œ Example 4: Function Parameters (Clean API) ğŸŒ
function displayUser({ username, age, country }) {
  console.log(`${username} is ${age} years old from ${country}`);
}

let user = { username: "Amit", age: 30, country: "India" };
displayUser(user);


âœ… Output:

Amit is 30 years old from India


ğŸ‘‰ Use case: Clean function parameters instead of writing user.username, user.age, etc.

ğŸ“Œ Example 5: Default Values âš¡
let product = {
  name: "Mobile",
  price: 20000
};

// stock is missing â†’ default used
let { name, price, stock = 50 } = product;

console.log(`${name} costs â‚¹${price}. Available stock: ${stock}`);


âœ… Output:

Mobile costs â‚¹20000. Available stock: 50


ğŸ‘‰ Use case: Ensures API or DB missing fields still have safe defaults.

âš¡ Summary
Feature	Example	Real-Life Use
Basic Destructuring	{ name, city } = customer	Quick access to customer details
Rename	{ product: item } = order	Avoid variable conflicts
Nested	{ holder: { name, city } } = bankAccount	Extract from complex JSON
Function Params	function({ username, age })	Cleaner API function calls
Default Values	{ stock = 50 } = product	Handle missing data safely

âœ… Object Destructuring = clean, readable, and real-world ready code.

=============================================================
Array Methods (map, filter, reduce, forEach, find, some, every, sort)
=============================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. map() â†’ Transform Each Item

ğŸ‘‰ Returns a new array after applying a function.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Add tax to product prices

let products = [100, 200, 300];

// Add 18% GST to each price
let pricesWithGST = products.map(price => price + price * 0.18);

console.log(pricesWithGST); // [118, 236, 354]


âœ… Use case: Updating all product prices with tax/discount.

ğŸ”¹ 2. filter() â†’ Select Items Based on Condition

ğŸ‘‰ Returns a new array with items that pass the condition.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Get students who passed

let students = [
  { name: "Amit", marks: 45 },
  { name: "Neha", marks: 80 },
  { name: "Raj", marks: 30 }
];

let passed = students.filter(s => s.marks >= 40);
console.log(passed);
// [ { name: 'Amit', marks: 45 }, { name: 'Neha', marks: 80 } ]


âœ… Use case: Filtering students, products in stock, active users, etc.

ğŸ”¹ 3. reduce() â†’ Accumulate Values

ğŸ‘‰ Reduces an array into a single value.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Total bill in shopping cart

let cart = [
  { item: "Shoes", price: 2000 },
  { item: "Bag", price: 1500 },
  { item: "Watch", price: 3000 }
];

let total = cart.reduce((sum, product) => sum + product.price, 0);
console.log(`Total Bill: â‚¹${total}`); 
// Total Bill: â‚¹6500


âœ… Use case: Summing cart total, salaries, votes, etc.

ğŸ”¹ 4. forEach() â†’ Loop Over Each Item

ğŸ‘‰ Executes a function for each element (no new array).

ğŸ“Œ Example: Notify users via email

let users = ["suraj@example.com", "neha@example.com", "amit@example.com"];

users.forEach(email => {
  console.log(`ğŸ“§ Sending email to ${email}`);
});


âœ… Use case: Running side effects like sending notifications, logging.

ğŸ”¹ 5. find() â†’ Find First Match

ğŸ‘‰ Returns the first item that matches condition.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Find employee by ID

let employees = [
  { id: 101, name: "Suraj" },
  { id: 102, name: "Neha" },
  { id: 103, name: "Raj" }
];

let emp = employees.find(e => e.id === 102);
console.log(emp); // { id: 102, name: "Neha" }


âœ… Use case: Lookup in database results.

ğŸ”¹ 6. some() â†’ Check if at least one item matches

ğŸ‘‰ Returns true / false.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Check if any product is out of stock

let inventory = [
  { item: "Laptop", stock: 5 },
  { item: "Phone", stock: 0 },
  { item: "Tablet", stock: 3 }
];

let isOutOfStock = inventory.some(p => p.stock === 0);
console.log(isOutOfStock); // true


âœ… Use case: Validate if at least one condition is true.

ğŸ”¹ 7. every() â†’ Check if all items match

ğŸ‘‰ Returns true / false.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Check if all students passed

let marks = [55, 60, 70, 40];

let allPassed = marks.every(m => m >= 40);
console.log(allPassed); // true


âœ… Use case: Validation rules (all fields filled, all students passed).

ğŸ”¹ 8. sort() â†’ Sort Array

ğŸ‘‰ Sorts in place (changes original array).

ğŸ“Œ Example: Sort products by price

let items = [
  { name: "Shoes", price: 2000 },
  { name: "Watch", price: 1500 },
  { name: "Bag", price: 3000 }
];

items.sort((a, b) => a.price - b.price);

console.log(items);
// [ { name: 'Watch', price: 1500 }, { name: 'Shoes', price: 2000 }, { name: 'Bag', price: 3000 } ]


âœ… Use case: Sorting products, names, scores, etc.

âš¡ Summary Table
Method	Purpose	Real Use Case
map()	Transform	Add tax to prices
filter()	Select items	Find passed students
reduce()	Accumulate	Calculate shopping total
forEach()	Loop with side effects	Send emails
find()	Find first match	Get employee by ID
some()	At least one true	Any product out of stock?
every()	All must be true	Did all students pass?
sort()	Arrange items	Sort products by price

=============================================================
Array & Object Spread/Rest
=============================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. Spread Operator (...)

ğŸ‘‰ Used to expand elements of an array or properties of an object.

âœ… Example 1: Array Spread â†’ Shopping Cart ğŸ›’
let cart1 = ["Shoes", "Watch"];
let cart2 = ["Bag", "Laptop"];

// Combine carts
let finalCart = [...cart1, ...cart2, "Sunglasses"];

console.log(finalCart);
// ["Shoes", "Watch", "Bag", "Laptop", "Sunglasses"]


âœ” Real use case: Merging shopping carts.

âœ… Example 2: Object Spread â†’ Employee Update ğŸ¢
let employee = {
  id: 101,
  name: "Suraj",
  role: "Developer"
};

// Promote employee (spread + new property)
let updatedEmployee = {
  ...employee,
  role: "Team Lead",
  department: "IT"
};

console.log(updatedEmployee);
// { id: 101, name: "Suraj", role: "Team Lead", department: "IT" }


âœ” Real use case: Updating employee details without modifying original object.

âœ… Example 3: Copying Arrays â†’ Student Marks ğŸ“
let marks = [80, 70, 90];
let copiedMarks = [...marks]; // independent copy

copiedMarks.push(100);

console.log("Original:", marks);     // [80, 70, 90]
console.log("Copied:", copiedMarks); // [80, 70, 90, 100]


âœ” Real use case: Prevent modifying original student data.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Rest Operator (...)

ğŸ‘‰ Used to collect multiple elements into an array or object.

âœ… Example 4: Function Parameters â†’ Restaurant Order ğŸ½ï¸
function placeOrder(customer, ...items) {
  console.log(`Customer: ${customer}`);
  console.log(`Items Ordered: ${items.join(", ")}`);
}

placeOrder("Neha", "Pizza", "Burger", "Coke");
// Customer: Neha
// Items Ordered: Pizza, Burger, Coke


âœ” Real use case: Taking unlimited food orders.

âœ… Example 5: Object Rest â†’ User Data ğŸ§‘â€ğŸ’»
let user = {
  id: 1,
  name: "Raj",
  email: "raj@example.com",
  password: "secret123"
};

// Extract only required data
let { password, ...publicData } = user;

console.log(publicData);
// { id: 1, name: "Raj", email: "raj@example.com" }


âœ” Real use case: Hiding sensitive info (passwords) before showing user profile.

âœ… Example 6: Array Destructuring with Rest â†’ Students ğŸ«
let students = ["Amit", "Neha", "Raj", "Pooja"];

// First student is leader, rest are team members
let [leader, ...teamMembers] = students;

console.log("Leader:", leader);           // Amit
console.log("Team Members:", teamMembers); // ["Neha", "Raj", "Pooja"]


âœ” Real use case: Assigning leader and team members.

âš¡ Summary
Operator	Works in	Purpose	Real Use Case
Spread (...)	Array/Object	Expand values	Merge carts, update employee
Rest (...)	Function/Object/Array	Collect values	Order items, hide password, group team

=============================================================
JSON (JSON.stringify, JSON.parse)
=============================================================
ğŸ”¹ What is JSON?

JSON (JavaScript Object Notation) â†’ lightweight data format for storing & exchanging data.

Data is in key-value pairs (similar to JS objects).

Commonly used in APIs and databases.

ğŸ”¹ 1. JSON.stringify() â†’ Convert JS Object â†’ JSON String

ğŸ“Œ Example: Sending Employee Data to Server (API request)

let employee = {
  id: 101,
  name: "Suraj",
  role: "Developer",
  skills: ["JavaScript", "React", "Node.js"]
};

// Convert object to JSON string before sending to server
let jsonData = JSON.stringify(employee);

console.log(jsonData);


âœ… Output:

{"id":101,"name":"Suraj","role":"Developer","skills":["JavaScript","React","Node.js"]}


âœ” Real use case: Sending employee details to backend/API.

ğŸ”¹ 2. JSON.parse() â†’ Convert JSON String â†’ JS Object

ğŸ“Œ Example: Receiving Order Data from API (Server Response)

let orderData = '{"orderId":501,"customer":"Neha","items":["Laptop","Bag"],"total":56000}';

// Convert JSON string to JS object
let order = JSON.parse(orderData);

console.log(order.customer); // Neha
console.log(order.items[0]); // Laptop


âœ” Real use case: Parsing API response to access order details.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Using Both Together

ğŸ“Œ Example: Save & Retrieve User Data in Local Storage (Browser)

// User profile object
let user = {
  id: 1,
  name: "Raj",
  email: "raj@example.com",
  isLoggedIn: true
};

// Store in localStorage (needs string format)
localStorage.setItem("user", JSON.stringify(user));

// Retrieve from localStorage
let userData = JSON.parse(localStorage.getItem("user"));

console.log(userData.name);   // Raj
console.log(userData.isLoggedIn); // true


âœ” Real use case: Saving user session in browser.

ğŸ”¹ 4. Nested JSON Example

ğŸ“Œ Example: Student Report Card

let student = {
  id: 10,
  name: "Amit",
  subjects: {
    math: 90,
    science: 85,
    english: 88
  },
  passed: true
};

// Convert to JSON string
let jsonString = JSON.stringify(student);

console.log("JSON String:", jsonString);

// Parse back to object
let studentObj = JSON.parse(jsonString);

console.log("Math Marks:", studentObj.subjects.math); // 90


âœ” Real use case: Storing structured data (marks, addresses, etc.) in databases/APIs.

âš¡ Summary
Method	Purpose	Real Use Case
JSON.stringify(obj)	Object â†’ JSON String	Send data to server, save in localStorage
JSON.parse(str)	JSON String â†’ Object	Read API response, get saved data


=============================================================
6. DOM Manipulation
=============================================================
ğŸ”¹ What is DOM?

DOM (Document Object Model) = A tree structure that represents HTML elements in a web page.
ğŸ‘‰ Using JavaScript, we can select, create, update, delete, and handle events on DOM elements.

ğŸ”¹ 1. Selecting Elements

ğŸ“Œ Example: Selecting a Button

<button id="loginBtn">Login</button>

// Different ways to select elements
let btn1 = document.getElementById("loginBtn");
let btn2 = document.querySelector("#loginBtn"); // CSS selector
let btn3 = document.getElementsByTagName("button")[0];

console.log(btn1.textContent); // Login


âœ” Real use case: Selecting buttons, forms, headings to manipulate them.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Changing Content

ğŸ“Œ Example: Online Exam System

<h2 id="status">Exam not started</h2>
<button onclick="startExam()">Start Exam</button>

function startExam() {
  document.getElementById("status").textContent = "Exam is running...";
}


âœ” Real use case: Updating exam status dynamically.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Changing Styles

ğŸ“Œ Example: Highlight Low Stock Products

<p id="product">Laptop - Only 2 left!</p>

let product = document.getElementById("product");
product.style.color = "red";
product.style.fontWeight = "bold";


âœ” Real use case: Highlight warnings, errors, discounts.

ğŸ”¹ 4. Creating & Adding Elements

ğŸ“Œ Example: Adding New Chat Message

<ul id="chatBox"></ul>

function addMessage(user, message) {
  let li = document.createElement("li");
  li.textContent = `${user}: ${message}`;
  document.getElementById("chatBox").appendChild(li);
}

addMessage("Suraj", "Hello, how are you?");


âœ” Real use case: Adding chat messages, comments, notifications dynamically.

ğŸ”¹ 5. Removing Elements

ğŸ“Œ Example: Remove Item from Cart

<ul id="cart">
  <li id="item1">Shoes <button onclick="removeItem('item1')">Remove</button></li>
</ul>

function removeItem(itemId) {
  let item = document.getElementById(itemId);
  item.remove(); // or item.parentNode.removeChild(item);
}


âœ” Real use case: Deleting items from shopping cart.

ğŸ”¹ 6. Handling Events

ğŸ“Œ Example: Login Button Click

<button id="loginBtn">Login</button>

let loginBtn = document.getElementById("loginBtn");

loginBtn.addEventListener("click", function() {
  alert("You clicked Login!");
});


âœ” Real use case: Handling clicks, form submissions, mouseover, keypress events.

ğŸ”¹ 7. Changing Attributes

ğŸ“Œ Example: Change Profile Picture

<img id="profilePic" src="default.jpg" width="150">

document.getElementById("profilePic").setAttribute("src", "user1.jpg");


âœ” Real use case: Updating image source, links, input values.

ğŸ”¹ 8. Form Handling

ğŸ“Œ Example: Get User Input

<input type="text" id="username" placeholder="Enter name">
<button onclick="showUser()">Submit</button>
<p id="output"></p>

function showUser() {
  let name = document.getElementById("username").value;
  document.getElementById("output").textContent = `Welcome, ${name}!`;
}


âœ” Real use case: Taking user input from forms.

ğŸ”¹ 2. document.querySelector()

ğŸ”¹ 3. document.querySelectorAll()

âš¡ Quick Comparison

Event Handling (addEventListener, event bubbling, delegation)
=====================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. addEventListener() â†’ Attach Multiple Event Listeners

ğŸ“Œ Example: Button Click & Mouseover

  btn.style.background = "lightgreen";
});
</script>


âœ… Real use case: Attach multiple events (click, hover, focus) to a button without overwriting.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Event Bubbling â†’ Event flows from child â†’ parent

ğŸ“Œ Example: Clicking a Child Triggers Parent Too

<div id="parent" style="padding:20px; background:#f1f1f1;">
  Parent Div
  <button id="child">Click Me</button>
</div>

  alert("Parent Div Clicked!");
});

  alert("Child Button Clicked!");
});
</script>


ğŸ” If you click Child Button:

First â†’ "Child Button Clicked!"

Then â†’ "Parent Div Clicked!" (because of bubbling)

âœ… Real use case: Detect clicks both on items and their containers (like clicking product + product card).

ğŸ”¹ 3. Event Delegation â†’ Handle Events on Parent Instead of Each Child

ğŸ“Œ Example: Shopping Cart (Multiple Buttons)

<ul id="productList">
  <li><button class="addToCart">Add Apple</button></li>
  <li><button class="addToCart">Add Mango</button></li>
  <li><button class="addToCart">Add Orange</button></li>
</ul>

<script>
document.getElementById("productList").addEventListener("click", function(event) {
  if (event.target.classList.contains("addToCart")) {
    alert(event.target.innerText + " clicked!");
  }
});
</script>


âœ… Real use case: Instead of attaching event listeners to each product button, attach one listener to the parent <ul> â†’ saves performance in large lists.

âš¡ Summary
Concept	Meaning	Real Use Case
addEventListener()	Attach events (multiple allowed)	Button click, hover
Event Bubbling	Events propagate child â†’ parent	Modal close when clicking outside
Event Delegation	Use parent to handle child events	Shopping cart, menus, chat apps

=====================================================================
7. Advanced JavaScript (Done)
=====================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. Closures

ğŸ“Œ A function â€œremembersâ€ variables from its outer scope, even after the outer function has finished.

Example: Banking App â€“ Balance Privacy

function createAccount(initialBalance) {
  let balance = initialBalance; // private variable

  return {
    deposit: function(amount) {
      balance += amount;
      return `Deposited â‚¹${amount}, New Balance: â‚¹${balance}`;
    },
    withdraw: function(amount) {
      if (amount <= balance) {
        balance -= amount;
        return `Withdrawn â‚¹${amount}, Remaining Balance: â‚¹${balance}`;
      } else {
        return "Insufficient funds!";
      }
    }
  };
}

const myAccount = createAccount(500);
console.log(myAccount.deposit(200)); // Deposited â‚¹200, New Balance: â‚¹700
console.log(myAccount.withdraw(1000)); // Insufficient funds!


âœ… Real use case â†’ Keeping data private in apps (like bank balance).

ğŸ”¹ 2. Promises & Async/Await

ğŸ“Œ Handle asynchronous operations (e.g., API calls, DB queries).

Example: Fetch Weather Data

// Using Promise
function fetchWeather(city) {
  return new Promise((resolve, reject) => {
    setTimeout(() => {
      if (city === "Pune") {
        resolve("â˜€ï¸ Sunny in Pune");
      } else {
        reject("City not found!");
      }
    }, 1000);
  });
}

// Using async/await
async function showWeather() {
  try {
    let result = await fetchWeather("Pune");
    console.log(result); 
  } catch (error) {
    console.log(error);
  }
}

showWeather();


âœ… Real use case â†’ APIs in weather apps, payment processing.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Event Loop & Concurrency

ğŸ“Œ JS executes code line by line (single-threaded), but async tasks go to event loop queue.

Example: Order Food App

console.log("1. Place order ğŸ”");

setTimeout(() => console.log("2. Cooking food â³"), 2000);

console.log("3. Continue scrolling Instagram ğŸ“±");


Output order:

1. Place order ğŸ”
3. Continue scrolling Instagram ğŸ“±
2. Cooking food â³


âœ… Real use case â†’ Non-blocking apps (chat apps, food ordering).

ğŸ”¹ 4. Prototypes & Inheritance

ğŸ“Œ Objects inherit properties/methods via prototype chain.

Example: Vehicle Inheritance

function Vehicle(type) {
  this.type = type;
}
Vehicle.prototype.drive = function() {
  return `${this.type} is moving ğŸš—`;
};

function Car(model) {
  Vehicle.call(this, "Car");
  this.model = model;
}
Car.prototype = Object.create(Vehicle.prototype);

const myCar = new Car("Tesla");
console.log(myCar.drive()); // Car is moving ğŸš—


âœ… Real use case â†’ Reuse code in OOP design.

ğŸ”¹ 5. Modules (import/export)

ğŸ“Œ Split code into files for better maintainability.

Example: E-commerce Cart

ğŸ“ cart.js

export function addToCart(item) {
  return `${item} added to cart ğŸ›’`;
}


ğŸ“ app.js

import { addToCart } from './cart.js';

console.log(addToCart("Laptop")); // Laptop added to cart ğŸ›’


âœ… Real use case â†’ Modular code in projects (React, Node.js).

ğŸ”¹ 6. Currying

ğŸ“Œ Break a function with multiple arguments into smaller functions.

Example: Online Order Discount

function applyDiscount(discount) {
  return function(price) {
    return price - (price * discount);
  };
}

let studentDiscount = applyDiscount(0.2);
console.log(studentDiscount(1000)); // 800


âœ… Real use case â†’ Reusable functions (discounts, tax calculation).

ğŸ”¹ 7. Debouncing & Throttling

ğŸ“Œ Improve performance by controlling how often a function runs.

Example: Search Suggestions (Debouncing)

let timer;
function search(query) {
  clearTimeout(timer);
  timer = setTimeout(() => {
    console.log("Searching for:", query);
  }, 500);
}

// Simulating typing
search("a");
search("ap");
search("app");


âœ… Real use case â†’ Google search suggestions, scroll events.

ğŸ”¹ 8. LocalStorage & SessionStorage

ğŸ“Œ Store data in the browser.

Example: Remember User Login

// Save login
localStorage.setItem("username", "Suraj");

// Retrieve
console.log(localStorage.getItem("username")); // Suraj

// Remove
localStorage.removeItem("username");


âœ… Real use case â†’ Store theme, login state, cart items.

âš¡ Summary (Advanced JS Topics)

âœ… Closures â†’ Private variables (Bank account)
âœ… Promises/Async â†’ Handle async (Weather API)
âœ… Event Loop â†’ Non-blocking tasks (Food app)
âœ… Prototypes â†’ Inheritance (Car from Vehicle)
âœ… Modules â†’ Split files (Cart system)
âœ… Currying â†’ Reuse functions (Discounts)
âœ… Debounce/Throttle â†’ Performance (Search, scroll)
âœ… Storage â†’ Save data (Login, theme, cart)

ES6+ Features (let/const, template literals, destructuring, modules, etc.)

ğŸ”¹ 1. let & const

ğŸ“Œ Block-scoped, unlike var.

  let newItem = "Laptop";

}

// âŒ newItem is not accessible here (block scope)


âœ… Real use case â†’ Prevent accidental variable overwrites.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Template Literals (Backticks ``)

ğŸ“Œ Easier string concatenation + multi-line strings.

Your total bill is â‚¹${amount}.

âœ… Real use case â†’ Create formatted messages, emails, invoices.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Object & Array Destructuring

ğŸ“Œ Extract values easily.

ğŸ”¹ 4. Default Parameters

ğŸ”¹ 5. Arrow Functions () => {}

ğŸ”¹ 6. Spread Operator ...

ğŸ”¹ 7. Rest Operator ...

ğŸ“Œ Collect remaining arguments.

// Others: Suraj, Sneha, Priya


âœ… Real use case â†’ Flexible functions (multiple inputs).

ğŸ”¹ 8. Modules (import/export)

ğŸ”¹ 9. Classes

=====================================================================
Spread & Rest Operator
=====================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. Spread Operator (...)
âœ… Example 1: Merging Shopping Carts
let cart1 = ["Laptop", "Phone"];
let cart2 = ["Headphones", "Charger"];

let mergedCart = [...cart1, ...cart2];
console.log(mergedCart);
// ["Laptop", "Phone", "Headphones", "Charger"]


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Merging multiple shopping carts in an e-commerce website.

âœ… Example 2: Copying an Object (User Profile)
let user = { name: "Suraj", age: 25 };
let updatedUser = { ...user, city: "Pune" };

console.log(updatedUser);
// { name: "Suraj", age: 25, city: "Pune" }


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Cloning objects and adding extra details (like when updating user profiles).

âœ… Example 3: Passing Array Values to Function
function calculateBill(a, b, c) {
  return a + b + c;
}

let prices = [100, 200, 300];
console.log(calculateBill(...prices)); // 600


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Expanding price arrays when calculating total bills.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Rest Operator (...)
âœ… Example 1: Restaurant Bill Split
function splitBill(mainPayer, ...others) {
  console.log(`Main payer: ${mainPayer}`);
  console.log(`Others sharing: ${others.join(", ")}`);
}

splitBill("Amit", "Riya", "Sneha", "Suraj");
// Main payer: Amit
// Others sharing: Riya, Sneha, Suraj


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Handling variable number of arguments (bill splitting, group payments).

âœ… Example 2: Extracting Remaining Properties
let user = { name: "Riya", age: 24, city: "Mumbai", country: "India" };

let { name, ...details } = user;
console.log(name);     // Riya
console.log(details);  // { age: 24, city: "Mumbai", country: "India" }


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Get required property (name) and keep the rest (user details).

âœ… Example 3: Dynamic Sum of Numbers
function totalAmount(...amounts) {
  return amounts.reduce((sum, amt) => sum + amt, 0);
}

console.log(totalAmount(200, 500, 300, 100)); // 1100


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Summing variable number of payments in a payment app.

âš¡ Key Difference
Operator	Meaning	Example Use Case
Spread	Expands array/object	Merge shopping carts, clone user profile
Rest	Collects arguments into array	Split bills, dynamic functions, extract details

=====================================================================
Default & Named Exports / Imports
=====================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. Named Exports / Imports

ğŸ“Œ You can export multiple functions/variables from a file.
ğŸ“Œ Import them using curly braces {}.

âœ… Example: mathUtils.js
// Named Exports
export function add(a, b) {
  return a + b;
}

export function subtract(a, b) {
  return a - b;
}

export const TAX_RATE = 0.18;

âœ… Example: app.js
// Named Imports
import { add, subtract, TAX_RATE } from './mathUtils.js';

console.log(add(10, 5));        // 15
console.log(subtract(10, 5));   // 5
console.log("Tax Rate:", TAX_RATE); // 0.18


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case â†’ Utility functions for billing, tax calculation, discounts.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Default Exports / Imports

ğŸ“Œ Each file can have only one default export.
ğŸ“Œ Import without curly braces.

âœ… Example: payment.js
// Default Export
export default function processPayment(amount) {
  return `Payment of â‚¹${amount} processed successfully âœ…`;
}

âœ… Example: app.js
// Default Import
import processPayment from './payment.js';

console.log(processPayment(1500));
// Payment of â‚¹1500 processed successfully âœ…


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case â†’ One main function/class per module (like a payment processor).

ğŸ”¹ 3. Mixing Default & Named Exports
âœ… Example: cart.js
export default function addToCart(item) {
  return `${item} added to cart ğŸ›’`;
}

export function removeFromCart(item) {
  return `${item} removed from cart âŒ`;
}

export const SHIPPING_CHARGE = 50;

âœ… Example: app.js
// Import default + named exports
import addToCart, { removeFromCart, SHIPPING_CHARGE } from './cart.js';

console.log(addToCart("Laptop"));      
// Laptop added to cart ğŸ›’

console.log(removeFromCart("Laptop")); 
// Laptop removed from cart âŒ

console.log("Shipping:", SHIPPING_CHARGE); 
// Shipping: 50


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case â†’

default â†’ Primary feature (addToCart).

named â†’ Extra utilities (removeFromCart, SHIPPING_CHARGE).

âš¡ Summary
Export Type	Import Syntax	Use Case
Named Export	import { fn } from	Multiple utilities from one file
Default Export	import fn from	One main feature per file
Mixing Both	import fn, { utils }	Main + extra features

=====================================================================
Promises (then, catch, finally)
=====================================================================
ğŸ”¹ What is a Promise?

A Promise in JavaScript is an object that represents the eventual completion (resolved) or failure (rejected) of an asynchronous operation.

It has 3 states:

Pending â€“ still working.

Fulfilled (Resolved) â€“ success.

Rejected â€“ failure.

ğŸ”¹ Syntax
new Promise((resolve, reject) => {
  // async work
  if (success) resolve(value);
  else reject(error);
})

ğŸ”¹ Real-World Examples
âœ… Example 1: Online Food Order
function orderFood(item) {
  return new Promise((resolve, reject) => {
    console.log(`Ordering ${item}... ğŸ”`);
    setTimeout(() => {
      if (item === "Pizza") {
        resolve("Pizza delivered! âœ…");
      } else {
        reject("Sorry, item not available âŒ");
      }
    }, 2000);
  });
}

orderFood("Pizza")
  .then(response => {
    console.log("Success:", response);
  })
  .catch(error => {
    console.log("Error:", error);
  })
  .finally(() => {
    console.log("Thank you for ordering with us ğŸ™");
  });

ğŸ” Output if item = "Pizza":
Ordering Pizza... ğŸ”
Success: Pizza delivered! âœ…
Thank you for ordering with us ğŸ™

ğŸ” Output if item = "Burger":
Ordering Burger... ğŸ”
Error: Sorry, item not available âŒ
Thank you for ordering with us ğŸ™


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case â†’ Food delivery apps (Zomato, Swiggy).

âœ… Example 2: Payment Gateway
function processPayment(amount) {
  return new Promise((resolve, reject) => {
    console.log("Processing payment of â‚¹" + amount);
    setTimeout(() => {
      let success = Math.random() > 0.3; // 70% chance success
      success ? resolve("Payment Successful âœ…") : reject("Payment Failed âŒ");
    }, 1500);
  });
}

processPayment(1000)
  .then(msg => console.log(msg))
  .catch(err => console.log(err))
  .finally(() => console.log("Transaction Complete ğŸ¦"));


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case â†’ Handling payments in online shopping apps.

âœ… Example 3: Fetch User Data (API Simulation)
function fetchUser(id) {
  return new Promise((resolve, reject) => {
    console.log("Fetching user data...");
    setTimeout(() => {
      if (id === 1) {
        resolve({ id: 1, name: "Suraj", role: "Admin" });
      } else {
        reject("User not found!");
      }
    }, 1000);
  });
}

fetchUser(1)
  .then(user => console.log("User Data:", user))
  .catch(err => console.log("Error:", err))
  .finally(() => console.log("API call finished âœ…"));


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case â†’ Fetching user details from server.

âš¡ Summary
Method	Purpose	Example Use Case
then()	Runs when Promise is resolved (success)	Show order delivered message
catch()	Runs when Promise is rejected (error)	Show error in payment
finally()	Runs always (success or fail)	Close loaders, thank message

=====================================================================
Async/Await
=====================================================================
ğŸ”¹ What is async/await?

async/await is a cleaner and more readable way to work with Promises.

Instead of chaining .then() and .catch(), you can write asynchronous code like synchronous code.

ğŸ”¹ Rules

async keyword â†’ makes a function return a Promise.

await keyword â†’ pauses the function until the Promise is resolved or rejected.

Use try...catch for error handling.

ğŸ”¹ Real-World Examples
âœ… Example 1: Online Food Order (Async/Await)
function orderFood(item) {
  return new Promise((resolve, reject) => {
    console.log(`Ordering ${item}... ğŸ”`);
    setTimeout(() => {
      if (item === "Pizza") {
        resolve("Pizza delivered! âœ…");
      } else {
        reject("Sorry, item not available âŒ");
      }
    }, 2000);
  });
}

async function placeOrder() {
  try {
    let result = await orderFood("Pizza");
    console.log("Success:", result);
  } catch (error) {
    console.log("Error:", error);
  } finally {
    console.log("Thank you for ordering with us ğŸ™");
  }
}

placeOrder();


ğŸ“Œ Cleaner than .then().catch().finally()

âœ… Example 2: Payment Gateway (Async/Await)
function processPayment(amount) {
  return new Promise((resolve, reject) => {
    console.log("Processing payment of â‚¹" + amount);
    setTimeout(() => {
      let success = Math.random() > 0.3; // 70% chance success
      success ? resolve("Payment Successful âœ…") : reject("Payment Failed âŒ");
    }, 1500);
  });
}

async function makePayment() {
  try {
    let status = await processPayment(1000);
    console.log(status);
  } catch (err) {
    console.log(err);
  } finally {
    console.log("Transaction Complete ğŸ¦");
  }
}

makePayment();


ğŸ“Œ Looks like synchronous flow but still async.

âœ… Example 3: Fetch User Data (API Simulation)
function fetchUser(id) {
  return new Promise((resolve, reject) => {
    console.log("Fetching user data...");
    setTimeout(() => {
      if (id === 1) {
        resolve({ id: 1, name: "Suraj", role: "Admin" });
      } else {
        reject("User not found!");
      }
    }, 1000);
  });
}

async function getUser() {
  try {
    let user = await fetchUser(1);
    console.log("User Data:", user);
  } catch (err) {
    console.log("Error:", err);
  } finally {
    console.log("API call finished âœ…");
  }
}

getUser();


ğŸ“Œ Perfect for working with APIs (like fetch() in real apps).

âš¡ Comparison: then/catch vs async/await
Feature	then/catch	async/await
Readability	More callbacks (nested)	Cleaner, looks synchronous
Error Handling	catch()	try...catch
Usage	Useful for chaining	Useful for step-by-step async tasks
Real World	API calls, payment, orders	Same, but easier to manage

ğŸ”¹ 1. What is AJAX?

AJAX (Asynchronous JavaScript and XML) is a technique to send & receive data asynchronously from a server without reloading the page.

Old way â†’ XMLHttpRequest (XHR).

Modern way â†’ fetch() API (simpler, Promise-based).

ğŸ”¹ 2. Fetch API Basics
fetch("https://api.example.com/data")
  .then(response => response.json()) // Convert to JSON
  .then(data => console.log(data))   // Use the data
  .catch(error => console.error("Error:", error));

=====================================================================
Event Loop & Concurrency Model
=====================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. What is the Event Loop?

JavaScript is single-threaded, meaning it executes one task at a time.

But JS can handle asynchronous tasks (like API calls, timers) using the Event Loop.

The Event Loop checks the call stack and message queue to execute tasks in the correct order.

Key Components:

Call Stack â†’ Where functions are executed (synchronous code).

Web APIs â†’ Browser APIs (like setTimeout, fetch) handle async tasks.

Callback Queue / Task Queue â†’ Holds async callbacks.

Event Loop â†’ Moves tasks from queue to stack when the stack is empty.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Real-Based Examples
âœ… Example 1: setTimeout & Synchronous Code
console.log("1. Start Cooking ğŸ³");

setTimeout(() => {
  console.log("2. Food is Ready ğŸ²");
}, 2000);

console.log("3. Scroll Instagram ğŸ“±");

ğŸ” Output:
1. Start Cooking ğŸ³
3. Scroll Instagram ğŸ“±
2. Food is Ready ğŸ²


Explanation:

Synchronous code (console.log) runs first â†’ goes to call stack.

setTimeout callback goes to Web APIs, then to callback queue after 2 sec.

Event Loop executes it after stack is empty.

ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Loading spinners while fetching data asynchronously.

âœ… Example 2: API Call Simulation (Promise)
console.log("1. Place Order ğŸ”");

fetch("https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/todos/1")
  .then(response => response.json())
  .then(data => console.log("2. Order Delivered:", data));

console.log("3. Check Notifications ğŸ””");

ğŸ” Output:
1. Place Order ğŸ”
3. Check Notifications ğŸ””
2. Order Delivered: {userId: 1, id: 1, title: "delectus aut ...", completed: false}


Explanation:

fetch() is asynchronous â†’ handled by Web API.

.then() callback waits in the microtask queue â†’ executed after stack is empty.

ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Async API calls in web apps (e-commerce, social media).

âœ… Example 3: setTimeout vs Promise (Microtasks vs Macrotasks)
console.log("Start ğŸ”¥");

setTimeout(() => console.log("Timeout â°"), 0);

Promise.resolve().then(() => console.log("Promise âœ…"));

console.log("End ğŸ");

ğŸ” Output:
Start ğŸ”¥
End ğŸ
Promise âœ…
Timeout â°


Explanation:

Promises go to microtask queue â†’ executed before setTimeout (macrotask).

Even if setTimeout = 0ms, it runs after microtasks.

ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Handling UI updates before delayed tasks.

âœ… Example 4: Real-Life Analogy (Food Delivery App)

User places order â†’ goes call stack.

Order sent to kitchen â†’ Web API (async).

User scrolls app â†’ synchronous code executes.

Kitchen prepares food â†’ callback in queue.

Event Loop moves callback â†’ console shows â€œFood Readyâ€.

âš¡ Summary
Concept	Description	Real Use Case
Call Stack	Executes synchronous code	Button click handler
Web APIs	Handles async tasks	setTimeout, fetch
Callback Queue	Holds async callbacks	API responses
Event Loop	Moves tasks from queue â†’ stack	Non-blocking UI updates
Microtask vs Macrotask	Promise vs setTimeout	UI before delayed tasks

âœ… Key Takeaway: JS never blocks the UI while waiting for async tasks.

=====================================================================
JavaScript Engine (V8 basics)
=====================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. What is a JavaScript Engine?

A JavaScript Engine is a program that reads, parses, and executes JavaScript code.

Converts JS code into machine code that your computer can understand.

Examples:

V8 â†’ Chrome, Node.js

SpiderMonkey â†’ Firefox

JavaScriptCore â†’ Safari

ğŸ”¹ 2. How V8 Works (Basics)

V8 engine has several components:

Parser â†’ Reads your JS code and creates an Abstract Syntax Tree (AST).

Interpreter (Ignition) â†’ Converts AST into bytecode (fast initial execution).

Compiler (TurboFan) â†’ Optimizes bytecode to machine code for better performance.

Garbage Collector â†’ Frees memory used by objects no longer needed.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Real-Based Examples
âœ… Example 1: Synchronous vs Asynchronous Execution
console.log("1. Start Task");

setTimeout(() => {
  console.log("2. Async Task Finished");
}, 1000);

console.log("3. Continue Task");


Output:

1. Start Task
3. Continue Task
2. Async Task Finished


Explanation (V8 internals):

console.log â†’ executed by V8â€™s interpreter.

setTimeout â†’ handled by Web API, callback queued.

Event Loop triggers callback â†’ V8 executes it later.

ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Non-blocking UI updates (like scrolling while fetching data).

âœ… Example 2: Just-In-Time (JIT) Compilation
function sumArray(arr) {
  return arr.reduce((acc, val) => acc + val, 0);
}

let nums = [1, 2, 3, 4, 5];
console.log(sumArray(nums));


Explanation (V8 internals):

V8 interprets the function initially.

If sumArray runs multiple times â†’ TurboFan compiler optimizes it into machine code for faster execution.

ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Performance-critical functions in apps (calculating totals in e-commerce).

âœ… Example 3: Garbage Collection
function createUser(name) {
  let user = { name };
  return user;
}

let user1 = createUser("Suraj");
user1 = null; // previous object eligible for garbage collection


Explanation (V8 internals):

The object { name: "Suraj" } is now unreachable.

V8â€™s garbage collector will free memory automatically.

ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Prevent memory leaks in long-running web apps (like dashboards).

âœ… Example 4: Inline Caching (Optimization)
function getUserName(user) {
  return user.name;
}

let u1 = { name: "Amit" };
let u2 = { name: "Sneha" };

console.log(getUserName(u1));
console.log(getUserName(u2));


Explanation:

V8 stores hidden classes for objects.

Accessing user.name repeatedly â†’ faster due to inline caching.

ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Optimized property access in large datasets.

âš¡ Summary (V8 Real-World Concepts)
Feature	What it Does	Example Use Case
Parser	Creates AST	Reading code
Interpreter	Converts JS â†’ bytecode	console.log
JIT Compiler (TurboFan)	Optimizes to machine code	sumArray repeatedly
Event Loop Execution	Async tasks	setTimeout, fetch
Garbage Collector	Frees unused memory	Nullifying objects
Hidden Classes / Inline Caching	Optimized property access	User objects, e-commerce data

âœ… Key Takeaways:

V8 makes JS fast via interpretation + JIT compilation.

Handles async tasks efficiently.

Memory management and optimizations happen automatically.

=====================================================================
8. Object-Oriented Programming (OOP) 
=====================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. What is OOP in JavaScript?

OOP is a programming paradigm that organizes code using objects and classes.
Core Concepts:

Class â†’ Blueprint for creating objects.

Object â†’ Instance of a class.

Constructor â†’ Special method to initialize object properties.

Methods â†’ Functions inside objects/classes.

Inheritance â†’ Child class can inherit properties/methods of parent class.

Encapsulation â†’ Keep data safe inside the object.

Polymorphism â†’ Same method behaves differently based on context.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Real-Based Examples
âœ… Example 1: Classes & Objects (Online Shopping)
class Product {
  constructor(name, price) {
    this.name = name;
    this.price = price;
  }

  getDetails() {
    return `${this.name} costs â‚¹${this.price}`;
  }
}

// Creating objects
let laptop = new Product("Laptop", 45000);
let phone = new Product("Phone", 15000);

console.log(laptop.getDetails()); // Laptop costs â‚¹45000
console.log(phone.getDetails());  // Phone costs â‚¹15000


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: E-commerce apps â†’ Represent products with properties & methods.

âœ… Example 2: Inheritance (Customer & Premium Customer)
class Customer {
  constructor(name) {
    this.name = name;
  }
  greet() {
    return `Hello, ${this.name}!`;
  }
}

// PremiumCustomer inherits Customer
class PremiumCustomer extends Customer {
  constructor(name, discount) {
    super(name); // call parent constructor
    this.discount = discount;
  }
  greet() {
    return `${super.greet()} You have ${this.discount}% discount!`;
  }
}

let customer1 = new Customer("Suraj");
let customer2 = new PremiumCustomer("Riya", 20);

console.log(customer1.greet()); // Hello, Suraj!
console.log(customer2.greet()); // Hello, Riya! You have 20% discount!


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Different types of users in an e-commerce platform.

âœ… Example 3: Encapsulation (Private Properties)
class BankAccount {
  #balance; // private property

  constructor(owner, balance) {
    this.owner = owner;
    this.#balance = balance;
  }

  deposit(amount) {
    this.#balance += amount;
    console.log(`â‚¹${amount} deposited. Current balance: â‚¹${this.#balance}`);
  }

  getBalance() {
    return this.#balance;
  }
}

let account = new BankAccount("Suraj", 5000);
account.deposit(2000); // â‚¹2000 deposited. Current balance: â‚¹7000
console.log(account.getBalance()); // 7000
// console.log(account.#balance); // âŒ Error: Private property


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Secure banking apps â†’ hide sensitive data like balance.

âœ… Example 4: Polymorphism (Multiple Behavior)
class Notification {
  send(message) {
    console.log(`Notification: ${message}`);
  }
}

class EmailNotification extends Notification {
  send(message) {
    console.log(`Email sent: ${message}`);
  }
}

class SMSNotification extends Notification {
  send(message) {
    console.log(`SMS sent: ${message}`);
  }
}

let notifications = [
  new Notification(),
  new EmailNotification(),
  new SMSNotification()
];

notifications.forEach(n => n.send("Your order is confirmed!"));


Output:

Notification: Your order is confirmed!
Email sent: Your order is confirmed!
SMS sent: Your order is confirmed!


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Send notifications through multiple channels (Email, SMS, App).

âš¡ Summary
OOP Concept	JS Feature	Real Use Case
Class & Object	class, new	Products, Orders
Inheritance	extends, super	Regular vs Premium Customer
Encapsulation	#private	Bank account balance
Polymorphism	Method overriding	Notifications via Email/SMS
Methods	Functions inside class	Order details, greetings

âœ… Key Takeaways:

OOP in JS helps organize complex apps.

Classes & inheritance make code reusable & maintainable.

Private properties & encapsulation secure sensitive data.

=====================================================================
Constructor Functions
=====================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. What is a Constructor Function?

A constructor function is a special type of function used to create multiple objects with the same structure.

By convention, its name starts with a capital letter.

You use the new keyword to create objects from it.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Syntax
function Person(name, age) {
  this.name = name;
  this.age = age;
  this.greet = function() {
    return `Hello, my name is ${this.name} and I am ${this.age} years old.`;
  };
}

let person1 = new Person("Suraj", 25);
let person2 = new Person("Riya", 24);

console.log(person1.greet());
console.log(person2.greet());


Output:

Hello, my name is Suraj and I am 25 years old.
Hello, my name is Riya and I am 24 years old.


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Creating multiple user objects in a web app.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Example 1: Online Shopping Products
function Product(name, price, category) {
  this.name = name;
  this.price = price;
  this.category = category;
  this.getDetails = function() {
    return `${this.name} (${this.category}) costs â‚¹${this.price}`;
  };
}

let laptop = new Product("Laptop", 45000, "Electronics");
let phone = new Product("Phone", 15000, "Electronics");

console.log(laptop.getDetails()); // Laptop (Electronics) costs â‚¹45000
console.log(phone.getDetails());  // Phone (Electronics) costs â‚¹15000


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: E-commerce websites creating multiple product objects dynamically.

ğŸ”¹ 4. Example 2: Bank Accounts
function BankAccount(owner, balance) {
  this.owner = owner;
  this.balance = balance;
  
  this.deposit = function(amount) {
    this.balance += amount;
    return `â‚¹${amount} deposited. Current balance: â‚¹${this.balance}`;
  };

  this.withdraw = function(amount) {
    if(amount > this.balance) return "Insufficient balance!";
    this.balance -= amount;
    return `â‚¹${amount} withdrawn. Remaining balance: â‚¹${this.balance}`;
  };
}

let account1 = new BankAccount("Suraj", 5000);
console.log(account1.deposit(2000));   // â‚¹2000 deposited. Current balance: â‚¹7000
console.log(account1.withdraw(1000));  // â‚¹1000 withdrawn. Remaining balance: â‚¹6000


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Banking apps to create multiple user accounts with methods.

ğŸ”¹ 5. Example 3: Employee Management
function Employee(name, position, salary) {
  this.name = name;
  this.position = position;
  this.salary = salary;

  this.getDetails = function() {
    return `${this.name} works as ${this.position} with a salary of â‚¹${this.salary}`;
  };
}

let emp1 = new Employee("Amit", "Developer", 50000);
let emp2 = new Employee("Sneha", "Designer", 45000);

console.log(emp1.getDetails()); // Amit works as Developer with a salary of â‚¹50000
console.log(emp2.getDetails()); // Sneha works as Designer with a salary of â‚¹45000


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: HR apps managing multiple employees.

ğŸ”¹ 6. Key Points
Feature	Description	Real Use Case
this keyword	Refers to the newly created object	Access object properties
new keyword	Creates a new instance	Generate multiple products/users
Methods inside function	Functions defined in constructor	Deposit, Withdraw, GetDetails

âœ… Key Takeaways:

Constructor functions are pre-ES6 way of creating objects (before class).

Perfect for creating multiple objects with shared structure.

Can include methods and properties for each object.

=====================================================================
Prototypes & Prototype Chain
=====================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. What is a Prototype?

Every JavaScript object has a hidden prototype object.

The prototype contains properties and methods that other objects can inherit.

Helps in memory efficiency, because methods can be shared across all instances instead of being recreated.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Prototype Chain

When you access a property or method on an object:

JS first looks on the object itself.

If not found, it looks at the prototype.

This continues up the chain until it reaches Object.prototype.

If not found, returns undefined.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Real-Based Examples
âœ… Example 1: Adding Methods to Prototype (Product)
function Product(name, price) {
  this.name = name;
  this.price = price;
}

// Adding method to prototype
Product.prototype.getDetails = function() {
  return `${this.name} costs â‚¹${this.price}`;
};

let laptop = new Product("Laptop", 45000);
let phone = new Product("Phone", 15000);

console.log(laptop.getDetails()); // Laptop costs â‚¹45000
console.log(phone.getDetails());  // Phone costs â‚¹15000


Explanation:

getDetails() is not created for every object, it lives in prototype.

Memory-efficient for many objects.

ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: E-commerce apps â†’ many products sharing common methods.

âœ… Example 2: Inheritance via Prototype Chain (Customer & Premium Customer)
function Customer(name) {
  this.name = name;
}

Customer.prototype.greet = function() {
  return `Hello, ${this.name}!`;
};

// PremiumCustomer inherits Customer
function PremiumCustomer(name, discount) {
  Customer.call(this, name); // call parent constructor
  this.discount = discount;
}

PremiumCustomer.prototype = Object.create(Customer.prototype);
PremiumCustomer.prototype.constructor = PremiumCustomer;

PremiumCustomer.prototype.greet = function() {
  return `${Customer.prototype.greet.call(this)} You have ${this.discount}% discount!`;
};

let customer1 = new Customer("Suraj");
let customer2 = new PremiumCustomer("Riya", 20);

console.log(customer1.greet()); // Hello, Suraj!
console.log(customer2.greet()); // Hello, Riya! You have 20% discount!


Explanation:

PremiumCustomer inherits methods from Customer via prototype chain.

Can override methods (greet) â†’ Polymorphism.

ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Different types of users in apps.

âœ… Example 3: Checking Prototype Chain
console.log(laptop.__proto__ === Product.prototype); // true
console.log(Product.prototype.__proto__ === Object.prototype); // true
console.log(Object.prototype.__proto__); // null


Explanation:

__proto__ shows the prototype of an object.

The chain ends at Object.prototype.

ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Debugging inheritance in large apps.

âœ… Example 4: Prototype Methods for Shared Behavior (Bank Account)
function BankAccount(owner, balance) {
  this.owner = owner;
  this.balance = balance;
}

BankAccount.prototype.deposit = function(amount) {
  this.balance += amount;
  return `â‚¹${amount} deposited. Current balance: â‚¹${this.balance}`;
};

BankAccount.prototype.withdraw = function(amount) {
  if(amount > this.balance) return "Insufficient balance!";
  this.balance -= amount;
  return `â‚¹${amount} withdrawn. Remaining balance: â‚¹${this.balance}`;
};

let acc1 = new BankAccount("Suraj", 5000);
let acc2 = new BankAccount("Riya", 7000);

console.log(acc1.deposit(2000)); // â‚¹2000 deposited. Current balance: â‚¹7000
console.log(acc2.withdraw(3000)); // â‚¹3000 withdrawn. Remaining balance: â‚¹4000


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Banking apps â†’ multiple accounts sharing common operations.

âš¡ Summary
Concept	Description	Real Use Case
Prototype	Object containing shared properties/methods	Product methods, Bank operations
Prototype Chain	Inheritance chain of objects	Customer â†’ PremiumCustomer
Object.create()	Create new object with specific prototype	Inheritance setup
Memory Efficiency	Methods on prototype shared by all instances	Avoid recreating functions for each object
Method Overriding	Child can override parent method	Premium customer discount

âœ… Key Takeaways:

Prototypes allow shared methods â†’ saves memory.

Prototype chain enables inheritance and method lookup.

Essential for understanding JS OOP under the hood.

class & extends
=====================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. What is a Class in JavaScript?

A class is a blueprint to create objects with properties and methods.

Introduced in ES6.

Syntax is cleaner than constructor functions.

ğŸ”¹ 2. What is extends?

extends is used for inheritance.

A child class can inherit properties and methods from a parent class.

Use super() to call the parent constructor.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Real-Based Examples
âœ… Example 1: Classes & Objects (E-commerce Product)

  constructor(name, price, category) {
    this.name = name;
    this.price = price;
    this.category = category;
  }

    return `${this.name} (${this.category}) costs â‚¹${this.price}`;
  }
}

let laptop = new Product("Laptop", 45000, "Electronics");
let phone = new Product("Phone", 15000, "Electronics");

console.log(phone.getDetails());  // Phone (Electronics) costs â‚¹15000


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Represent products in an e-commerce app.

âœ… Example 2: Inheritance with extends (Customer & Premium Customer)

    this.name = name;
  }

  greet() {
    return `Hello, ${this.name}!`;
  }

class BankAccount {

  constructor(owner, balance) {
    this.owner = owner;
    this.#balance = balance;
  }

  deposit(amount) {
    this.#balance += amount;
    return `â‚¹${amount} deposited. Current balance: â‚¹${this.#balance}`;
  }

    return this.#balance;
  }

super & constructor
=====================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. constructor in JS

The constructor is a special method inside a class used to initialize object properties.

Every class can have one constructor.

If you donâ€™t define one, JS provides a default constructor.

ğŸ”¹ 2. super in JS

super is used in child classes to:

Call the parent class constructor

Access parent class methods

Must be called in child constructor before using this.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Real-Based Examples
âœ… Example 1: Basic Constructor
class Product {
  constructor(name, price) {
    this.name = name;
    this.price = price;
  }

  getDetails() {
    return `${this.name} costs â‚¹${this.price}`;
  }
}

let laptop = new Product("Laptop", 45000);
console.log(laptop.getDetails()); // Laptop costs â‚¹45000


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Create multiple products in an e-commerce platform.

âœ… Example 2: super in Child Class (Customer & PremiumCustomer)

  constructor(name) {
    this.name = name;
  }

  greet() {
    return `Hello, ${this.name}!`;
  }
}

  constructor(name, discount) {
    super(name); // call parent constructor
    this.discount = discount;
  }

    return `${super.greet()} You have ${this.discount}% discount!`;
  }
}

super.greet() â†’ calls parent method inside child method.

âœ… Key Takeaways:
