=====================================================================
Encapsulation, Inheritance, Polymorphism
=====================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. Encapsulation

Encapsulation is the concept of hiding internal data of an object and controlling access through methods.

In JS (ES6+), we use private properties with # and getter/setter methods.

âœ… Real-Based Example: Bank Account
class BankAccount {
  #balance; // private property

  constructor(owner, balance) {
    this.owner = owner;
    this.#balance = balance;
  }

  deposit(amount) {
    this.#balance += amount;
    console.log(`â‚¹${amount} deposited. Current balance: â‚¹${this.#balance}`);
  }

  withdraw(amount) {
    if(amount > this.#balance) {
      console.log("Insufficient balance!");
    } else {
      this.#balance -= amount;
      console.log(`â‚¹${amount} withdrawn. Remaining balance: â‚¹${this.#balance}`);
    }
  }

  getBalance() {
    return this.#balance;
  }
}

let acc = new BankAccount("Suraj", 5000);
acc.deposit(2000);       // â‚¹2000 deposited. Current balance: â‚¹7000
acc.withdraw(1000);      // â‚¹1000 withdrawn. Remaining balance: â‚¹6000
console.log(acc.getBalance()); // 6000
// console.log(acc.#balance); // âŒ Error: Private property


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Banking app â†’ keep balance safe from direct access.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Inheritance

Inheritance allows a child class to reuse properties and methods from a parent class.

Use extends and super() in JS.

âœ… Real-Based Example: Customer & Premium Customer
class Customer {
  constructor(name) {
    this.name = name;
  }

  greet() {
    return `Hello, ${this.name}!`;
  }
}

class PremiumCustomer extends Customer {
  constructor(name, discount) {
    super(name); // call parent constructor
    this.discount = discount;
  }

  greet() {
    return `${super.greet()} You have ${this.discount}% discount!`;
  }
}

let c1 = new Customer("Suraj");
let c2 = new PremiumCustomer("Riya", 20);

console.log(c1.greet()); // Hello, Suraj!
console.log(c2.greet()); // Hello, Riya! You have 20% discount!


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Different types of users with shared properties and customized behavior.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Polymorphism

Polymorphism means same method behaves differently depending on the object.

Achieved by method overriding in child classes.

âœ… Real-Based Example: Notifications
class Notification {
  send(message) {
    console.log(`Notification: ${message}`);
  }
}

class EmailNotification extends Notification {
  send(message) {
    console.log(`Email sent: ${message}`);
  }
}

class SMSNotification extends Notification {
  send(message) {
    console.log(`SMS sent: ${message}`);
  }
}

let notifications = [
  new Notification(),
  new EmailNotification(),
  new SMSNotification()
];

notifications.forEach(n => n.send("Your order is confirmed!"));


Output:

Notification: Your order is confirmed!
Email sent: Your order is confirmed!
SMS sent: Your order is confirmed!


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Multi-channel notifications system (parent + child behavior).

ğŸ”¹ 4. Full Real-Based Example (Banking System)
class BankAccount {
  #balance;
  constructor(owner, balance) {
    this.owner = owner;
    this.#balance = balance;
  }
  deposit(amount) { this.#balance += amount; }
  withdraw(amount) { this.#balance -= amount; }
  getBalance() { return this.#balance; }
}

class SavingsAccount extends BankAccount {
  constructor(owner, balance, interestRate) {
    super(owner, balance);
    this.interestRate = interestRate;
  }
  calculateInterest() { return this.getBalance() * (this.interestRate / 100); }
}

class CurrentAccount extends BankAccount {
  constructor(owner, balance, overdraftLimit) {
    super(owner, balance);
    this.overdraftLimit = overdraftLimit;
  }
  withdraw(amount) {
    if(amount > this.getBalance() + this.overdraftLimit) {
      console.log("Exceeded overdraft limit!");
    } else {
      super.withdraw(amount);
      console.log(`â‚¹${amount} withdrawn. Remaining balance: â‚¹${this.getBalance()}`);
    }
  }
}

let sAcc = new SavingsAccount("Suraj", 5000, 5);
console.log(sAcc.calculateInterest()); // 250

let cAcc = new CurrentAccount("Riya", 3000, 1000);
cAcc.withdraw(3500); // â‚¹3500 withdrawn. Remaining balance: â‚¹-500
cAcc.withdraw(5000); // Exceeded overdraft limit!


âœ… Whatâ€™s Demonstrated:

Encapsulation â†’ #balance private.

Inheritance â†’ SavingsAccount & CurrentAccount inherit BankAccount.

Polymorphism â†’ CurrentAccount overrides withdraw method.

âš¡ Summary
Concept	JS Feature	Real Use Case
Encapsulation	#private, getter/setter	Secure bank balance
Inheritance	extends, super()	Customer â†’ PremiumCustomer
Polymorphism	Method overriding	Notifications, Custom withdraw

=====================================================================
Object.create()
=====================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. What is Object.create()?

Object.create() is a method to create a new object using an existing object as its prototype.

Useful for prototypal inheritance without using classes or constructor functions.

Syntax:

let newObj = Object.create(protoObj);


protoObj â†’ The object to be used as the prototype of the new object.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Real-Based Examples
âœ… Example 1: Basic Object Inheritance
const productProto = {
  getDetails: function() {
    return `${this.name} costs â‚¹${this.price}`;
  }
};

const laptop = Object.create(productProto);
laptop.name = "Laptop";
laptop.price = 45000;

const phone = Object.create(productProto);
phone.name = "Phone";
phone.price = 15000;

console.log(laptop.getDetails()); // Laptop costs â‚¹45000
console.log(phone.getDetails());  // Phone costs â‚¹15000


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: E-commerce app â†’ multiple products sharing common methods.

âœ… Example 2: Banking System (Prototype Inheritance)
const bankAccountProto = {
  deposit: function(amount) {
    this.balance += amount;
    console.log(`â‚¹${amount} deposited. Current balance: â‚¹${this.balance}`);
  },
  withdraw: function(amount) {
    if(amount > this.balance) console.log("Insufficient balance!");
    else {
      this.balance -= amount;
      console.log(`â‚¹${amount} withdrawn. Remaining balance: â‚¹${this.balance}`);
    }
  }
};

const acc1 = Object.create(bankAccountProto);
acc1.owner = "Suraj";
acc1.balance = 5000;

const acc2 = Object.create(bankAccountProto);
acc2.owner = "Riya";
acc2.balance = 7000;

acc1.deposit(2000);  // â‚¹2000 deposited. Current balance: â‚¹7000
acc2.withdraw(3000); // â‚¹3000 withdrawn. Remaining balance: â‚¹4000


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Multiple bank accounts sharing deposit/withdraw functionality.

âœ… Example 3: Inheritance Chain
const customerProto = {
  greet: function() {
    return `Hello, ${this.name}!`;
  }
};

const premiumCustomerProto = Object.create(customerProto);
premiumCustomerProto.greet = function() {
  return `${customerProto.greet.call(this)} You have ${this.discount}% discount!`;
};

const customer1 = Object.create(customerProto);
customer1.name = "Suraj";

const customer2 = Object.create(premiumCustomerProto);
customer2.name = "Riya";
customer2.discount = 20;

console.log(customer1.greet()); // Hello, Suraj!
console.log(customer2.greet()); // Hello, Riya! You have 20% discount!


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Customer system â†’ Regular & Premium customers sharing behavior through prototypes.

âœ… Example 4: Shared Methods Without Classes
const notificationProto = {
  send: function(message) {
    console.log(`Notification: ${message}`);
  }
};

const emailNotification = Object.create(notificationProto);
emailNotification.send = function(message) {
  console.log(`Email sent: ${message}`);
};

const smsNotification = Object.create(notificationProto);
smsNotification.send = function(message) {
  console.log(`SMS sent: ${message}`);
};

emailNotification.send("Order confirmed!"); // Email sent: Order confirmed!
smsNotification.send("Order confirmed!");   // SMS sent: Order confirmed!


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Multi-channel notifications without classes.

âš¡ Summary
Feature	Description	Real Use Case
Object.create(proto)	Creates object with specified prototype	Products, Bank Accounts
Prototypal Inheritance	Methods shared across multiple objects	Customer â†’ PremiumCustomer
Memory Efficient	Methods stored on prototype, not instance	Notifications, Banking system
Alternative to Classes	Simple inheritance without ES6 class	Lightweight apps, utility objects

âœ… Key Takeaways:

Object.create() is a powerful tool for prototypal inheritance.

Allows shared methods without using classes or constructor functions.

Great for memory-efficient, real-world apps like e-commerce, banking, and notification systems.


===================================================================
ğŸ”µ 9. Functional Programming
===================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. What is Functional Programming (FP)?

Functional Programming is a programming paradigm where:

Functions are first-class citizens (they can be passed, returned, stored like data).

Focus on immutability (not changing data directly).

Functions are pure (same input â†’ same output, no side effects).

Uses higher-order functions, recursion, and composition.

ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Data processing in e-commerce, banking, analytics, etc.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Types / Core Concepts of Functional Programming
âœ… 1. Pure Functions

A function that always returns the same output for the same input and has no side effects.

// Pure Function: Tax Calculation
function calculateTax(price, taxRate) {
  return price * taxRate;
}

console.log(calculateTax(1000, 0.18)); // 180
console.log(calculateTax(1000, 0.18)); // 180 (always same)


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Tax/discount calculations in an e-commerce cart.

âœ… 2. Immutability

Data is not modified, instead new copies are created.

const cart = ["Laptop", "Phone"];

// Instead of modifying directly
const newCart = [...cart, "Tablet"];

console.log(cart);    // ["Laptop", "Phone"]
console.log(newCart); // ["Laptop", "Phone", "Tablet"]


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Shopping cart system (original cart should not be mutated directly).

âœ… 3. Higher-Order Functions

Functions that take other functions as arguments or return a function.

function applyDiscount(discountFn, price) {
  return discountFn(price);
}

const studentDiscount = price => price * 0.8;
const festiveDiscount = price => price * 0.9;

console.log(applyDiscount(studentDiscount, 1000)); // 800
console.log(applyDiscount(festiveDiscount, 1000)); // 900


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Flexible discount strategies in billing.

âœ… 4. First-Class Functions

Functions can be stored in variables, passed, and returned.

const notify = (msg) => console.log("Notification:", msg);

function sendNotification(fn, message) {
  fn(message);
}

sendNotification(notify, "Order Shipped!"); 
// Notification: Order Shipped!


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Notification service where the sending mechanism (email, SMS, push) can change.

âœ… 5. Function Composition

Combining small functions to build more complex functions.

const addGST = price => price + price * 0.18;
const applyCoupon = price => price - 100;

const finalPrice = (price) => applyCoupon(addGST(price));

console.log(finalPrice(1000)); // 1080


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Billing system â†’ Apply GST â†’ Apply coupon discount.

âœ… 6. Recursion

Using a function to call itself instead of loops.

function factorial(n) {
  if (n === 0) return 1;
  return n * factorial(n - 1);
}

console.log(factorial(5)); // 120


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Calculating factorials, tree structures, or nested categories in e-commerce.

âœ… 7. Map, Filter, Reduce (Functional Iteration)

Instead of for loops, FP uses these built-in higher-order functions.

const orders = [500, 1200, 3000, 700];

// Apply 10% discount to all orders
const discounted = orders.map(o => o * 0.9);

// Get only high-value orders
const highValue = orders.filter(o => o > 1000);

// Total revenue
const total = orders.reduce((sum, o) => sum + o, 0);

console.log(discounted); // [450, 1080, 2700, 630]
console.log(highValue);  // [1200, 3000]
console.log(total);      // 5400


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Analytics dashboard for e-commerce orders.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Summary Table of FP Concepts
Concept	Meaning	Real Use Case
Pure Functions	Same input â†’ Same output	Tax, Discount calculations
Immutability	Donâ€™t modify original data	Shopping cart, banking balance
Higher-Order Functions	Functions taking/returning functions	Discounts, Callbacks
First-Class Functions	Treat functions as values	Notifications, APIs
Function Composition	Combine smaller functions	Billing pipeline
Recursion	Function calls itself	Factorial, Tree navigation
Map/Filter/Reduce	Functional iteration	Reports, Analytics
âš¡ Real Example: E-commerce Order Processing (All FP Concepts Together)
const orders = [
  { id: 1, price: 1000, category: "electronics" },
  { id: 2, price: 500, category: "clothing" },
  { id: 3, price: 2000, category: "electronics" }
];

// Pure Function
const addGST = price => price + price * 0.18;

// Higher-Order Function
const applyDiscount = discount => price => price - discount;

// Composition
const finalPrice = (price) => applyDiscount(100)(addGST(price));

// Map / Filter / Reduce
const electronicsRevenue = orders
  .filter(order => order.category === "electronics")
  .map(order => finalPrice(order.price))
  .reduce((sum, price) => sum + price, 0);

console.log("Total Electronics Revenue:", electronicsRevenue);


ğŸ“Œ Output â†’ Total Electronics Revenue: 3420

ğŸ‘‰ So, Functional Programming in JS = writing clean, reusable, predictable code with functions as building blocks.

===================================================================
Pure Functions
===================================================================
ğŸ”¹ What is a Pure Function?

A Pure Function is a function that:

Always returns the same output for the same input.

Has no side effects (it doesnâ€™t change external variables, DOM, or state).

âœ… Example 1: Tax Calculation (E-commerce Checkout)
// Pure Function
function calculateTax(price, taxRate) {
  return price * taxRate;
}

console.log(calculateTax(1000, 0.18)); // 180
console.log(calculateTax(1000, 0.18)); // 180 (same input, same output)


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: While calculating tax in an e-commerce cart.

âœ… Example 2: Discount Price
// Pure Function
function applyDiscount(price, discount) {
  return price - discount;
}

console.log(applyDiscount(500, 50)); // 450
console.log(applyDiscount(500, 50)); // 450


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Applying discount coupons in online shopping.

âœ… Example 3: Banking - Interest Calculation
// Pure Function
function calculateInterest(principal, rate, time) {
  return (principal * rate * time) / 100;
}

console.log(calculateInterest(10000, 5, 2)); // 1000
console.log(calculateInterest(10000, 5, 2)); // 1000


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Calculating simple interest for loans/deposits.

âœ… Example 4: Delivery Fee Calculation (Food Delivery App)
// Pure Function
function deliveryFee(distanceKm, ratePerKm) {
  return distanceKm * ratePerKm;
}

console.log(deliveryFee(10, 5)); // 50
console.log(deliveryFee(10, 5)); // 50


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Calculating delivery charges based on distance.

ğŸš« Example of NOT a Pure Function
let discount = 50;

function applyDiscount(price) {
  // âŒ Not pure: uses external variable
  return price - discount;
}

console.log(applyDiscount(500)); // depends on external `discount`
discount = 100;
console.log(applyDiscount(500)); // output changes!


âš ï¸ Problem â†’ This function depends on an external variable, so itâ€™s not pure.

âœ¨ Summary:

Pure functions = predictable, testable, reusable.

Useful in e-commerce checkout, banking apps, delivery apps, billing systems.


===================================================================
Immutability
===================================================================
ğŸ”¹ What is Immutability?

Immutability means not modifying the original data.

Instead, a new copy of the data is created and returned.

This is important in functional programming and real-world apps like banking, e-commerce, ticket booking, etc.

âœ… Example 1: Shopping Cart (E-commerce)
const cart = ["Laptop", "Phone"];

// Function that follows immutability
function addItem(cart, item) {
  return [...cart, item]; // creates a new array, doesn't modify original
}

const newCart = addItem(cart, "Tablet");

console.log("Original Cart:", cart);     // ["Laptop", "Phone"]
console.log("Updated Cart:", newCart);   // ["Laptop", "Phone", "Tablet"]


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: When a customer adds an item to the cart, the old cart remains unchanged (important for undo/redo or order history).

âœ… Example 2: Bank Balance Update (Banking System)
const account = { name: "Suraj", balance: 5000 };

// Immutable function
function deposit(account, amount) {
  return { ...account, balance: account.balance + amount };
}

const updatedAccount = deposit(account, 2000);

console.log("Old Account:", account);            // balance: 5000
console.log("Updated Account:", updatedAccount); // balance: 7000


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Keeps transaction history safe â€” old balance isnâ€™t lost.

âœ… Example 3: Employee Records (HR System)
const employee = { id: 1, name: "Akash", role: "Developer" };

// Immutable update
function promoteEmployee(employee, newRole) {
  return { ...employee, role: newRole };
}

const promoted = promoteEmployee(employee, "Team Lead");

console.log("Old Employee:", employee);  // role: Developer
console.log("Promoted Employee:", promoted); // role: Team Lead


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Employee record updates should not overwrite history (audit trail).

âœ… Example 4: Food Delivery Orders
const order = { id: 101, items: ["Burger", "Fries"] };

// Immutable add item
function addOrderItem(order, newItem) {
  return { ...order, items: [...order.items, newItem] };
}

const updatedOrder = addOrderItem(order, "Coke");

console.log("Original Order:", order);       // items: ["Burger", "Fries"]
console.log("Updated Order:", updatedOrder); // items: ["Burger", "Fries", "Coke"]


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Customer modifies order, but old state is preserved for records.

ğŸš« Example of Mutable Function (Bad Practice)
let numbers = [1, 2, 3];

function addNumber(arr, num) {
  arr.push(num); // âŒ modifies original array
  return arr;
}

addNumber(numbers, 4);
console.log(numbers); // [1, 2, 3, 4] â†’ Original changed!


âš ï¸ Problem: Mutating functions make debugging harder (unexpected changes in shared data).

âœ¨ Benefits of Immutability in Functions

Predictable behavior (no hidden changes).

Undo/Redo features (shopping cart, forms).

Time-travel debugging (used in Redux, React apps).

Safer in concurrency/multi-user apps (banking, stock trading).


===================================================================
First-class Functions
===================================================================
ğŸ”¹ What are First-Class Functions?

In JavaScript, functions are first-class citizens.
That means:

Functions can be stored in variables.

Functions can be passed as arguments.

Functions can be returned from other functions.

ğŸ“Œ This is the foundation of functional programming.

âœ… Example 1: Storing Function in a Variable (Notifications)
// Function stored in a variable
const notify = function(message) {
  console.log("Notification:", message);
};

notify("Your order has been shipped!");


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Notification systems (storing SMS, Email, Push functions in variables).

âœ… Example 2: Passing Function as an Argument (Payment Gateway)
function processPayment(amount, paymentMethod) {
  paymentMethod(amount);
}

// Different payment methods
const payWithCard = amt => console.log(`Paid â‚¹${amt} using Card`);
const payWithUPI = amt => console.log(`Paid â‚¹${amt} using UPI`);

// Passing functions
processPayment(1000, payWithCard);
processPayment(500, payWithUPI);


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Payment gateways use functions to handle different payment methods dynamically.

âœ… Example 3: Returning a Function (Discount Generator)
function createDiscount(discountPercent) {
  return function(price) {
    return price - (price * discountPercent / 100);
  };
}

const studentDiscount = createDiscount(20);
const festiveDiscount = createDiscount(10);

console.log(studentDiscount(1000)); // 800
console.log(festiveDiscount(1000)); // 900


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: E-commerce systems generate dynamic discounts.

âœ… Example 4: Array Methods (Map / Filter / Reduce use functions)
const orders = [500, 1200, 800];

// Using function as argument
const withGST = orders.map(price => price + price * 0.18);

console.log(withGST); // [590, 1416, 944]


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Processing order amounts with GST in billing.

âœ… Example 5: Event Handling (Web Apps)
document.getElementById("btn").addEventListener("click", function() {
  console.log("Button Clicked!");
});


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Passing a function directly to handle UI events.

ğŸš« Example (Not First-Class Functions in Some Languages)

In some older languages, functions are not treated as data (you cannot assign them to variables or return them).
ğŸ‘‰ JavaScriptâ€™s first-class functions make it powerful for callbacks, promises, async/await, and functional programming.

âœ¨ Summary

Store in variables â†’ Notification example

Pass as arguments â†’ Payment gateway example

Return from function â†’ Discount system example

Use in array methods â†’ Order processing example

Event handling â†’ Web apps

===================================================================
Higher-Order Functions
===================================================================
ğŸ”¹ What is a Higher-Order Function (HOF)?

A Higher-Order Function is a function that does one or both of the following:

Takes another function as an argument.

Returns another function.

ğŸ‘‰ All Higher-Order Functions are built upon the concept of first-class functions.

âœ… Example 1: Payment Processing (Takes Function as Argument)
function processPayment(amount, paymentMethod) {
  return paymentMethod(amount);  // HOF calls another function
}

const payWithCard = amt => `Paid â‚¹${amt} using Card ğŸ’³`;
const payWithUPI  = amt => `Paid â‚¹${amt} using UPI ğŸ“±`;

console.log(processPayment(1000, payWithCard));
console.log(processPayment(500, payWithUPI));


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Payment gateways call different payment methods dynamically.

âœ… Example 2: Discount Generator (Returns Function)
function createDiscount(discountPercent) {
  return function(price) {  // Returning a function
    return price - (price * discountPercent / 100);
  };
}

const studentDiscount = createDiscount(20);
const festiveDiscount = createDiscount(10);

console.log(studentDiscount(1000)); // 800
console.log(festiveDiscount(1000)); // 900


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: E-commerce apps create different discount strategies.

âœ… Example 3: Array Methods (Built-in HOFs â†’ map, filter, reduce)
const orders = [500, 1200, 300, 800];

// 1. Apply GST (map)
const withGST = orders.map(price => price + price * 0.18);

// 2. Filter high-value orders
const highValue = orders.filter(price => price > 1000);

// 3. Calculate total revenue
const total = orders.reduce((sum, price) => sum + price, 0);

console.log("With GST:", withGST);
console.log("High Value:", highValue);
console.log("Total Revenue:", total);


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Processing e-commerce orders efficiently.

âœ… Example 4: Middleware in Authentication
function authenticateUser(role) {
  return function(req) {
    if (req.userRole === role) {
      return "Access Granted âœ…";
    } else {
      return "Access Denied âŒ";
    }
  };
}

const adminAuth = authenticateUser("admin");
const userAuth  = authenticateUser("user");

console.log(adminAuth({ userRole: "admin" })); // âœ…
console.log(userAuth({ userRole: "guest" })); // âŒ


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Web apps use HOFs in authentication middleware.

âœ… Example 5: Event Handling with HOF
function addEvent(id, handler) {
  document.getElementById(id).addEventListener("click", handler);
}

addEvent("buyBtn", () => console.log("Item Purchased ğŸ›’"));
addEvent("cancelBtn", () => console.log("Order Cancelled âŒ"));


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Reusable function for attaching multiple event listeners.

âœ¨ Summary Table
Concept	Example Code	Real Use Case
Takes function as arg	processPayment(fn)	Payment methods
Returns function	createDiscount()	Dynamic discount
Built-in HOFs	map, filter, reduce	Order processing
Middleware	authenticateUser(role)	Authentication
Event Handling	addEvent(id, fn)	UI interactions

âš¡ In short:

First-Class Functions â†’ Functions can be treated as values.

Higher-Order Functions â†’ Functions that use other functions (take or return them).


===================================================================
Currying & Partial Application
===================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. Currying in JavaScript

ğŸ‘‰ Definition:
Currying is the process of transforming a function with multiple arguments into a sequence of functions, each taking a single argument.

// Normal function
function calculateTotal(price, quantity, tax) {
  return price * quantity + tax;
}

// Curried version
function curriedCalculateTotal(price) {
  return function(quantity) {
    return function(tax) {
      return price * quantity + tax;
    };
  };
}

console.log(curriedCalculateTotal(100)(2)(10)); // 210


âœ… Real Use Case: E-commerce Checkout
You can reuse curried functions to partially apply known values (like tax or default quantity).

const priceWithTax = curriedCalculateTotal(100); 
const withTwoQty   = priceWithTax(2); 

console.log(withTwoQty(10));  // 210
console.log(withTwoQty(20));  // 220

ğŸ”¹ 2. Partial Application in JavaScript

ğŸ‘‰ Definition:
Partial application is when you fix some arguments of a function and create a new function with the remaining arguments.

function calculateTotal(price, quantity, tax) {
  return price * quantity + tax;
}

// Partial Application: Fix price = 100
const calculateForItem = calculateTotal.bind(null, 100);

console.log(calculateForItem(2, 10)); // 210
console.log(calculateForItem(3, 5));  // 305


âœ… Real Use Case: Food Delivery App
Fixing the item price in a food delivery system.

const pizzaOrder = calculateTotal.bind(null, 250);
console.log(pizzaOrder(2, 20)); // 520

ğŸ”¹ 3. Currying vs Partial Application
Feature	Currying	Partial Application
Definition	Breaks a function into nested single-argument functions	Fixes some arguments and creates a new function
Example	f(a)(b)(c)	f.bind(null, a)
Flexibility	Always takes one argument at a time	Can fix one or more arguments at once
Real Use Case	Checkout system where tax/discount is applied step by step	Food order where price is fixed, but quantity & tax vary
ğŸ”¹ 4. Real-World Example: Online Food Ordering System
// Normal function
function orderTotal(price, quantity, tax) {
  return price * quantity + tax;
}

// Currying
function curriedOrder(price) {
  return function(quantity) {
    return function(tax) {
      return price * quantity + tax;
    };
  };
}

// Partial Application
const burgerOrder = orderTotal.bind(null, 150); // Fix price of burger

console.log("Curried Pizza:", curriedOrder(200)(2)(30)); // 430
console.log("Burger Order:", burgerOrder(3, 20));        // 470


ğŸ“Œ Explanation:

Currying: Breaks order into steps â†’ Price â†’ Quantity â†’ Tax.

Partial Application: Fixes burger price, only quantity & tax change.

âœ¨ Summary

Currying â†’ Chain of single-argument functions.

Partial Application â†’ Pre-fills some arguments, keeps rest open.

Both are useful in e-commerce, billing, food delivery, and payment gateways for reusability & clean code.

===================================================================
Composition & Pipelines
===================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. What is Function Composition?

ğŸ‘‰ Function Composition = combining two or more small functions into a new function.
It allows you to build complex logic from reusable pieces.

const addGST = price => price + price * 0.18;
const applyDiscount = price => price - 100;

// Composition
const finalPrice = price => applyDiscount(addGST(price));

console.log(finalPrice(1000)); // 1080


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: E-commerce checkout system â†’ Apply GST â†’ Apply discount.

ğŸ”¹ 2. What is a Pipeline?

ğŸ‘‰ Pipeline = executing functions in sequence, passing output of one as input to the next (left-to-right flow).
Itâ€™s like a factory assembly line.

// Simple pipeline function
const pipe = (...fns) => (value) => fns.reduce((acc, fn) => fn(acc), value);

const addGST = price => price + price * 0.18;
const applyDiscount = price => price - 100;
const addDelivery = price => price + 50;

// Pipeline
const checkoutPipeline = pipe(addGST, applyDiscount, addDelivery);

console.log(checkoutPipeline(1000)); // 1130


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Food delivery app billing â†’ GST â†’ Discount â†’ Delivery charge.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Real-World Example: Bank Loan Processing
// Functions
const addProcessingFee = amount => amount + 500;
const addInterest = amount => amount + amount * 0.1;
const deductTax = amount => amount - 200;

// Composition (manual nesting)
const finalLoanAmount = amount => deductTax(addInterest(addProcessingFee(amount)));

console.log(finalLoanAmount(10000)); // 11200


ğŸ“Œ Use Case: Bank loan calculation â†’ Processing fee â†’ Interest â†’ Tax.

ğŸ”¹ 4. Real-World Example: Data Cleaning in Analytics
const trimSpaces = str => str.trim();
const toLowerCase = str => str.toLowerCase();
const removeSpecialChars = str => str.replace(/[^a-z0-9 ]/gi, "");

// Pipeline
const cleanData = pipe(trimSpaces, toLowerCase, removeSpecialChars);

console.log(cleanData("   Hello@World!!  ")); // "helloworld"


ğŸ“Œ Use Case: Cleaning user input for analytics or search engines.

ğŸ”¹ 5. Comparison: Composition vs Pipeline
Feature	Composition	Pipeline
Flow	Usually right-to-left (nested)	Left-to-right (assembly line)
Example	f(g(h(x)))	pipe(f, g, h)(x)
Readability	Harder when deeply nested	Cleaner, step-by-step
Use Case	Mathematical/logical operations	Data transformations, ETL, checkout systems
ğŸ”¹ 6. Advanced Example: E-commerce Checkout Pipeline
// Step functions
const addGST = price => price + price * 0.18;
const applyDiscount = price => price - 200;
const addDelivery = price => price + 50;
const roundOff = price => Math.round(price);

// Pipe function
const pipe = (...fns) => (val) => fns.reduce((acc, fn) => fn(acc), val);

// Build pipeline
const checkout = pipe(addGST, applyDiscount, addDelivery, roundOff);

console.log("Final Bill:", checkout(1200)); // Final Bill: 1266


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Checkout pipeline ensures billing is consistent, reusable, and testable.

âœ¨ Summary

Composition = combine functions (f(g(x))).

Pipeline = sequence of functions (pipe(f, g, h)(x)).

Used in e-commerce billing, banking, food delivery, and data processing systems.

10. Browser APIs

ğŸ”¹ 1. What are Browser APIs?

ğŸ‘‰ Browser APIs are built-in interfaces provided by the browser that let JavaScript interact with the browser environment (outside the core JS language).

ğŸ“Œ Example: Handling DOM, making HTTP requests, using camera, local storage, geolocation, etc.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Types of Browser APIs with Real Examples
âœ… A. DOM (Document Object Model) API

Used for interacting with HTML & CSS.

Example: Dynamic To-Do App

<button id="add">Add Task</button>
<ul id="tasks"></ul>

<script>
document.getElementById("add").addEventListener("click", () => {
  const task = document.createElement("li");
  task.textContent = "âœ… New Task";
  document.getElementById("tasks").appendChild(task);
});
</script>


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Adding/removing tasks in a to-do list.

âœ… B. Fetch API

Used for making HTTP requests.

Example: Weather App

fetch("https://api.open-meteo.com/v1/forecast?latitude=18.52&longitude=73.85&current_weather=true")
  .then(res => res.json())
  .then(data => console.log("Current Temperature:", data.current_weather.temperature))
  .catch(err => console.error(err));


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Fetching live weather data for a city.

âœ… C. Storage APIs

localStorage â†’ persists data even after browser reload.

sessionStorage â†’ clears data when tab closes.

Example: Remember Dark Mode Preference

// Save preference

// Load preference

ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Remember user theme (dark/light) in apps.

âœ… D. Geolocation API

Used for accessing the userâ€™s location.

Example: Food Delivery App

navigator.geolocation.getCurrentPosition(
  position => {
    console.log("Latitude:", position.coords.latitude);
    console.log("Longitude:", position.coords.longitude);
  },
  error => console.error("Error getting location", error)
);


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Auto-detect delivery location in Zomato/Swiggy.

âœ… E. Canvas API

Used for 2D graphics, charts, games.

Example: Drawing a Circle

<canvas id="myCanvas" width="200" height="200"></canvas>
<script>
const canvas = document.getElementById("myCanvas");

ctx.beginPath();
ctx.arc(100, 100, 50, 0, 2 * Math.PI);
ctx.fillStyle = "blue";
ctx.fill();
</script>


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Drawing charts, signatures, or animations.

âœ… F. Web Storage & IndexedDB API

For structured storage in the browser.

Example: Storing User Profile

indexedDB.open("UserDB", 1).onsuccess = function(event) {
  console.log("Database opened successfully!");
};


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Offline storage in apps like Google Keep.

âœ… G. Notifications API

Show native notifications.

Example: Chat App

if (Notification.permission === "granted") {
  new Notification("ğŸ”” New Message from Akash!");
} else {
  Notification.requestPermission();
}


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: WhatsApp Web notifications.

âœ… H. Media APIs

Access camera, microphone.

Example: Video Call

navigator.mediaDevices.getUserMedia({ video: true, audio: true })
  .then(stream => {
    document.querySelector("video").srcObject = stream;
  })
  .catch(err => console.error("Camera error:", err));


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Zoom/Google Meet camera access.

âœ… I. Web Workers API

Run scripts in background (multithreading).

Example: Heavy Calculation without Freezing UI

const worker = new Worker("worker.js");
worker.postMessage(50000000);

worker.onmessage = function(e) {
  console.log("Sum:", e.data);
};


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Processing large data files in background.

âœ… J. Clipboard API

Read & write to clipboard.

  .then(() => console.log("Copied to clipboard!"));


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: "Copy OTP" button in banking apps.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Categories of Browser APIs

DOM & UI APIs â†’ document, classList, canvas.

Network APIs â†’ fetch, WebSocket, XMLHttpRequest.

Storage APIs â†’ localStorage, sessionStorage, IndexedDB.

Multimedia APIs â†’ MediaDevices, Audio, Canvas.

âœ… Summary:

===================================================================
LocalStorage & SessionStorage
===================================================================
ğŸ”‘ 1. LocalStorage

Stores data with no expiration.

Data remains even after the browser is closed and reopened.

Stored per domain.

ğŸ“Œ Syntax

// Set item
localStorage.setItem("key", "value");

// Get item
let data = localStorage.getItem("key");

// Remove item
localStorage.removeItem("key");

// Clear all
localStorage.clear();

âœ… Real Example: Saving Dark Mode Preference
<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<head>
  <title>Dark Mode Example</title>
</head>
<body>
  <button id="toggle">Toggle Dark Mode</button>

  <script>
    const body = document.body;
    const btn = document.getElementById("toggle");

    // Apply stored mode on page load
    if (localStorage.getItem("theme") === "dark") {
      body.style.backgroundColor = "black";
      body.style.color = "white";
    }

    btn.addEventListener("click", () => {
      if (localStorage.getItem("theme") === "dark") {
        localStorage.setItem("theme", "light");
        body.style.backgroundColor = "white";
        body.style.color = "black";
      } else {
        localStorage.setItem("theme", "dark");
        body.style.backgroundColor = "black";
        body.style.color = "white";
      }
    });
  </script>
</body>
</html>


ğŸ‘‰ Even after refreshing or reopening the browser, the dark mode preference stays saved.

ğŸ”‘ 2. SessionStorage

Stores data only for a single session.

Data is deleted once the browser tab/window is closed.

Useful for temporary storage.

ğŸ“Œ Syntax

// Set item
sessionStorage.setItem("key", "value");

// Get item
let data = sessionStorage.getItem("key");

// Remove item
sessionStorage.removeItem("key");

// Clear all
sessionStorage.clear();

âœ… Real Example: Storing Logged-In Session Data
<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<head>
  <title>Session Example</title>
</head>
<body>
  <button id="login">Login</button>
  <button id="logout">Logout</button>

  <script>
    const loginBtn = document.getElementById("login");
    const logoutBtn = document.getElementById("logout");

    // On login
    loginBtn.addEventListener("click", () => {
      sessionStorage.setItem("user", "Suraj");
      alert("User logged in: " + sessionStorage.getItem("user"));
    });

    // On logout
    logoutBtn.addEventListener("click", () => {
      sessionStorage.removeItem("user");
      alert("User logged out!");
    });

    // Auto-check if user exists
    if (sessionStorage.getItem("user")) {
      alert("Welcome back: " + sessionStorage.getItem("user"));
    }
  </script>
</body>
</html>


ğŸ‘‰ As soon as you close the tab, the user session data is cleared.

âš–ï¸ LocalStorage vs SessionStorage
Feature	LocalStorage ğŸ—‚ï¸	SessionStorage ğŸ•’
Expiration	Never (until cleared)	Ends with tab/browser close
Scope	Across tabs & windows	Only in the current tab
Use Case	Preferences, themes, shopping cart	Temporary login data, form data during session
Size Limit	~5MB	~5MB

===================================================================
Geolocation API
===================================================================
ğŸŒ Geolocation API Examples with Real Scenarios
âœ… Example 1: Show Userâ€™s Current Coordinates (Basic Example)

ğŸ‘‰ Real Case: Used in weather apps to get userâ€™s location for local weather.

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<head>
  <title>Current Location</title>
</head>
<body>
  <h2>Get Current Location</h2>
  <button onclick="getLocation()">Get My Location</button>
  <p id="location"></p>

  <script>
    function getLocation() {
      const output = document.getElementById("location");

      if (navigator.geolocation) {
        navigator.geolocation.getCurrentPosition((pos) => {
          output.innerHTML =
            "Latitude: " + pos.coords.latitude + "<br>" +
            "Longitude: " + pos.coords.longitude;
        }, (err) => {
          output.innerHTML = "Error: " + err.message;
        });
      } else {
        output.innerHTML = "Geolocation not supported in this browser.";
      }
    }
  </script>
</body>
</html>

âœ… Example 2: Track Userâ€™s Movement (Live Tracking)

ğŸ‘‰ Real Case: Used in Ola/Uber for real-time driver tracking.

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<head>
  <title>Live Tracking</title>
</head>
<body>
  <h2>Tracking Your Location...</h2>
  <p id="track"></p>

  <script>
    if (navigator.geolocation) {
      navigator.geolocation.watchPosition((pos) => {
        document.getElementById("track").innerHTML =
          "Latitude: " + pos.coords.latitude + "<br>" +
          "Longitude: " + pos.coords.longitude + "<br>" +
          "Accuracy: " + pos.coords.accuracy + " meters";
      }, (err) => {
        document.getElementById("track").innerHTML = "Error: " + err.message;
      });
    }
  </script>
</body>
</html>

âœ… Example 3: Show Location on Google Maps

ğŸ‘‰ Real Case: Zomato/Swiggy showing delivery personâ€™s live location on Google Maps.

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<head>
  <title>Show Location on Google Maps</title>
</head>
<body>
  <h2>Your Location on Map</h2>
  <button onclick="showMap()">Open in Google Maps</button>

  <script>
    function showMap() {
      if (navigator.geolocation) {
        navigator.geolocation.getCurrentPosition((pos) => {
          const lat = pos.coords.latitude;
          const lon = pos.coords.longitude;
          window.open(`https://www.google.com/maps?q=${lat},${lon}`, "_blank");
        });
      }
    }
  </script>
</body>
</html>

âœ… Example 4: Weather App â€“ Get Location and Show Weather

ğŸ‘‰ Real Case: Weather apps (AccuWeather, Weather.com, Google Weather).

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<head>
  <title>Weather App</title>
</head>
<body>
  <h2>Weather App</h2>
  <button onclick="getWeather()">Get My Weather</button>
  <p id="weather"></p>

  <script>
    function getWeather() {
      const output = document.getElementById("weather");

      if (navigator.geolocation) {
        navigator.geolocation.getCurrentPosition(async (pos) => {
          const lat = pos.coords.latitude;
          const lon = pos.coords.longitude;

          // Fetch weather from OpenWeatherMap API
          const res = await fetch(`https://api.open-meteo.com/v1/forecast?latitude=${lat}&longitude=${lon}&current_weather=true`);
          const data = await res.json();

          output.innerHTML = 
            `ğŸŒ¡ Temperature: ${data.current_weather.temperature}Â°C <br> 
             ğŸ’¨ Windspeed: ${data.current_weather.windspeed} km/h`;
        });
      }
    }
  </script>
</body>
</html>

âœ… Example 5: Form Autofill with Location

ğŸ‘‰ Real Case: E-commerce apps autofill your address/location when ordering.

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<head>
  <title>Auto Location Form</title>
</head>
<body>
  <h2>Shipping Form</h2>
  <form>
    Address: <input type="text" id="address" size="50"><br><br>
    <button type="button" onclick="fillAddress()">Use My Location</button>
  </form>

  <script>
    function fillAddress() {
      if (navigator.geolocation) {
        navigator.geolocation.getCurrentPosition((pos) => {
          const lat = pos.coords.latitude;
          const lon = pos.coords.longitude;
          document.getElementById("address").value =
            `Lat: ${lat}, Lon: ${lon}`;
        });
      }
    }
  </script>
</body>
</html>

âš¡ Real-World Use Cases of Geolocation API

âœ… Zomato/Swiggy â†’ Show live food delivery tracking
âœ… Google Maps â†’ Turn-by-turn navigation

âœ… Weather apps â†’ Show live weather based on location


===================================================================
Fetch & API Calls
===================================================================
ğŸ“Œ Fetch & API Calls in JavaScript

The Fetch API is a modern way to make HTTP requests (GET, POST, PUT, DELETE) in JavaScript. It is promise-based, easier, and cleaner than the old XMLHttpRequest.

âœ… Basic Syntax
fetch(url, options)
  .then(response => response.json())
  .then(data => console.log(data))
  .catch(error => console.log("Error:", error));


url â†’ API endpoint (server address)

options â†’ method, headers, body, etc.

âœ… Example 1: GET Request â€“ Fetching Data

ğŸ‘‰ Real Case: Showing a list of products in an E-commerce app

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<head>
  <title>Fetch Example</title>
</head>
<body>
  <h2>Products</h2>
  <ul id="products"></ul>

  <script>
    fetch("https://fakestoreapi.com/products") // Fake products API
      .then(res => res.json())
      .then(data => {
        const list = document.getElementById("products");
        data.forEach(product => {
          const li = document.createElement("li");
          li.textContent = product.title + " - $" + product.price;
          list.appendChild(li);
        });
      })
      .catch(err => console.log("Error:", err));
  </script>
</body>
</html>


ğŸ‘‰ This fetches fake products and displays them like an online shop.

âœ… Example 2: POST Request â€“ Submitting a Form

ğŸ‘‰ Real Case: User registration in websites like LinkedIn, Facebook.

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<head>
  <title>POST Example</title>
</head>
<body>
  <h2>Register</h2>
  <form id="registerForm">
    <input type="text" id="username" placeholder="Username" required><br><br>
    <input type="email" id="email" placeholder="Email" required><br><br>
    <button type="submit">Register</button>
  </form>
  <p id="msg"></p>

  <script>
    document.getElementById("registerForm").addEventListener("submit", function(e) {
      e.preventDefault();

      const user = {
        username: document.getElementById("username").value,
        email: document.getElementById("email").value
      };

      fetch("https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/users", {
        method: "POST",
        headers: { "Content-Type": "application/json" },
        body: JSON.stringify(user)
      })
      .then(res => res.json())
      .then(data => {
        document.getElementById("msg").innerText = "User registered: " + data.username;
      })
      .catch(err => console.log("Error:", err));
    });
  </script>
</body>
</html>


ğŸ‘‰ Simulates a signup form and sends user data to an API.

âœ… Example 3: PUT Request â€“ Update Data

ğŸ‘‰ Real Case: Editing profile details in LinkedIn / Facebook.

  method: "PUT",
  headers: { "Content-Type": "application/json" },
  body: JSON.stringify({
    id: 1,
    username: "Suraj",
    email: "suraj@example.com"
  })
})
.then(res => res.json())
.then(data => console.log("Updated User:", data));

âœ… Example 4: DELETE Request â€“ Remove Data

ğŸ‘‰ Real Case: Deleting a post in Instagram or Twitter.

  method: "DELETE"
})
.then(() => console.log("Post deleted successfully!"))
.catch(err => console.log("Error:", err));

âš¡ Real-World Use Cases of Fetch API

===================================================================
Web Workers
===================================================================
ğŸ§µ Web Workers in JavaScript
ğŸ“Œ What are Web Workers?

Web Workers allow you to run JavaScript code in the background, separate from the main UI thread.

They help prevent the UI from freezing or becoming unresponsive during heavy computations.

They run in a separate thread but cannot directly manipulate the DOM.

âœ… Why Use Web Workers?

Heavy calculations (e.g., math operations, large loops).

Fetching and processing large amounts of data.

Background tasks (chat notifications, stock price updates).

Real-time apps (games, data visualization).

ğŸ“Œ How to Create a Web Worker

Create a worker script file (e.g., worker.js).

Use new Worker("worker.js") in the main script.

Communicate using postMessage() and onmessage.

âœ… Example 1: Simple Web Worker

ğŸ‘‰ Real Case: Doing a heavy calculation without blocking UI.

ğŸ”¹ worker.js

// worker.js
self.onmessage = function(e) {
  let num = e.data;
  let result = 0;
  for (let i = 0; i <= num; i++) {
    result += i;
  }
  self.postMessage(result);
};


ğŸ”¹ index.html

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<head>
  <title>Web Worker Example</title>
</head>
<body>
  <h2>Web Worker Demo</h2>
  <button onclick="startWorker()">Calculate Sum</button>
  <p id="output"></p>

  <script>
    function startWorker() {
      const worker = new Worker("worker.js");
      worker.postMessage(1000000000); // heavy calculation

      worker.onmessage = function(e) {
        document.getElementById("output").innerText = "Sum: " + e.data;
      };
    }
  </script>
</body>
</html>


ğŸ‘‰ The sum is calculated in background while UI stays responsive.

âœ… Example 2: Background Data Fetching

ğŸ‘‰ Real Case: Fetching live stock prices or crypto prices in background.

ğŸ”¹ worker.js

// worker.js
self.onmessage = async function(e) {
  const url = e.data;

  try {
    const response = await fetch(url);
    const data = await response.json();
    self.postMessage(data);
  } catch (error) {
    self.postMessage({ error: error.message });
  }
};


ğŸ”¹ index.html

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<head>
  <title>Stock Price Worker</title>
</head>
<body>
  <h2>Crypto Prices</h2>
  <button onclick="getPrices()">Fetch Prices</button>
  <pre id="prices"></pre>

  <script>
    function getPrices() {
      const worker = new Worker("worker.js");
      worker.postMessage("https://api.coindesk.com/v1/bpi/currentprice.json");

      worker.onmessage = function(e) {
        document.getElementById("prices").innerText = JSON.stringify(e.data, null, 2);
      };
    }
  </script>
</body>
</html>


ğŸ‘‰ Data fetching runs without blocking UI, perfect for finance or trading apps.

âœ… Example 3: Real-Time Chat Notification Worker

ğŸ‘‰ Real Case: WhatsApp Web or Slack checking for new messages.

ğŸ”¹ worker.js

// worker.js
setInterval(() => {
  self.postMessage("ğŸ”” New chat notification at " + new Date().toLocaleTimeString());
}, 5000);


ğŸ”¹ index.html

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html>
<head>
  <title>Chat Notifications</title>
</head>
<body>
  <h2>Chat App</h2>
  <div id="chat"></div>

  <script>
    const worker = new Worker("worker.js");

    worker.onmessage = function(e) {
      const chatBox = document.getElementById("chat");
      const msg = document.createElement("p");
      msg.textContent = e.data;
      chatBox.appendChild(msg);
    };
  </script>
</body>
</html>


ğŸ‘‰ User gets non-blocking notifications while using the app.

âš¡ Real-World Use Cases of Web Workers

Google Maps â†’ Heavy route calculations.

Photo editing apps â†’ Processing images without freezing UI.

Finance apps â†’ Background stock/crypto price updates.

Gaming apps â†’ Smooth animations + background AI calculations.

Chat apps â†’ Live message notifications.

ğŸ“Œ Quick Summary

Web Workers = Run tasks in background.

Use for heavy computations, data fetching, real-time updates.

Communicate with â†’ postMessage() and onmessage.

ğŸ”” Notifications API in JavaScript
âœ… What is it?

The Notifications API allows web apps to send system-level notifications to users.

Useful for reminders, alerts, messages, order updates, news, etc.

ğŸ”¹ Basic Syntax
if ("Notification" in window) {
  Notification.requestPermission().then(permission => {
    if (permission === "granted") {
      new Notification("Hello! ğŸš€", {
        body: "This is a test notification",
        icon: "https://via.placeholder.com/100"
      });
    }
  });
}

ğŸ”¹ Real-World Example: Task Reminder App
function showTaskReminder(task) {
  if (Notification.permission === "granted") {
    new Notification("ğŸ“ Task Reminder", {
      body: `Don't forget: ${task}`,
      icon: "https://cdn-icons-png.flaticon.com/512/992/992700.png"
    });
  }
}

// Example: Show reminder after 5 seconds

  showTaskReminder("Complete your project report");
}, 5000);


ğŸ‘‰ Use Case: Task management apps (Todoist, Trello, Jira).

ğŸ“‹ Clipboard API in JavaScript
âœ… What is it?

The Clipboard API allows web apps to read from or write to the clipboard.

Useful for copy-paste features, code snippet sharing, URL copy buttons.

ğŸ”¹ Write to Clipboard (Copy Feature)

async function copyToClipboard() {
  const text = document.getElementById("copyText").value;
  await navigator.clipboard.writeText(text);
  alert("âœ… Copied to clipboard: " + text);
}
</script>


ğŸ‘‰ Use Case: Copy invite link, coupon codes, or referral codes.

ğŸ”¹ Read from Clipboard (Paste Feature)
<button onclick="pasteFromClipboard()">Paste Text</button>

  const text = await navigator.clipboard.readText();
  document.getElementById("output").innerText = "ğŸ“‹ Pasted: " + text;
}
</script>


ğŸ‘‰ Use Case: Paste OTP codes, paste chat text, paste email addresses.

===================================================================
11. Asynchronous JavaScript (Deep Dive)
===================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. What is Asynchronous JavaScript?

JavaScript is single-threaded (it can execute only one thing at a time).
ğŸ‘‰ Asynchronous programming allows JS to perform long-running tasks (like API calls, file reading, timers) without blocking the main thread.

ğŸ“Œ Example: While fetching weather data from an API, the UI should still be usable.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Ways to Handle Asynchronous Code
âœ… A. Callbacks

A function passed as an argument to another function, executed after a task finishes.

Example: Sending OTP

function sendOTP(mobile, callback) {
  console.log(`ğŸ“© Sending OTP to ${mobile}...`);
  setTimeout(() => {
    callback("âœ… OTP Sent Successfully!");
  }, 2000);
}

sendOTP("9876543210", (msg) => {
  console.log(msg);
});


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Sending OTP and confirming delivery.

âš ï¸ Problem: Callback Hell (nested callbacks).

âœ… B. Promises

Promise = Object representing eventual success/failure of async task.

Example: Food Delivery Status

const orderFood = new Promise((resolve, reject) => {
  setTimeout(() => {
    const success = true;
    success ? resolve("ğŸ• Order Delivered!") : reject("âŒ Delivery Failed");
  }, 3000);
});

orderFood
  .then(msg => console.log(msg))       // success
  .catch(err => console.error(err))   // error
  .finally(() => console.log("Order Process Completed"));


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Zomato order status tracking.

âœ… C. Async / Await

Syntactic sugar over Promises â†’ makes code look synchronous.

Example: Weather App

async function getWeather(city) {
  try {
    const res = await fetch(
      `https://api.open-meteo.com/v1/forecast?latitude=18.52&longitude=73.85&current_weather=true`
    );
    const data = await res.json();
    console.log(`ğŸŒ¤ï¸ Weather in ${city}: ${data.current_weather.temperature}Â°C`);
  } catch (err) {
    console.error("Error fetching weather:", err);
  }
}

getWeather("Pune");


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Fetching live weather updates.

âœ… D. Event Loop & Concurrency Model

JS executes sync code first (Call Stack).

Async tasks (timers, fetch) go to Web APIs.

Callback Queue waits until stack is empty.

Event Loop pushes them back into execution.

Example: ATM Transaction

console.log("Insert Card");   // sync

setTimeout(() => console.log("Processing Transaction..."), 2000); // async

console.log("Enter PIN");     // sync


ğŸ“Œ Output:

Insert Card
Enter PIN
Processing Transaction...


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Banking transaction steps (UI works while waiting).

ğŸ”¹ 3. Real-World Async Examples
âœ… 1. Social Media Feed Loading
async function loadFeed() {
  console.log("Fetching posts...");
  const res = await fetch("https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/posts?_limit=3");
  const posts = await res.json();
  posts.forEach(post => console.log("ğŸ“°", post.title));
}
loadFeed();


ğŸ“Œ Instagram loads feed while still letting you scroll older posts.

âœ… 2. E-commerce Cart Update
function addToCart(product) {
  return new Promise(resolve => {
    setTimeout(() => resolve(`${product} added to cart ğŸ›’`), 1000);
  });
}

async function checkout() {
  console.log(await addToCart("Shoes"));
  console.log(await addToCart("Watch"));
  console.log("Proceed to payment ğŸ’³");
}
checkout();


ğŸ“Œ Flipkart/Amazon updating cart asynchronously.

âœ… 3. Ride Booking (Uber/Ola Simulation)
function findDriver() {
  return new Promise(resolve => {
    setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸš– Driver Found!"), 2000);
  });
}

  console.log("Searching for drivers...");
  let status = await findDriver();
  console.log(status);
}
bookRide();


ğŸ“Œ Uber shows "Searching for driverâ€¦" until one accepts.

ğŸ”¹ 4. Key Async Tools in JS

Callbacks â†’ old way (used in timers, event listeners).

Promises â†’ cleaner, avoids callback hell.

Async/Await â†’ most modern, best readability.

Event Loop â†’ explains how async tasks execute.

Fetch API â†’ network requests.

setTimeout / setInterval â†’ delayed/repeated tasks.

Web Workers â†’ heavy tasks in background.

âœ… Summary:
Asynchronous JavaScript allows apps like Swiggy, Uber, Instagram, YouTube to stay responsive while handling background tasks (API calls, notifications, timers).


===================================================================
Callbacks
===================================================================

ğŸ”¹ 1. What is a Callback?

ğŸ‘‰ A callback is a function passed as an argument to another function, and it is executed after the main function finishes its task.

JavaScript is asynchronous, so callbacks are often used to handle results of tasks like fetching data, reading files, or waiting for timers.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Basic Example of Callback
function greetUser(name, callback) {
  console.log("Hello " + name);
  callback(); // calling the callback function
}

function showMessage() {
  console.log("Welcome to our website!");
}

greetUser("Suraj", showMessage);


ğŸ“Œ Output:

Hello Suraj
Welcome to our website!

ğŸ”¹ 3. Real-World Examples of Callbacks
âœ… A. Login System Example
function loginUser(username, password, callback) {
  console.log("â³ Checking credentials...");
  setTimeout(() => {
    if (username === "admin" && password === "1234") {
      callback(true);
    } else {
      callback(false);
    }
  }, 2000);
}

loginUser("admin", "1234", (isLoggedIn) => {
  if (isLoggedIn) {
    console.log("âœ… Login Successful!");
  } else {
    console.log("âŒ Invalid Credentials");
  }
});


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Verifying user login in websites/apps.

âœ… B. Payment Processing Example
function processPayment(amount, callback) {
  console.log(`ğŸ’³ Processing payment of â‚¹${amount}...`);
  setTimeout(() => {
    callback("âœ… Payment Successful!");
  }, 3000);
}

processPayment(500, (message) => {
  console.log(message);
});


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: UPI/credit card payments (async confirmation).

âœ… C. Reading Data (Simulation)
function fetchData(callback) {
  console.log("â³ Fetching data from server...");
  setTimeout(() => {
    callback({ id: 1, name: "Suraj", role: "Developer" });
  }, 2000);
}

fetchData((user) => {
  console.log("User Data:", user);
});


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Getting user profile from database.

ğŸ”¹ 4. Callback Hell (Problem)

When callbacks are nested inside each other, the code becomes unreadable.

Example:

getUser(1, (user) => {
  getPosts(user.id, (posts) => {
    getComments(posts[0].id, (comments) => {
      console.log(comments);
    });
  });
});


ğŸ“Œ This is called Callback Hell â†’ solved by Promises and Async/Await.

ğŸ”¹ 5. Summary

Callback = Function passed into another function.

Used for async tasks (login, API calls, payments).

Problems: Callback Hell â†’ harder to read & debug.

Solution: Promises & Async/Await.

===================================================================
Promises (chaining)
===================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. What is a Promise?

ğŸ‘‰ A Promise in JavaScript represents a value that may be available now, later, or never.

Pending â†’ Initial state.

Fulfilled â†’ Operation completed successfully (resolve).

Rejected â†’ Operation failed (reject).

ğŸ”¹ 2. Promise Chaining

Promise chaining means calling multiple .then() methods in sequence, where the output of one promise is input to the next.

âœ… A. Basic Example of Chaining
new Promise((resolve, reject) => {
  resolve(10);
})
.then(num => {
  console.log("Step 1:", num);  
  return num * 2;   // pass to next then
})
.then(num => {
  console.log("Step 2:", num);
  return num + 5;
})
.then(num => {
  console.log("Step 3:", num);
});


ğŸ“Œ Output:

Step 1: 10
Step 2: 20
Step 3: 25

ğŸ”¹ 3. Real-World Examples of Promise Chaining
âœ… A. Online Shopping Checkout
function selectProduct() {
  return new Promise(resolve => {
    setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ›’ Product Selected"), 1000);
  });
}

function makePayment() {
  return new Promise(resolve => {
    setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ’³ Payment Successful"), 1500);
  });
}

function orderConfirmation() {
  return new Promise(resolve => {
    setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ“¦ Order Confirmed"), 1000);
  });
}

// Promise Chaining
selectProduct()
  .then(msg => {
    console.log(msg);
    return makePayment();
  })
  .then(msg => {
    console.log(msg);
    return orderConfirmation();
  })
  .then(msg => {
    console.log(msg);
    console.log("ğŸ‰ Shopping Completed!");
  });


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Flipkart/Amazon checkout flow.

âœ… B. Food Delivery App (Zomato/Swiggy)
function placeOrder() {
  return new Promise(resolve => {
    setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ” Order Placed"), 1000);
  });
}

function prepareFood() {
  return new Promise(resolve => {
    setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ‘¨â€ğŸ³ Food Prepared"), 2000);
  });
}

function deliverFood() {
  return new Promise(resolve => {
    setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸš´ Food Delivered"), 1500);
  });
}

// Chain the promises
placeOrder()
  .then(msg => {
    console.log(msg);
    return prepareFood();
  })
  .then(msg => {
    console.log(msg);
    return deliverFood();
  })
  .then(msg => {
    console.log(msg);
    console.log("âœ… Order Completed!");
  });


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Zomato order tracking.

âœ… C. Student Exam Result Flow
function registerStudent() {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ“ Student Registered"), 1000));
}

function giveExam() {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve("âœï¸ Exam Completed"), 2000));
}

function getResult() {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ“ Passed with 85%"), 1500));
}

// Chaining
registerStudent()
  .then(msg => {
    console.log(msg);
    return giveExam();
  })
  .then(msg => {
    console.log(msg);
    return getResult();
  })
  .then(msg => {
    console.log(msg);
  });


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Exam registration â†’ writing exam â†’ getting result.

ğŸ”¹ 4. Handling Errors in Chaining
new Promise((resolve, reject) => {
  reject("âŒ Payment Failed");
})
.then(msg => {
  console.log(msg);
  return "Proceeding...";
})
.catch(err => {
  console.error("Error:", err);
})
.finally(() => {
  console.log("â¡ï¸ Process Ended");
});


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Handling failed payments.

ğŸ”¹ 5. Summary

Promise chaining â†’ sequential execution of async tasks.

Avoids Callback Hell.

Best for processes like:

E-commerce checkout ğŸ›’

Food delivery ğŸ”

Booking apps (Uber, MakeMyTrip) ğŸš–

Payments ğŸ’³

===================================================================
Async/Await with try...catch
===================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. What is async/await?

async â†’ makes a function return a Promise.

await â†’ pauses execution until the Promise resolves/rejects.

try...catch â†’ handles errors (instead of .catch() in promises).

ğŸ‘‰ It makes asynchronous code look synchronous (cleaner than Promise chaining).

ğŸ”¹ 2. Basic Example
async function fetchNumber() {
  try {
    const result = await Promise.resolve(10);
    console.log("Number:", result);
  } catch (error) {
    console.error("Error:", error);
  }
}

fetchNumber();


ğŸ“Œ Output:

Number: 10

ğŸ”¹ 3. Real-World Examples
âœ… A. Weather App Example
async function getWeather(city) {
  try {
    const response = await fetch(
      "https://api.open-meteo.com/v1/forecast?latitude=18.52&longitude=73.85&current_weather=true"
    );
    if (!response.ok) throw new Error("ğŸŒ©ï¸ Failed to fetch weather");
    const data = await response.json();
    console.log(`ğŸŒ¤ï¸ Weather in ${city}: ${data.current_weather.temperature}Â°C`);
  } catch (error) {
    console.error("Error:", error.message);
  }
}

getWeather("Pune");


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Weather app fetching live API data.

âœ… B. Food Delivery (Zomato/Swiggy)
function placeOrder() {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ” Order Placed"), 1000));
}
function prepareFood() {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ‘¨â€ğŸ³ Food Prepared"), 2000));
}
function deliverFood() {
  return new Promise((resolve, reject) =>
    setTimeout(() => reject("âŒ Delivery Failed"), 1500)
  );
}

async function orderFood() {
  try {
    console.log(await placeOrder());
    console.log(await prepareFood());
    console.log(await deliverFood()); // will throw error
    console.log("âœ… Order Completed");
  } catch (error) {
    console.error("Error:", error);
  } finally {
    console.log("â¡ï¸ Order process finished");
  }
}

orderFood();


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Order placed & prepared, but delivery failed â†’ error handled gracefully.

âœ… C. Login System
function loginUser(username, password) {
  return new Promise((resolve, reject) => {
    setTimeout(() => {
      if (username === "admin" && password === "1234") {
        resolve("âœ… Login Successful");
      } else {
        reject("âŒ Invalid Credentials");
      }
    }, 1500);
  });
}

async function login() {
  try {
    const message = await loginUser("admin", "1234");
    console.log(message);
  } catch (error) {
    console.error("Login Error:", error);
  }
}

login();


ğŸ“Œ Real Use Case: Handling login authentication.

ğŸ”¹ 4. Why use try...catch with Async/Await?

âœ… Cleaner error handling than .catch()
âœ… Prevents app crashes
âœ… More readable, especially with multiple awaits

ğŸ”¹ 5. Summary

async/await simplifies asynchronous code.

try...catch is best for error handling in async functions.

Real uses: APIs (weather, payments, login, delivery tracking).

===================================================================
Parallel vs Sequential Execution
===================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. Why Important?

JavaScript is single-threaded, but with async features (Promises, async/await, Web APIs) it can:

Run tasks sequentially (one after another).

Run tasks in parallel (start multiple tasks at the same time, wait for all to complete).

ğŸ”¹ 2. Sequential Execution (One by One)

ğŸ‘‰ Each task waits for the previous task to finish before starting the next.

Example: Food Delivery Process

function placeOrder() {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ” Order Placed"), 1000));
}
function prepareFood() {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ‘¨â€ğŸ³ Food Prepared"), 2000));
}
function deliverFood() {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸš´ Food Delivered"), 1500));
}

async function sequentialOrder() {
  console.time("Sequential");
  console.log(await placeOrder());   // waits 1s
  console.log(await prepareFood());  // waits 2s
  console.log(await deliverFood());  // waits 1.5s
  console.timeEnd("Sequential");
}

sequentialOrder();


ğŸ“Œ Output:

ğŸ” Order Placed
ğŸ‘¨â€ğŸ³ Food Prepared
ğŸš´ Food Delivered
Sequential: ~4.5s


âœ… Real Use Case: Must happen in order â†’ You canâ€™t deliver food before itâ€™s prepared.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Parallel Execution (At the Same Time)

ğŸ‘‰ Multiple tasks start together and run independently.
ğŸ‘‰ Best when tasks donâ€™t depend on each other.

Example: Fetching User Data & Notifications Together

function fetchUser() {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ‘¤ User Data Fetched"), 2000));
}
function fetchNotifications() {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ”” Notifications Fetched"), 2000));
}

async function parallelExecution() {
  console.time("Parallel");
  const [user, notifications] = await Promise.all([fetchUser(), fetchNotifications()]);
  console.log(user);
  console.log(notifications);
  console.timeEnd("Parallel");
}

parallelExecution();


ğŸ“Œ Output:

ğŸ‘¤ User Data Fetched
ğŸ”” Notifications Fetched
Parallel: ~2s


âœ… Real Use Case: Social media app loads profile & notifications simultaneously.

ğŸ”¹ 4. Comparison
Feature	Sequential	Parallel
Execution	One after another	Multiple at once
Speed	Slower (waits for each task)	Faster (tasks overlap)
Use Case	Steps depend on each other (food delivery, checkout flow)	Independent tasks (fetching user + posts, loading ads + images)
Code	Multiple await (one by one)	Promise.all / Promise.allSettled
ğŸ”¹ 5. Extra Example: E-commerce Checkout

ğŸ‘‰ Sequential:

Add to cart â†’

Make payment â†’

Generate invoice

ğŸ‘‰ Parallel:

Fetch recommended products

Fetch user profile

Fetch delivery address

All at the same time.

ğŸ”¹ 6. Summary

Sequential Execution: Tasks depend on each other â†’ slower but necessary.

Parallel Execution: Independent tasks â†’ faster (use Promise.all).

Real-world:

Sequential: Food delivery, payments, booking tickets.

Parallel: Loading feeds, notifications, profile data together.

ğŸ‘‰ Do you want me to also cover Promise.allSettled, Promise.any, and Promise.race with real-world use cases (since theyâ€™re part of handling parallel execution)?

===================================================================
Promise.all, Promise.race, Promise.any
===================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. Promise.all()

ğŸ‘‰ Waits for all promises to resolve.
ğŸ‘‰ If one fails, the whole thing fails.

Example: Loading Dashboard Data

function fetchUser() {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ‘¤ User Data"), 1000));
}
function fetchOrders() {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ“¦ Orders Data"), 2000));
}
function fetchNotifications() {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ”” Notifications"), 1500));
}

async function loadDashboard() {
  try {
    const results = await Promise.all([fetchUser(), fetchOrders(), fetchNotifications()]);
    console.log(results); // [ 'ğŸ‘¤ User Data', 'ğŸ“¦ Orders Data', 'ğŸ”” Notifications' ]
  } catch (error) {
    console.error("âŒ Failed to load dashboard:", error);
  }
}

loadDashboard();


ğŸ“Œ Real Case:
Social media app loads profile + posts + notifications together.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Promise.race()

ğŸ‘‰ Returns the first promise (resolved or rejected).
ğŸ‘‰ Useful for timeouts.

Example: Video Streaming (First CDN Wins)

function fetchFromCDN1() {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸ“¡ CDN1 Response"), 2000));
}
function fetchFromCDN2() {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve("ğŸš€ CDN2 Response"), 1000));
}

async function loadVideo() {
  try {
    const fastest = await Promise.race([fetchFromCDN1(), fetchFromCDN2()]);
    console.log("âœ… Using:", fastest);
  } catch (error) {
    console.error("âŒ Error:", error);
  }
}

loadVideo();


ğŸ“Œ Real Case:
Video streaming chooses fastest server for buffering.

ğŸ”¹ 3. Promise.any()

ğŸ‘‰ Returns the first fulfilled promise (ignores rejections).
ğŸ‘‰ If all fail, it throws AggregateError.

Example: Login with Multiple Accounts (Google, Facebook, Email)

function loginWithGoogle() {
  return new Promise((_, reject) => setTimeout(() => reject("âŒ Google failed"), 1000));
}
function loginWithFacebook() {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve("âœ… Facebook Login"), 2000));
}
function loginWithEmail() {
  return new Promise((_, reject) => setTimeout(() => reject("âŒ Email failed"), 500));
}

async function login() {
  try {
    const result = await Promise.any([loginWithGoogle(), loginWithFacebook(), loginWithEmail()]);
    console.log("Logged in via:", result);
  } catch (error) {
    console.error("All logins failed:", error);
  }
}

login();


ğŸ“Œ Real Case:
User tries to login â†’ any successful method works.

ğŸ”¹ 4. Quick Comparison
Method	Resolves When	Fails When	Real Case
Promise.all	All promises succeed	One fails	Dashboard loads profile + notifications + orders
Promise.race	First promise settles (resolve/reject)	No failure rule (depends on first)	CDN server selection
Promise.any	First promise resolves (ignores rejects)	All fail	Login with multiple providers

âœ… Summary:

Use Promise.all â†’ when everything must succeed.

Use Promise.race â†’ when you only need the fastest result.

Use Promise.any â†’ when you just need one success, no matter which.

===================================================================
Event Bubbling vs Capturing
===================================================================
ğŸ”¹ Event Flow in JavaScript

When an event happens (like a click), the browser follows a 3-phase event flow:

Capturing Phase â†’ Event goes from root â†’ target (document â†’ html â†’ body â†’ parent â†’ child).

Target Phase â†’ Event happens on the target element.

Bubbling Phase â†’ Event goes from target â†’ root (child â†’ parent â†’ body â†’ html â†’ document).

ğŸ”¹ Event Bubbling (Default)

ğŸ‘‰ Event starts from the target element and bubbles up to its ancestors.
ğŸ‘‰ This is the default behavior in JavaScript.

Example: Click inside a shopping cart item

<div id="cart">
  <div id="item">ğŸ›’ Item</div>
</div>

<script>
document.getElementById("cart").addEventListener("click", () => {
  console.log("ğŸ› Cart clicked!");
});

document.getElementById("item").addEventListener("click", () => {
  console.log("âœ… Item clicked!");
});
</script>


ğŸ‘‰ If you click on item, youâ€™ll see:

âœ… Item clicked!
ğŸ› Cart clicked!


ğŸ“Œ Real Case:
Clicking a button inside a form triggers both the button event and the formâ€™s event.

ğŸ”¹ Event Capturing (Trickling)

ğŸ‘‰ Event starts from the root (document) and goes down to the target.
ğŸ‘‰ To enable capturing, set the third argument of addEventListener to true.

Example: Website Navigation Menu

<div id="menu">
  <button id="home">ğŸ  Home</button>
</div>

<script>
document.getElementById("menu").addEventListener("click", () => {
  console.log("ğŸ“‚ Menu captured!");
}, true); // ğŸ‘ˆ capturing enabled

document.getElementById("home").addEventListener("click", () => {
  console.log("ğŸ  Home clicked!");
});
</script>


ğŸ‘‰ If you click on home, youâ€™ll see:

ğŸ“‚ Menu captured!
ğŸ  Home clicked!


ğŸ“Œ Real Case:
Useful when you want parent elements to handle the event first (e.g., navigation intercept before button action).

ğŸ”¹ Stopping Event Propagation

Sometimes you donâ€™t want the event to bubble up or capture further.
ğŸ‘‰ Use event.stopPropagation().

Example: Prevent modal closing when clicking inside content

<div id="modal">
  <div id="content">ğŸ“¦ Modal Content (Click me)</div>
</div>

<script>
document.getElementById("modal").addEventListener("click", () => {
  console.log("âŒ Modal closed!");
});

document.getElementById("content").addEventListener("click", (event) => {
  event.stopPropagation(); // prevent bubbling
  console.log("âœ… Content clicked, modal stays open!");
});
</script>

ğŸ”¹ Key Differences: Bubbling vs Capturing
Feature	Bubbling	Capturing
Flow	Target â†’ Parent â†’ Root	Root â†’ Parent â†’ Target
Default	âœ… Yes	âŒ No (needs {capture: true})
Usage	Most common, default in browsers	Rare, but useful for interception
Example	Button click inside a form	Navigation menu handling before button click

âœ… Summary:

Event Bubbling (default): Child â†’ Parent â†’ Document.

Event Capturing: Document â†’ Parent â†’ Child.

Use stopPropagation() if you want to stop the event flow.

Debouncing & Throttling
===================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. Why Debouncing & Throttling?

When you attach events like scroll, resize, keyup, search, button click, they can fire hundreds of times per second â†’ leading to performance issues.

ğŸ‘‰ Solution: Use Debouncing or Throttling to limit event execution.

ğŸ”¹ 2. Debouncing

ğŸ‘‰ Ensures the function is executed only after a delay of inactivity.
ğŸ‘‰ If the event is triggered again before the delay, the timer resets.

âœ… Best for:

Search bar suggestions

Window resizing

Form validation

Example: Search Suggestion (Google-like Search Box)

<input type="text" id="search" placeholder="Search..." />

<script>
function fetchSuggestions(query) {
  console.log("ğŸ” Fetching suggestions for:", query);
}

// Debounce function
function debounce(fn, delay) {
  let timer;
  return function (...args) {
    clearTimeout(timer);
    timer = setTimeout(() => fn.apply(this, args), delay);
  };
}

const debouncedSearch = debounce(fetchSuggestions, 500);

document.getElementById("search").addEventListener("input", (e) => {
  debouncedSearch(e.target.value);
});
</script>


ğŸ“Œ Real Case:
When typing javascript, API call happens only after the user stops typing (500ms).

ğŸ”¹ 3. Throttling

ğŸ‘‰ Ensures the function is executed at most once in a given interval, no matter how many times the event is triggered.

âœ… Best for:

Scroll position tracking

Button click (prevent spamming)

Game controls / animations

Example: Scroll Event (Load More Products)

<script>
function loadProducts() {
  console.log("ğŸ“¦ Loading more products at", new Date().toLocaleTimeString());
}

// Throttle function
function throttle(fn, limit) {
  let inThrottle;
  return function (...args) {
    if (!inThrottle) {
      fn.apply(this, args);
      inThrottle = true;
      setTimeout(() => inThrottle = false, limit);
    }
  };
}

const throttledScroll = throttle(loadProducts, 2000);

window.addEventListener("scroll", throttledScroll);
</script>


ğŸ“Œ Real Case:
When scrolling a shopping site, loadProducts is called only once every 2 seconds.

ğŸ”¹ 4. Comparison Table
Feature	Debouncing	Throttling
Execution	After a pause in activity	At fixed intervals
Use Case	Search bar, validation	Scroll, resize, click spam
Example	Typing in search box	Infinite scroll / API polling
Code Behavior	Delays function until user stops	Limits execution to once per X ms
ğŸ”¹ 5. Real-world Scenarios

âœ… Debounce:

Google search autocomplete

Validating email/password in forms

Window resize event (only calculate layout after resize stops)

âœ… Throttle:

Infinite scroll (load more content)

Prevent double form submission

Tracking mouse position while dragging

âœ… Summary:

Debouncing: Waits for user to stop doing something before executing.

Throttling: Executes function at regular intervals while the event keeps firing.

Modules (ESM vs CommonJS)
===================================================================
ğŸ”¹ 1. Why Modules?

In real projects, code becomes large â†’ we split it into reusable files (modules).

Each module can export variables, functions, or classes and import them in other files.

ğŸ”¹ 2. CommonJS (CJS)

ğŸ‘‰ Used in Node.js (default before ES6).
ğŸ‘‰ Uses require() and module.exports.
ğŸ‘‰ Synchronous (loads modules at runtime).

Example: Node.js Shopping Cart (CJS)

ğŸ“ cart.js

function addItem(item) {
  console.log(`âœ… Added ${item} to cart`);
}

function removeItem(item) {
  console.log(`âŒ Removed ${item} from cart`);
}

module.exports = { addItem, removeItem }; // export functions


ğŸ“ app.js

const cart = require('./cart'); // import module

cart.addItem("Laptop");
cart.removeItem("Phone");


ğŸ“Œ Output:

âœ… Added Laptop to cart
âŒ Removed Phone from cart

ğŸ”¹ 3. ES Modules (ESM)

ğŸ‘‰ Introduced in ES6 (2015).
ğŸ‘‰ Uses import and export.
ğŸ‘‰ Asynchronous (better for browsers).
ğŸ‘‰ Native support in modern Node.js ("type": "module" in package.json).

Example: E-commerce Checkout (ESM)

ğŸ“ checkout.js

export function pay(amount) {
  console.log(`ğŸ’³ Payment of $${amount} successful!`);
}

export const TAX = 0.18;

export default function checkout(items) {
  console.log("ğŸ› Checking out:", items.join(", "));
}


ğŸ“ app.js

import checkout, { pay, TAX } from './checkout.js';

checkout(["Shoes", "Shirt"]);
pay(100 * (1 + TAX));


ğŸ“Œ Output:

ğŸ› Checking out: Shoes, Shirt
ğŸ’³ Payment of $118 successful!

ğŸ”¹ 4. Key Differences (CJS vs ESM)
Feature	CommonJS (CJS)	ES Modules (ESM)
Syntax	require() / module.exports	import / export
Execution	Synchronous (runtime)	Asynchronous (compile-time)
Usage	Default in Node.js (older)	Standard in modern JS (Node & Browsers)
Exports	module.exports = {}	export / export default
Imports	const x = require('x')	import x from 'x'
Browser Support	âŒ (needs bundler)	âœ… Native in modern browsers
Tree-shaking	âŒ No	âœ… Yes (remove unused code in bundlers)
ğŸ”¹ 5. Real-World Usage

CommonJS (CJS): Still widely used in old Node.js projects (require('express')).

ESM: Modern apps (React, Angular, Vue, Next.js, Deno, modern Node).

âœ… Summary:

Use CJS if maintaining legacy Node.js apps.

Use ESM for modern web apps (default in React, Angular, Vue, etc.).
