Git & GitHub
==================================================================================
ğŸ”¹ What is Git?

A version control system (VCS) used to track changes in source code.

Helps developers collaborate, revert changes, and maintain history.

Works locally on your machine.

ğŸ”¹ What is GitHub?

A cloud platform that hosts Git repositories.

Adds collaboration features â†’ issues, pull requests, actions (CI/CD).

Think of it as Google Drive for code (but with version control).

ğŸ”¹ Why Git & GitHub for JavaScript Developers?

Manage project versions (React, Node.js, Angular apps).

Work in teams (feature branches, pull requests).

Showcase projects (portfolio on GitHub).

Integrate with tools (CI/CD, deployment).

ğŸ”¹ Basic Git Workflow
# Initialize git in a project
git init

# Stage files
git add .

# Commit changes
git commit -m "Initial commit"

# Connect to GitHub remote
git remote add origin https://github.com/username/repo.git

# Push to GitHub
git push -u origin main

ğŸ”¹ Example: JavaScript Project with Git & GitHub

Create a Node.js project:

mkdir my-app
cd my-app
npm init -y


Create a file index.js:

console.log("Hello Git & GitHub with JavaScript!");


Initialize Git and commit:

git init
git add .
git commit -m "First commit - Hello JS"


Push to GitHub:

git remote add origin https://github.com/your-username/my-app.git
git push -u origin main


Now your JavaScript project is live on GitHub ğŸ‰

ğŸ”¹ Real-World Use Case

Team working on a React app:

Each developer works on a separate branch (feature/login, feature/cart).

Merge changes via pull requests on GitHub.

GitHub Actions run automated tests before merging.

ğŸ”‘ Summary

Git = tool for version control (local).

GitHub = hosting + collaboration platform (remote).

Essential for JavaScript developers to manage, share, and collaborate on code.

ğŸ‘‰ Do you want me to also prepare a list of must-know Git commands for JavaScript developers (with real examples like pushing a React/Node project)?

You said:
Git & GitHub brief with examples and all commands list out 
ChatGPT said:

Perfect ğŸ‘ Letâ€™s cover Git & GitHub in a brief way, with real-world examples, and then list out the most important commands for JavaScript developers.

ğŸ”¹ Git (Brief)

Definition: Git is a distributed version control system.

Purpose: Track changes in your code, collaborate with others, and maintain project history.

Scope: Works on your local machine.

âœ… Example:
You make changes to a Node.js app â†’ Git lets you save versions (commits), so you can roll back if something breaks.

ğŸ”¹ GitHub (Brief)

Definition: GitHub is a cloud platform to host Git repositories.

Purpose: Enables collaboration (pull requests, issues, reviews).

Scope: Works online as a remote repository.

âœ… Example:
You push your React project to GitHub â†’ your team can clone it, make branches, and collaborate.

ğŸ”¹ Basic Git Workflow (Example)
# 1. Initialize Git in project
git init  

# 2. Stage files
git add .  

# 3. Commit changes
git commit -m "First commit - Hello JS"  

# 4. Link GitHub repo
git remote add origin https://github.com/username/my-app.git  

# 5. Push to GitHub
git push -u origin main

ğŸ”¹ Real-World Example (JavaScript Project with Git & GitHub)

Create a Node.js project:

mkdir my-app && cd my-app
npm init -y
echo "console.log('Hello GitHub!')" > index.js


Initialize Git & Commit:

git init
git add .
git commit -m "Initial commit - JS app"


Push to GitHub:

git remote add origin https://github.com/your-username/my-app.git
git push -u origin main


Now your Node.js project is live on GitHub ğŸ‰

ğŸ”¹ Must-Know Git Commands (With Examples)
ğŸŸ¢ Setup
git config --global user.name "Your Name"
git config --global user.email "you@example.com"

ğŸŸ¢ Repository Basics
git init                   # Initialize a new repo
git clone URL              # Clone from GitHub
git status                 # Check status of files
git log                    # Show commit history

ğŸŸ¢ Staging & Committing
git add file.js            # Stage one file
git add .                  # Stage all files
git commit -m "Message"    # Commit with a message
git commit -am "Message"   # Add + commit tracked files

ğŸŸ¢ Branches
git branch                 # List branches
git branch new-feature     # Create branch
git checkout new-feature   # Switch branch
git checkout -b login      # Create + switch branch
git merge new-feature      # Merge branch into current
git branch -d new-feature  # Delete branch

ğŸŸ¢ Remote Repositories (GitHub)
git remote -v              # Show remotes
git remote add origin URL  # Add GitHub repo
git push -u origin main    # Push branch
git pull origin main       # Pull latest changes
git fetch                  # Fetch without merging
git clone URL              # Clone repo from GitHub

ğŸŸ¢ Undo Changes
git reset file.js          # Unstage a file
git checkout -- file.js    # Discard changes
git reset --hard HEAD      # Reset to last commit
git revert <commit>        # Undo a commit (safe way)

ğŸŸ¢ Collaboration
git pull origin main       # Get changes from GitHub
git push origin main       # Push changes
git push origin branch     # Push feature branch
git merge branch           # Merge branch into main

ğŸŸ¢ Stash (Temporary Save)
git stash                  # Save uncommitted changes
git stash apply            # Reapply stashed changes
git stash drop             # Delete stash

ğŸ”‘ Summary

Git = local version control tool.

GitHub = online collaboration & hosting platform.

Workflow = init â†’ add â†’ commit â†’ push â†’ pull â†’ merge.

Commands = setup, branch, commit, push, pull, stash, reset.

ESLint & Prettier
==================================================================================
ESLint & Prettier in JavaScript
ğŸ”¹ ESLint

ESLint is a static code analysis tool for identifying and fixing problems in JavaScript/TypeScript code.

Ensures consistent code quality.

Catches syntax errors, unused variables, bad practices.

Allows custom rules.

ğŸ‘‰ Key Features:

Enforces coding standards.

Detects potential bugs (like unused imports, undefined variables).

Supports plugins (React, TypeScript, Node).

âœ… Real Example:

# Install ESLint
npm install eslint --save-dev

# Initialize ESLint
npx eslint --init

// bad.js
let name = "Suraj"
console.log("Hello")


Run ESLint:

npx eslint bad.js


ğŸ“Œ Output:

2:7  warning  'name' is assigned a value but never used  no-unused-vars

ğŸ”¹ Prettier

Prettier is a code formatter that automatically formats your code for readability.

Opinionated (has strict formatting rules).

Works with JS, TS, HTML, CSS, JSON, Markdown, etc.

Ensures consistent style across the team.

ğŸ‘‰ Key Features:

Removes code style debates (spaces vs tabs, single vs double quotes).

Formats automatically on save.

Works with ESLint (together).

âœ… Real Example:

# Install Prettier
npm install --save-dev prettier

// ugly.js
function greet(name){console.log("Hello, "+name);}
greet("Suraj")


Run Prettier:

npx prettier --write ugly.js


ğŸ“Œ Output (formatted code):

function greet(name) {
  console.log("Hello, " + name);
}
greet("Suraj");

ğŸ”¹ Using ESLint + Prettier Together

Install plugin:

npm install --save-dev eslint-config-prettier eslint-plugin-prettier


Update .eslintrc.json:

{
  "extends": ["eslint:recommended", "plugin:prettier/recommended"],
  "plugins": ["prettier"],
  "rules": {
    "prettier/prettier": "error"
  }
}


Now ESLint will also check Prettier formatting issues.

âœ… When to Use What?

ESLint â†’ Finds problems (quality, best practices).

Prettier â†’ Fixes formatting (style consistency).

Both Together â†’ Clean + consistent + bug-free code.

ğŸ” Debugging with Chrome DevTools

Chrome DevTools is a built-in set of developer tools in Google Chrome that helps debug, profile, and optimize JavaScript, CSS, and HTML in real time.

You can open it with:

Right-click â†’ Inspect

Or press F12 / Ctrl+Shift+I (Windows) / Cmd+Opt+I (Mac)

âš¡ Key Features for JavaScript Debugging
1. Console

Logs values with console.log(), console.error(), console.warn().

Inspect objects and arrays.

Run JavaScript snippets interactively.

âœ… Example:

const user = { name: "Suraj", age: 25 };

2. Sources Panel

View all loaded JavaScript files.

âœ… Real Example:

  let total = price * qty;
  return total;

Line-of-code Breakpoint â†’ Pause at a specific line.

4. Watch Expressions

âœ… Example:

=============================
5. Call Stack
=============================
Shows the order of function calls.

Helps identify where an error originates.

âœ… Example:

ğŸ‘‰ Call Stack will show step1 â†’ step2 â†’ step3 â†’ Error.

6. Network Panel

âœ… Example:

  .then(res => res.json())
  .then(data => console.log(data));


ğŸ‘‰ Open Network tab â†’ Check users API request and response.

7. Performance & Memory

Record CPU usage & JS execution time.

Detect memory leaks.

âœ… Example: Detect unnecessary loops:

  // Performance issue
}

ğŸ”‘ Real-Life Debugging Workflow

Set breakpoints in suspicious code.

Use Network tab for API debugging.

Use Console for quick testing.

CI/CD with JavaScript projects
==================================================================================
âš¡ CI/CD (Continuous Integration & Continuous Deployment) for JavaScript Projects

CI/CD automates building, testing, and deploying your JavaScript (Node.js, React, Angular, Vue, etc.) apps.

ğŸ”¹ What is CI/CD?

CI (Continuous Integration) â†’ Every time you push code, your project is built + tested automatically.

CD (Continuous Deployment/Delivery) â†’ After passing tests, the code is deployed automatically to staging/production.

ğŸ”¹ CI/CD Pipeline for JavaScript

Version Control â†’ Push code to GitHub/GitLab/Bitbucket.

Build â†’ Install dependencies (npm install / yarn install).

Lint & Test â†’ Run ESLint + Jest/Mocha/Cypress tests.

Bundle/Build â†’ (React/Angular/Vue â†’ npm run build).

Deploy â†’ Push to server, cloud (AWS, Netlify, Vercel, Heroku, etc.).

ğŸ”¹ Example CI/CD Setup (GitHub Actions for Node.js Project)

ğŸ“‚ Project Structure:

my-app/
 â”£ src/
 â”£ tests/
 â”£ package.json
 â”£ .eslintrc.json
 â”£ .github/workflows/ci.yml


ğŸ‘‰ ci.yml

name: CI/CD Pipeline

on:
  push:
    branches:
      - main
  pull_request:
    branches:
      - main

jobs:
  build-test-deploy:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest

    steps:
      # 1ï¸âƒ£ Checkout repo
      - name: Checkout Code
        uses: actions/checkout@v3

      # 2ï¸âƒ£ Setup Node.js
      - name: Setup Node.js
        uses: actions/setup-node@v3
        with:
          node-version: 18

      # 3ï¸âƒ£ Install dependencies
      - name: Install Dependencies
        run: npm install

      # 4ï¸âƒ£ Run Linter
      - name: Run ESLint
        run: npm run lint

      # 5ï¸âƒ£ Run Tests
      - name: Run Tests
        run: npm test

      # 6ï¸âƒ£ Build Project (if frontend project)
      - name: Build App
        run: npm run build

      # 7ï¸âƒ£ Deploy (example: Netlify CLI)
      - name: Deploy to Netlify
        run: npm run deploy
        env:
          NETLIFY_AUTH_TOKEN: ${{ secrets.NETLIFY_AUTH_TOKEN }}
          NETLIFY_SITE_ID: ${{ secrets.NETLIFY_SITE_ID }}

ğŸ”¹ Real Example Workflows
âœ… Node.js API (Express)

Run tests with Jest before deploying.

Deploy to Heroku / AWS / Render after passing tests.

âœ… React / Angular / Vue App

Run npm run build.

Deploy static files to Netlify / Vercel / GitHub Pages.

ğŸ”¹ Benefits

Fewer bugs (tests catch issues early).

Faster delivery (automation saves time).

Consistency (same build every time).

Confidence (every commit is tested & deployable).

ğŸ”¹ Common CI/CD Tools for JS

âœ… In short:

==================================================================================
Performance Optimization
==================================================================================
âš¡ Performance Optimization in JavaScript

Optimizing performance means making JavaScript apps faster, smoother, and more efficient in both frontend (React/Angular/Vue) and backend (Node.js).

ğŸ”¹ 1. Minimize DOM Manipulation

Direct DOM operations are expensive.

âœ… Example (bad):

for (let i = 0; i < 1000; i++) {
  let div = document.createElement("div");
  div.textContent = i;
  document.body.appendChild(div); // expensive (1000 reflows)
}


âœ… Optimized:

let fragment = document.createDocumentFragment();
for (let i = 0; i < 1000; i++) {
  let div = document.createElement("div");
  div.textContent = i;
  fragment.appendChild(div);
}
document.body.appendChild(fragment); // single reflow

ğŸ”¹ 2. Use Debouncing & Throttling

For events like scroll, resize, keyup.

âœ… Example (Debounce for search input):

function debounce(fn, delay) {
  let timer;
  return (...args) => {
    clearTimeout(timer);
    timer = setTimeout(() => fn(...args), delay);
  };
}

document.getElementById("search").addEventListener(
  "input",
  debounce((e) => {
    console.log("Searching for:", e.target.value);
  }, 500)
);

ğŸ”¹ 3. Lazy Loading

Load images/components only when needed.

âœ… Example:

<img src="placeholder.jpg" data-src="big-image.jpg" class="lazy" />

const imgs = document.querySelectorAll("img.lazy");
const observer = new IntersectionObserver((entries) => {
  entries.forEach((entry) => {
    if (entry.isIntersecting) {
      entry.target.src = entry.target.dataset.src;
      observer.unobserve(entry.target);
    }
  });
});
imgs.forEach((img) => observer.observe(img));

ğŸ”¹ 4. Code Splitting & Tree Shaking

Code splitting â†’ Load only whatâ€™s needed. (Webpack, Rollup, Vite)

Tree shaking â†’ Remove unused code.

âœ… Example in React (dynamic import):

const Profile = React.lazy(() => import("./Profile"));

ğŸ”¹ 5. Use Efficient Data Structures

Choose the right structure for lookups/search.

âœ… Example:
Instead of searching in an array:

let users = ["alice", "bob", "charlie"];
console.log(users.includes("bob")); // O(n)


Use a Set:

let userSet = new Set(["alice", "bob", "charlie"]);
console.log(userSet.has("bob")); // O(1)

ğŸ”¹ 6. Memoization / Caching

Avoid recalculating expensive results.

âœ… Example:

function expensive(n) {
  console.log("Computing...");
  return n * n;
}

const memo = {};
function memoizedExpensive(n) {
  if (memo[n]) return memo[n];
  memo[n] = expensive(n);
  return memo[n];
}

console.log(memoizedExpensive(5)); // Computing... 25
console.log(memoizedExpensive(5)); // Cached 25

ğŸ”¹ 7. Web Workers

Offload heavy tasks to background threads.

âœ… Example:

// worker.js
self.onmessage = function (e) {
  let sum = 0;
  for (let i = 0; i < 1e9; i++) sum += i;
  self.postMessage(sum);
};

// main.js
const worker = new Worker("worker.js");
worker.onmessage = (e) => console.log("Result:", e.data);

ğŸ”¹ 8. Reduce Network Calls

Use caching (localStorage, sessionStorage, IndexedDB).

Combine API requests when possible.

âœ… Example:

// Cache user data in localStorage
if (localStorage.getItem("users")) {
  console.log(JSON.parse(localStorage.getItem("users")));
} else {
  fetch("/api/users")
    .then((res) => res.json())
    .then((data) => localStorage.setItem("users", JSON.stringify(data)));
}

ğŸ”¹ 9. Optimize Loops

Avoid unnecessary iterations.

âœ… Example (bad):

for (let i = 0; i < arr.length; i++) { ... } // recalculates length each time


âœ… Optimized:

for (let i = 0, len = arr.length; i < len; i++) { ... }

ğŸ”¹ 10. Backend (Node.js) Optimizations

Use Cluster Mode (pm2 or Node.js cluster).

Use Streams instead of loading big files into memory.

Enable GZIP compression in Express.

Optimize queries with indexing (MongoDB, SQL).

âœ… Summary

Frontend â†’ DOM optimization, lazy loading, debouncing, memoization.

Code â†’ Tree shaking, code splitting, efficient data structures.

Backend â†’ Clustering, caching, database optimization.

19. Advanced & Expert Level
==================================================================================
Event-Driven Architecture
==================================================================================
What is Event-Driven Architecture?

Event-Driven Architecture is a software design pattern where components communicate by producing and consuming events instead of directly calling each other.

Event = A change in state or an action that occurred (e.g., "UserRegistered", "OrderPlaced", "PaymentFailed").

Components are loosely coupled â†’ they donâ€™t call each other directly, they just listen for and react to events.

ğŸ”¹ Key Components

Event Producer (Publisher)

Generates events when something happens.

Example: A shopping appâ€™s checkout service emits an event "OrderPlaced".

Event Broker (Message Bus / Queue)

Transports events between producers and consumers.

Examples: Kafka, RabbitMQ, AWS SNS/SQS, Azure Event Hub.

Event Consumer (Subscriber)

Listens for specific events and reacts to them.

Example: Inventory service listens to "OrderPlaced" and updates stock.

ğŸ”¹ Real-Life Example (E-Commerce)

Imagine Amazon checkout flow:

Producer: User places an order â†’ OrderPlaced event emitted.

Consumers:

ğŸ“¦ Inventory Service â†’ reduces stock.

ğŸ’³ Payment Service â†’ charges the customer.

ğŸ“§ Notification Service â†’ sends confirmation email.

ğŸ‘‰ All services work independently, only listening to events they care about.

ğŸ”¹ Benefits

âœ… Loose coupling â†’ services donâ€™t depend tightly on each other.
âœ… Scalability â†’ can add new consumers without touching existing code.
âœ… Asynchronous â†’ better performance & resilience.

ğŸ”¹ Simple JavaScript Example

Letâ€™s simulate an event-driven system using Node.js EventEmitter:

const EventEmitter = require('events');
const eventBus = new EventEmitter();

// Consumers
eventBus.on('orderPlaced', (orderId) => {
  console.log(`ğŸ“¦ Inventory updated for Order: ${orderId}`);
});

eventBus.on('orderPlaced', (orderId) => {
  console.log(`ğŸ’³ Payment processed for Order: ${orderId}`);
});

eventBus.on('orderPlaced', (orderId) => {
  console.log(`ğŸ“§ Email sent for Order: ${orderId}`);
});

// Producer
function placeOrder(orderId) {
  console.log(`ğŸ›’ Order placed: ${orderId}`);
  eventBus.emit('orderPlaced', orderId);
}

// Trigger
placeOrder(101);


ğŸ‘‰ Output:

ğŸ›’ Order placed: 101
ğŸ“¦ Inventory updated for Order: 101
ğŸ’³ Payment processed for Order: 101
ğŸ“§ Email sent for Order: 101

ğŸ”¹ Where is EDA Used?

E-commerce apps (orders, payments, stock).

IoT systems (sensors emitting data).

Microservices communication.

Real-time apps (chat apps, live dashboards).

âš¡ In short:
Event-Driven Architecture = Producers emit events â†’ Broker transports events â†’ Consumers react to events. It makes apps scalable, flexible, and loosely coupled.

==================================================================================
WebSockets & Real-time Apps
==================================================================================
ğŸ”¹ What are WebSockets?

WebSockets are a communication protocol that provides full-duplex, persistent connections between a client (e.g., browser) and a server.

ğŸ‘‰ Unlike HTTP (request-response), WebSockets allow both client and server to send data anytime without repeatedly opening new connections.

ğŸ”¹ How WebSockets Work

Handshake â†’ Client requests an upgrade from HTTP to WebSocket.

Open Connection â†’ A persistent TCP connection is created.

Real-time Communication â†’ Both server and client can push messages.

Close Connection â†’ Either side can close when done.

ğŸ”¹ Why WebSockets?

ğŸŒ Real-time communication

âš¡ Low latency (no need for polling the server repeatedly)

ğŸ”„ Bi-directional (both client & server can initiate messages)

ğŸ”¹ Real-World Use Cases

Chat apps (WhatsApp Web, Slack, Messenger)

Online gaming (multiplayer updates in real time)

Stock trading apps (live price updates)

Live dashboards (monitoring IoT, analytics, crypto prices)

Collaboration tools (Google Docs, Figma live editing)

ğŸ”¹ Simple WebSocket Example (Node.js + Browser)
Server (Node.js with ws)
// Install: npm install ws
const WebSocket = require('ws');
const server = new WebSocket.Server({ port: 8080 });

server.on('connection', (socket) => {
  console.log('ğŸ”— Client connected');

  // Send message to client
  socket.send('Welcome to WebSocket server!');

  // Listen for messages
  socket.on('message', (message) => {
    console.log(`ğŸ“© Received: ${message}`);
    socket.send(`Echo: ${message}`);
  });

  // Handle disconnect
  socket.on('close', () => console.log('âŒ Client disconnected'));
});

Client (Browser)
<script>
  const socket = new WebSocket('ws://localhost:8080');

  socket.onopen = () => {
    console.log('Connected to server');
    socket.send('Hello from client ğŸ‘‹');
  };

  socket.onmessage = (event) => {
    console.log('ğŸ“¢ Server says:', event.data);
  };

  socket.onclose = () => console.log('Disconnected');
</script>


ğŸ‘‰ Output:

Connected to server
ğŸ“¢ Server says: Welcome to WebSocket server!
ğŸ“¢ Server says: Echo: Hello from client ğŸ‘‹

ğŸ”¹ Alternatives & Enhancements

Socket.IO (Node.js) â†’ adds fallbacks, rooms, auto-reconnect.

SignalR (ASP.NET) â†’ real-time communication for .NET apps.

Firebase Realtime Database â†’ real-time updates without manual socket handling.

âš¡ In short:
WebSockets enable real-time, two-way communication, making them ideal for chat apps, stock tickers, gaming, and live dashboards.

==================================================================================
Microservices with Node.js
==================================================================================
ğŸ”¹ Why Microservices?

âœ… Scalability â†’ Scale only the service that needs more power (e.g., Payment).
âœ… Flexibility â†’ Each microservice can use different technologies.
âœ… Faster development â†’ Teams work independently.
âœ… Resilience â†’ If one service fails, others still work.

ğŸ”¹ Example: E-Commerce App

Instead of a single backend handling everything, split into:

User Service â†’ manages user login/registration.

Product Service â†’ manages product catalog.

Order Service â†’ handles orders.

Payment Service â†’ processes payments.

ğŸ”¹ Building Microservices with Node.js
1. Set Up Independent Services

Each service is a separate Node.js project with its own package.json, routes, and database.

ğŸ“Œ Example: User Service (Express + MongoDB)

// user-service/index.js
const express = require('express');
const mongoose = require('mongoose');
const app = express();
app.use(express.json());

// MongoDB connection
mongoose.connect('mongodb://localhost:27017/users');

// User model
const User = mongoose.model('User', { name: String, email: String });

// Routes
app.post('/users', async (req, res) => {
  const user = new User(req.body);
  await user.save();
  res.send(user);
});

app.get('/users', async (req, res) => {
  const users = await User.find();
  res.send(users);
});

// Run service
app.listen(4000, () => console.log('ğŸ‘¤ User Service running on port 4000'));


ğŸ“Œ Example: Order Service (Express + MongoDB)

// order-service/index.js
const express = require('express');
const mongoose = require('mongoose');
const axios = require('axios'); // For communication
const app = express();
app.use(express.json());

// MongoDB connection
mongoose.connect('mongodb://localhost:27017/orders');

// Order model
const Order = mongoose.model('Order', { userId: String, product: String });

// Routes
app.post('/orders', async (req, res) => {
  // Check user before placing order
  const user = await axios.get(`http://localhost:4000/users/${req.body.userId}`);
  
  if (!user.data) return res.status(400).send('User not found');

  const order = new Order(req.body);
  await order.save();
  res.send(order);
});

// Run service
app.listen(5000, () => console.log('ğŸ“¦ Order Service running on port 5000'));

ğŸ”¹ Communication Between Services

Synchronous â†’ REST APIs (axios, fetch).

Asynchronous â†’ Message brokers (Kafka, RabbitMQ, NATS) for event-driven architecture.

ğŸ”¹ Tools for Node.js Microservices

Express.js / Fastify â†’ Web framework.

Docker â†’ Containerize each service.

Kubernetes â†’ Deploy and scale services.

NATS / Kafka / RabbitMQ â†’ Messaging between services.

API Gateway (Kong, Nginx, Express Gateway) â†’ Entry point for all requests.

ğŸ”¹ Real-World Example

ğŸ‘‰ Netflix, Uber, Amazon all use microservices:

Netflix â†’ streaming, recommendations, payments â†’ separate services.

Uber â†’ maps, ride-matching, billing, notifications â†’ independent services.

âš¡ In short:
Microservices with Node.js = build small, independent services with Express + DB, make them communicate via REST or messaging, and manage them using Docker & Kubernetes.

==================================================================================
GraphQL with JavaScript
==================================================================================
ğŸ”¹ What is GraphQL?

GraphQL is a query language for APIs and a runtime to execute those queries.

Developed by Facebook (2012), open-sourced in 2015.

Alternative to REST API.

Client specifies exactly the data it needs, and the server responds with just that.

ğŸ”¹ Why GraphQL (vs REST)?
Feature	REST	GraphQL
Data Fetching	Fixed endpoints, may return too much/too little data	Client asks only for needed fields
Multiple Resources	Multiple requests needed	Single query can fetch related data
Versioning	Requires /v1, /v2	Schema evolves without breaking clients
Flexibility	Less flexible	Very flexible
ğŸ”¹ Core Concepts

Schema â†’ Defines types and relationships.

Queries â†’ Read data.

Mutations â†’ Modify data (create, update, delete).

Resolvers â†’ Functions that fetch the data.

Subscriptions â†’ Real-time updates (like WebSockets).

ğŸ”¹ Example: GraphQL with JavaScript (Node.js + Apollo Server)
Install:
npm init -y
npm install apollo-server graphql

Server Code:
const { ApolloServer, gql } = require('apollo-server');

// Schema
const typeDefs = gql`
  type User {
    id: ID!
    name: String!
    email: String!
  }

  type Query {
    users: [User]
    user(id: ID!): User
  }

  type Mutation {
    addUser(name: String!, email: String!): User
  }
`;

// Fake data
let users = [
  { id: '1', name: 'Suraj', email: 'suraj@example.com' },
  { id: '2', name: 'Pooja', email: 'pooja@example.com' }
];

// Resolvers
const resolvers = {
  Query: {
    users: () => users,
    user: (_, { id }) => users.find(u => u.id === id),
  },
  Mutation: {
    addUser: (_, { name, email }) => {
      const newUser = { id: String(users.length + 1), name, email };
      users.push(newUser);
      return newUser;
    },
  },
};

// Create Apollo Server
const server = new ApolloServer({ typeDefs, resolvers });

// Run server
server.listen().then(({ url }) => {
  console.log(`ğŸš€ Server ready at ${url}`);
});

ğŸ”¹ How Queries Look in GraphQL
Example Query:
query {
  users {
    id
    name
  }
}


ğŸ‘‰ Response:

{
  "data": {
    "users": [
      { "id": "1", "name": "Suraj" },
      { "id": "2", "name": "Pooja" }
    ]
  }
}

Example Mutation:
mutation {
  addUser(name: "Ravi", email: "ravi@example.com") {
    id
    name
    email
  }
}


ğŸ‘‰ Response:

{
  "data": {
    "addUser": {
      "id": "3",
      "name": "Ravi",
      "email": "ravi@example.com"
    }
  }
}

ğŸ”¹ Real-World Use Cases

GitHub API v4 â†’ Fully GraphQL-based.

Shopify, Twitter, Netflix â†’ Use GraphQL for efficient APIs.

Mobile apps â†’ Avoids over-fetching data.

Microservices â†’ GraphQL can act as a unified API gateway.

âš¡ In short:
GraphQL with JavaScript = define schema + resolvers, then query data exactly as needed. Itâ€™s powerful for flexibility, performance, and developer productivity.

==================================================================================
Security in JS (XSS, CSRF, JWT, OAuth)
==================================================================================
ğŸ” 1. Cross-Site Scripting (XSS)

What it is:
An attacker injects malicious JavaScript into your site (via input fields, comments, query params). When other users visit, the script executes in their browser.

Example:

<!-- User enters this as a comment -->
<script>alert("Hacked!")</script>


If not sanitized, it runs on every visitorâ€™s browser.

Prevention:

Escape user input (innerText instead of innerHTML).

Use sanitization libraries (DOMPurify).

Apply Content Security Policy (CSP).

ğŸ” 2. Cross-Site Request Forgery (CSRF)

What it is:
An attacker tricks a logged-in user into sending unwanted requests to your server.

Example:

Youâ€™re logged into a bank site.

A malicious site runs:

<img src="https://bank.com/transfer?amount=1000&to=attacker" />


The browser auto-sends your cookies â†’ attacker gets money.

Prevention:

Use CSRF tokens (unique per request).

Use SameSite cookies.

Validate Origin/Referer headers.

ğŸ” 3. JSON Web Tokens (JWT)

What it is:
A compact, signed token (base64 format) used for authentication and data exchange.

Example:

// JWT payload
{
  "userId": "12345",
  "role": "admin",
  "exp": 1717023456
}


The server signs this with a secret key. Clients send it in headers:

Authorization: Bearer <jwt-token>


Prevention & Best Practices:

Keep tokens short-lived.

Store in HTTP-only cookies (not localStorage).

Verify signature & expiration on the server.

ğŸ” 4. OAuth 2.0

What it is:
A framework for delegated authorization. Instead of sharing your password, you grant apps limited access.

Example:

You log in to Spotify using Google.

Google sends Spotify an access token, not your password.

Prevention & Best Practices:

Always use HTTPS.

Use PKCE flow for SPAs.

Refresh tokens securely stored (HTTP-only cookies).

âœ… Summary:

XSS â†’ sanitize/escape user input.

CSRF â†’ CSRF tokens + SameSite cookies.

JWT â†’ secure, stateless authentication.

OAuth â†’ safe delegated access (Google, GitHub login).

==================================================================================
Server-Side Rendering (Next.js, Nuxt.js)
==================================================================================

ğŸŒ Server-Side Rendering (SSR)
ğŸ” What is SSR?

In CSR (Client-Side Rendering) â†’ the browser loads an empty HTML shell + JS bundle, then builds the page. (Slower initial load, bad SEO).

In SSR â†’ the server renders the HTML of the page with actual data before sending it to the browser. The browser just hydrates it with JS.

âœ… Benefits of SSR:

Faster First Contentful Paint (FCP)

SEO-friendly (search engines see actual HTML)

Better social media previews (Open Graph tags work)

Good for dynamic content (blogs, e-commerce)

âš›ï¸ Next.js (React SSR Framework)

Built on React.

Uses getServerSideProps for SSR.

Supports SSG (Static Site Generation) + ISR (Incremental Static Regeneration).

Example â€“ SSR in Next.js

// pages/index.js
export default function Home({ data }) {
  return <h1>Users: {data.length}</h1>;
}

export async function getServerSideProps() {
  const res = await fetch("https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/users");
  const data = await res.json();

  return { props: { data } }; // sent to component as props
}


ğŸ‘‰ When user requests /, Next.js server fetches data â†’ renders HTML â†’ sends it to browser.

ğŸ–¼ Nuxt.js (Vue SSR Framework)

Built on Vue.js.

Supports SSR, SSG, CSR out of the box.

Has file-based routing like Next.js.

Example â€“ SSR in Nuxt.js

<template>
  <div>
    <h1>Users: {{ users.length }}</h1>
  </div>
</template>

<script setup>
const { data: users } = await useFetch("https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/users");
</script>


ğŸ‘‰ Nuxt automatically runs useFetch on server for SSR.

ğŸ”„ Next.js vs Nuxt.js (Quick Comparison)
Feature	Next.js (React)	Nuxt.js (Vue)
Language	React + JS/TS	Vue + JS/TS
Data Fetching	getServerSideProps, getStaticProps	useFetch, asyncData
Rendering Modes	SSR, SSG, ISR, CSR	SSR, SSG, CSR
Ecosystem	Backed by Vercel	Backed by NuxtLabs
SEO & Performance	âœ…	âœ…

âœ… When to use SSR (Next/Nuxt)?

Blogs, News sites (SEO matters).

E-commerce (product pages load instantly).

SaaS dashboards (personalized content).

JavaScript Interview Questions & Answers (Zero â†’ Hero)
===================================================
ğŸ”¹ Basics

What is JavaScript?
ğŸ‘‰ A high-level, interpreted programming language used for web development, originally for browsers but now also on servers (Node.js).

Difference between var, let, and const?

var â†’ function scoped, hoisted, allows redeclaration.

let â†’ block scoped, no redeclaration, hoisted in TDZ.

const â†’ block scoped, immutable binding (but object properties can change).

What are data types in JS?

Primitive: string, number, boolean, null, undefined, bigint, symbol.

Reference: objects, arrays, functions.

What is == vs ===?

== â†’ loose equality (performs type coercion).

=== â†’ strict equality (checks value + type).

What are template literals?
ğŸ‘‰ Strings wrapped in backticks (``) that support interpolation:

const name = "Suraj";
console.log(`Hello ${name}!`);

ğŸ”¹ Intermediate

What is Hoisting in JavaScript?
ğŸ‘‰ Variables and function declarations are moved to the top of scope before execution.

var hoisted as undefined.

let & const hoisted but stay in TDZ.

Explain Closures.
ğŸ‘‰ A closure is when a function "remembers" variables from its lexical scope even after that scope has ended.

function counter() {
  let count = 0;
  return () => ++count;
}
const inc = counter();
console.log(inc()); // 1
console.log(inc()); // 2

================================================
What is Event Loop in JS?
ğŸ‘‰ JS is single-threaded; the Event Loop manages the call stack & callback queue for async operations (setTimeout, Promises).

Difference between null and undefined?

undefined â†’ declared but not assigned.

null â†’ explicitly assigned as "no value".

What are Promises?
ğŸ‘‰ Objects that represent eventual completion/failure of an async operation.

ğŸ”¹ Advanced

What is this in JavaScript?
ğŸ‘‰ Refers to the execution context.

In global â†’ window (browser).

Inside function â†’ depends on call.

Inside arrow function â†’ inherits from parent scope.

What is Prototype Inheritance?
ğŸ‘‰ Objects in JS inherit properties from their prototype chain.

What is call, apply, bind?

call â†’ calls function with given this & args.

apply â†’ same but args as array.

bind â†’ returns new function with bound this.

What is Currying?
ğŸ‘‰ Transforming a function with multiple args into multiple functions taking one arg each.

const add = a => b => a + b;
console.log(add(2)(3)); // 5

===================================================
Expert / System-Level

Explain Event Delegation.
ğŸ‘‰ Instead of attaching event listeners to multiple child elements, attach it to a parent and use event.target.

What are Generators?
ğŸ‘‰ Functions that can pause & resume using yield.

What is Async/Await?
ğŸ‘‰ Syntactic sugar over promises for cleaner async code.

async function getData() {
  const res = await fetch('/api');
  return res.json();
}

Explain WeakMap & WeakSet.
ğŸ‘‰ Special collections where keys are weakly referenced (garbage-collected). Useful for caching.

How does JavaScript handle memory management?
ğŸ‘‰ Automatic Garbage Collection (GC) removes unreferenced objects.
=========================================================================
ğŸ”¹ Security / Performance

What is XSS & how to prevent it?
ğŸ‘‰ Cross-Site Scripting. Prevent by escaping input, using CSP, sanitizers.

What is CSRF & how to prevent?
ğŸ‘‰ Cross-Site Request Forgery. Prevent with CSRF tokens, SameSite cookies.

How do you optimize JS performance?

Minify & bundle code.

Use lazy loading.

Avoid memory leaks.

Use async/await properly.


==========================================================================
ğŸ”¹ Latest ECMAScript Features

What is Optional Chaining?
ğŸ‘‰ Safe property access.

console.log(user?.address?.city);


What is Nullish Coalescing (??)?
ğŸ‘‰ Returns right-hand value only if left-hand is null or undefined.

What are ES Modules (ESM)?
ğŸ‘‰ Standard import/export syntax.

import { add } from './math.js';
export const pi = 3.14;


What is BigInt?
ğŸ‘‰ A data type for integers beyond Number.MAX_SAFE_INTEGER.

What is Dynamic Import?
ğŸ‘‰ Load modules on demand.

import('./module.js').then(m => m.run());
=======================================================
ğŸ”¹ Basics
=======================================================
What is JavaScript?
A high-level, interpreted programming language used mainly for web development (frontend + backend).

Difference between var, let, and const

var: function-scoped, hoisted, can be re-declared.

let: block-scoped, no re-declaration, hoisted but in TDZ.

const: block-scoped, cannot be reassigned.

Data Types
Primitive (string, number, boolean, null, undefined, bigint, symbol) & Non-primitive (objects, arrays).

Operators
Arithmetic, Comparison, Logical, Ternary, Nullish coalescing (??), Optional chaining (?.).

Type Conversion (Coercion)

Implicit: "5" + 1 = "51"

Explicit: Number("5") = 5.

=======================================================
ğŸ”¹ Functions & Scope
=======================================================
Function Declaration vs Expression vs Arrow

Declaration: function sum(){}

Expression: const sum = function(){}

Arrow: const sum = () => {}

Default Parameters & Rest/Spread

Default: function greet(name="Guest"){}

Rest: function sum(...nums){}

Spread: [...arr1, ...arr2].

Scope & Hoisting

Global, Local, Block scope.

Hoisting: variables & functions are moved to top of scope.

Closures
A function that remembers variables from its outer scope even after the outer function has finished.

=======================================================
ğŸ”¹ Objects & Arrays
=======================================================
Objects in JS
Key-value pairs, created using {} or new Object().

Array Methods

Iteration: forEach, map, filter, reduce.

Mutation: push, pop, shift, unshift, splice.

Destructuring

Arrays: const [a,b] = [1,2]

Objects: const {x,y} = obj.

this keyword
Depends on context: global, object method, arrow function, event handler.

=======================================================
ğŸ”¹ Advanced Concepts
=======================================================
Prototype & Inheritance
JS uses prototype-based inheritance. Objects inherit from their prototype chain.

ES6+ Features
let/const, arrow functions, template literals, classes, modules (import/export), async/await.

Event Loop & Concurrency
JS is single-threaded â†’ uses event loop, call stack, task queue, microtasks (Promises).

Promises & Async/Await

Promise: then, catch, finally.

Async/Await: cleaner syntax for asynchronous code.

Modules

CommonJS (require, module.exports)

ES Modules (import, export).

=======================================================
ğŸ”¹ Browser & DOM
=======================================================
DOM Manipulation
document.getElementById, querySelector, innerHTML, addEventListener.

Events & Event Bubbling
Capturing â†’ Target â†’ Bubbling phases. Use event.stopPropagation().

LocalStorage, SessionStorage, Cookies

LocalStorage: persistent.

SessionStorage: per session.

Cookies: store data with expiry.

Web APIs
Fetch API, Geolocation, WebSockets, Notifications.

=======================================================
ğŸ”¹ Security
=======================================================
XSS (Cross-Site Scripting)
Malicious script injection â†’ prevent with sanitization.

CSRF (Cross-Site Request Forgery)
Fake requests â†’ prevent with CSRF tokens.

JWT (JSON Web Tokens)
Securely transmit user data. Used in authentication.

OAuth
Authorization protocol (e.g., Google login).

=======================================================
ğŸ”¹ Performance & Testing
=======================================================
Performance Optimization
Debouncing, Throttling, Lazy loading, Memoization.

Testing in JS

Unit Testing: Jest, Mocha.

TDD (Test Driven Development).

ğŸ”¹ Frameworks & Tools

React, Angular, Vue (SPA frameworks)

Backend with JavaScript. REST APIs, middleware, CRUD apps.

ğŸ§  I. Theory Questions (Conceptual)

What is JavaScript and how is it different from Java?
â†’ JS is a scripting language for web apps; Java is an OOP compiled language.

Explain Hoisting in JavaScript.
â†’ Variables and functions are moved to the top of their scope before execution.

What is the difference between == and ===?
â†’ == performs type coercion, === checks both value and type.

Explain Event Bubbling and Capturing.
â†’ Event Bubbling: event travels bottom-up.
â†’ Capturing: event travels top-down.

What are Closures?
â†’ A function that has access to its outer scope even after the outer function has returned.

What are Promises and how do you handle them?
â†’ Promises handle async code. Use .then(), .catch(), or async/await.

What is a Higher-Order Function?
â†’ A function that takes another function as argument or returns one.

Difference between Call, Apply, and Bind?
â†’

call() â†’ calls function with individual args.

apply() â†’ calls function with array args.

bind() â†’ returns a new function with this bound.

ğŸ§© II. MCQs (Multiple Choice Questions)
1ï¸âƒ£
console.log(typeof null);


A) null
B) undefined
C) object âœ…
D) NaN

ğŸŸ¢ Explanation:
typeof null returns "object" â€” a legacy bug in JS since its first version.


âœ… Basic import Syntax in JavaScript
1. Default Import

Used when a module exports a default value:

// math.js
export default function add(x, y) {
  return x + y;
}

// main.js
import add from './math.js';
console.log(add(2, 3)); // 5

2. Named Import

Used to import specific exports (non-default) from a module:

// utils.js
export const multiply = (a, b) => a * b;
export const divide = (a, b) => a / b;

// main.js
import { multiply, divide } from './utils.js';
console.log(multiply(2, 3)); // 6

3. Import All (Namespace Import)

Imports everything into a single object:

// utils.js
export const multiply = (a, b) => a * b;
export const divide = (a, b) => a / b;

// main.js
import * as Utils from './utils.js';
console.log(Utils.multiply(2, 3)); // 6

4. Mixing Default and Named Imports
// math.js
export default function add(a, b) {
  return a + b;
}
export const subtract = (a, b) => a - b;

// main.js
import add, { subtract } from './math.js';

5. Import for Side Effects Only
// logger.js
console.log('Logger initialized');

// main.js
import './logger.js'; // Runs the file, doesn't import any bindings

âš ï¸ Notes

JavaScript modules must be loaded with type="module" in HTML:

<script type="module" src="main.js"></script>


Or be handled via a bundler like Webpack, Rollup, or in frameworks like React, Vue, etc.

File extensions (.js, .mjs) may be required in native environments (like in the browser or Deno).

2ï¸âƒ£
let x = [1, 2, 3];
let y = x;
y.push(4);
console.log(x);


A) [1, 2, 3]
B) [1, 2, 3, 4] âœ…
C) [4]
D) Error

ğŸŸ¢ Explanation:
Arrays are reference types â€” y and x point to the same memory.

3ï¸âƒ£
let a = "5";
let b = 2;
console.log(a + b);


A) 7
B) 52 âœ…
C) NaN
D) Error

ğŸŸ¢ Explanation:
String + number performs string concatenation.

4ï¸âƒ£
let a = [1,2,3];
console.log(typeof a);


A) array
B) object âœ…
C) list
D) undefined

ğŸ’» III. Coding Output Questions
1ï¸âƒ£
function test() {
  for (var i = 0; i < 3; i++) {
    setTimeout(() => console.log(i), 1000);
  }
}
test();


ğŸŸ¢ Output:

3
3
3


âœ… Explanation:
var is function-scoped. By the time setTimeout executes, i becomes 3.

2ï¸âƒ£

  count++;

1
2
3ï¸âƒ£

ğŸŸ¢ Output:
[20, 40, 60]


4ï¸âƒ£

  console.log("Start");
  resolve("Success");
});

console.log("End");


ğŸŸ¢ Output:

Start
End
Success


âœ… Explanation:
Promises execute synchronously until .then(), which runs asynchronously.

ğŸ§  JavaScript Interview Questions (Basic â†’ Advanced)
=======================================================================================================
ğŸŸ¢ 1. JavaScript Fundamentals

1. What is JavaScript?
â†’ JavaScript is a high-level, interpreted, single-threaded programming language used to create interactive web applications.

2. What are the data types in JS?
â†’ Primitive: string, number, boolean, null, undefined, symbol, bigint
â†’ Non-Primitive: object, array, function

3. What is the difference between let, const, and var?
â†’ var â†’ function-scoped; allows redeclaration
â†’ let â†’ block-scoped; allows reassignment
â†’ const â†’ block-scoped; cannot be reassigned

4. What is Hoisting?
â†’ JS moves variable and function declarations to the top of their scope before execution.

5. What is the difference between == and ===?
â†’ == compares values with type conversion; === compares both type and value strictly.

ğŸŸ¢ 2. Functions and Scope

6. What is a Closure?
â†’ A closure allows a function to access variables from its outer scope even after the outer function has returned.

7. What are Higher-Order Functions?
â†’ Functions that take another function as an argument or return one.

8. What is the difference between function declaration and expression?
â†’ Declaration is hoisted; expression is not.

9. What is IIFE (Immediately Invoked Function Expression)?
â†’ A function that runs as soon as it is defined.
(function() { console.log("Hello!"); })();

10. What is Lexical Scope?
â†’ The accessibility of variables based on where they are defined in the code hierarchy.

ğŸŸ¢ 3. Objects and Arrays

11. How do you copy objects in JS?
â†’ Shallow copy: Object.assign({}, obj) or { ...obj }
â†’ Deep copy: structuredClone(obj) or JSON.parse(JSON.stringify(obj))

12. What are prototypes in JS?
â†’ Every object in JS inherits properties from its prototype object.

13. Difference between map(), filter(), and reduce()?
â†’ map() â†’ transforms array elements
â†’ filter() â†’ selects elements
â†’ reduce() â†’ accumulates values

14. What is Destructuring?
â†’ Extracting values from arrays or objects into distinct variables.

15. What is Spread and Rest Operator?
â†’ Spread (...) expands arrays; Rest (...) collects multiple arguments into one array.

ğŸŸ¢ 4. Asynchronous JavaScript

16. What are Callbacks?
â†’ Functions passed as arguments to be executed later.

17. What are Promises?
â†’ Objects that represent the eventual completion (or failure) of an asynchronous operation.

18. What is Async/Await?
â†’ Syntactic sugar over Promises to write asynchronous code that looks synchronous.

19. What is the Event Loop?
â†’ The mechanism that handles async operations, managing the call stack and callback queue.

20. What is the difference between Microtask and Macrotask?
â†’ Microtasks (Promises) run before Macrotasks (setTimeout, setInterval).

ğŸŸ¢ 5. DOM and Events

21. What is DOM?
â†’ Document Object Model represents HTML as a tree structure accessible by JS.

22. What is Event Bubbling and Capturing?
â†’ Bubbling: event moves from inner to outer element.
â†’ Capturing: event moves from outer to inner element.

23. How do you prevent event bubbling?
â†’ Use event.stopPropagation().

24. What is Event Delegation?
â†’ Attaching one event listener to a parent instead of multiple children.

25. How do you manipulate DOM elements?
â†’ document.getElementById(), .innerHTML, .appendChild(), .classList.add() etc.

ğŸŸ¢ 6. ES6+ Features

26. What are Arrow Functions?
â†’ Shorter function syntax that doesnâ€™t bind its own this.

27. What are Template Literals?
â†’ String interpolation using backticks `Hello ${name}`.

28. What are Modules in JS?
â†’ Using import and export to organize code across files.

29. What are Classes in JS?
â†’ Syntactic sugar over prototypes for object-oriented programming.

30. What are Default Parameters?
â†’ Set default values in functions if no argument is passed.

ğŸŸ¢ 7. Advanced Topics

31. What is Debouncing and Throttling?
â†’ Techniques to limit how often functions execute â€” used for performance optimization.

32. What is Memoization?
â†’ Caching results of function calls to improve performance.

33. What is Currying?
â†’ Transforming a function with multiple arguments into a sequence of functions with one argument each.

34. What are Generators?
â†’ Functions that can pause execution and resume using yield.

35. What is Event Loop starvation?
â†’ When long-running tasks block the event loop and delay async operations.

ğŸŸ¢ 8. Error Handling

36. What is try...catch used for?
â†’ To handle exceptions gracefully.

37. What is finally used for?
â†’ Executes regardless of success or failure.

38. How do you create custom errors?
â†’ throw new Error("Custom message");

39. What is Promise.catch() used for?
â†’ To handle rejected Promises.

40. What are runtime vs syntax errors?
â†’ Syntax errors â†’ before execution; Runtime errors â†’ during execution.

ğŸŸ¢ 9. Performance and Optimization

41. How to optimize loops in JS?
â†’ Use caching, for...of, or array methods instead of nested loops.

42. What is Lazy Loading?
â†’ Loading resources or modules only when needed.

43. What is Minification?
â†’ Removing unnecessary characters from source code for faster load times.

44. What causes Memory Leaks in JS?
â†’ Unused variables, event listeners not removed, or global references.

45. How do you profile performance in Chrome DevTools?
â†’ Using the Performance and Memory tabs.

10. Security in JavaScript

46. What is XSS (Cross-Site Scripting)?
â†’ Injecting malicious scripts into web pages. Prevent using innerText or sanitizing input.

47. What is CSRF (Cross-Site Request Forgery)?
â†’ Forcing a user to perform actions they didnâ€™t intend. Prevent using CSRF tokens.

48. What is JWT (JSON Web Token)?
â†’ A secure token format for authentication.

49. What is CORS?
â†’ Cross-Origin Resource Sharing controls which domains can access resources.

50. How do you secure cookies in JS?
â†’ Use HttpOnly, Secure, and SameSite flags.
